Summary 0:
Pre-marketing of UCITS management companies — requirements for marketing communications
Pre-Marketing of UCTS funds
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1286/2014 on marketing communications for UCITS
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the requirements for pre-marketed marketing communications by UCITS* management companies.
It aims to ensure that marketing communications to investors are fair, clear and not misleading.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
a UCITS authorised in accordance with Article 5 of Directive 2009/65/EC;
a management company as defined in point 2 of Article 2(1) of Directive 2014/31/EU on the management of a UCITS;
an investment firm that is authorised as an investment firm, as a credit institution, or as a social entrepreneurship fund.
UCITS management company
A UCITS might be externally or internally managed, depending on its legal form. The regulation applies both to companies, the regular business of which is managing UCITS, and to management companies that are externally managed.
Management companies must:
ensure that the content of marketing communications addressed to investors is:
fair, clear, not misleading;
not offensive;
inform investors of the content or of the addressees of the contents of the marketing communications;
provide information on fees or charges.
A third party may only engage in marketing communications on behalf of an authorised manager of a qualifying venture capital fund where it is authorised by the European Investment Funds for Social Entrepreneurship Fund (EuVECA) or EuSEF (European Social Entrepreneur Fund for the Development of Entrepreneurial Entrepreneurships).
The marketing communications must be:
clear and clear;
represent the objectives and objectives of the fund;
contain information on the fund’s assets and liabilities;
be presented in a manner that is fair and clear.
The European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) is responsible for maintaining a central database containing information on UCITS marketing communications.
In addition, the European Commission must establish guidelines on the application of the regulation to marketing communications, taking into account on-line aspects of such marketing communications and taking account of on-site aspects such as the nature and content of such communications. The Commission must issue guidelines to the Commission by 2 February 2021.
By 2 August 2024, the Commission must conduct an evaluation of the implementation of this regulation and submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) on its application. This report must include information on:
the number of investors who have received marketing communications from a management company under this regulation;
the content of these communications; and
the fees and charges that the management company has charged.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    Егганка бакова гакка  года  
It has applied since 2 August 2014 and had to become law in the EU countries by 2 August 2015.
BACKGROUND
The UCITS Directive is one of a series of measures designed to protect the financial stability of the European Union (EU). The other measures are:
Directive 2013/36/EU establishing the European Financial Stability Facility (ESF) and repealing Directives 2006/48/EC and 2006/49/EC (Directive 2002/87/EC on the prudential supervision of credit institutions and investment firms, amending Directive 2002/84/EC, and repealing Council Directives 2002/85/EC.
Regulations (EU, Euratom) No 552/2014 and (EU), No 1284/2014 have been amended several times.
For more information, see:
‘UCITS’ on the ESMA website.
* KEY TERMS
* UCITS: an investment fund that is managed by a UCIT management company. A UCITS may be externally managed, internally managed or externally-managed.

Summary 1:
EU harmonisation legislation on products — rules on the supply chain and the market surveillance of goods
EU harmonization legislation on goods
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/644 on the harmonisation of Union harmonisation on products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the enforcement of EU harmonisation laws on products.
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ customs and market surveillance authorities have the necessary information and cooperation with economic operators to ensure the proper enforcement of these laws.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies from 1 January 2021. However, Articles 29, 30, 31, 32, 33 and 36 shall apply from 1 July 2021.
EU countries are encouraged to use ICSMS for interactions between customs, market surveillance and economic operators as an alternative to the national systems.
The legislation applies to:
products imported into the EU from non-EU countries;
products exported to the EU;
pre-export control systems;
supply chain controls.
In order to achieve a high degree of compliance with the legislation, the European Commission must be able to approve specific pre-export controls systems. These must be in place for:
air conditioning systems in motor vehicles;
motor vehicles and motorcycles;
vehicle parts and components;
electrical equipment;
personal protective equipment.
Importers and importers must inform the competent authorities in charge of the control on products entering the customs territory of the EU of the release of products for free circulation, and of the results of controls, where such information is relevant for enforcement of the legislation.
the competent authorities must inform economic operators in the EU countries of their tasks regarding products subject to certain EU legislation, including:
contact information of economic operators with tasks relating to certain products;
information to economic operators regarding the product compliance requirements;
the information to be indicated with the product in order to facilitate checks throughout the product’s supply chain.
If a product is found to be in breach of the applicable legislation, EU countries must take the necessary measures in respect of the non-compliant product and enter the relevant information in the information and communication system referred to in Annex II of the regulation.
Economic operators must ensure that these measures are implemented in accordance with national law and that they are effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
National market surveillance strategies
The Commission is encouraged to develop national strategies for market surveillance. These strategies should be based on the following principles:
the protection of consumers’ interests;
protection of intellectual property rights;
promotion of fair competition;
ensuring that products are not offered for sale online through their services.
Information to and cooperation between economic operators and customs authorities
EU governments must provide the Commission with:
information on the products covered by the legislation;
a list of the products that are subject to the legislation and the economic operators whose tasks are to assist and cooperate with the authorities;
procedural rights and obligations on economic operators that impose obligations on them;
rules on the information to and coordination with the national authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2018 and had to become law in the European Union countries by 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to improve the functioning of its internal market for goods. These include:
Directive 2006/40/EC relating to emissions from air conditioning systems and motor vehicles and amending Council Directive 70/156/EC (OJ L 161, 14.6.2006, pp. 1-8);
Regulations (EU, Euratom) No 1304/ 2013, (EU and EU) No 1316/2013 on cross-border parcel delivery services and repealing Regulation (EC) No 966/2012;
Decision No 541/2014/EU and repealing Decision No 571/2012.
For more information, see:
European Union Harmonisation (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Pre-exportation control system: a system designed to prevent, detect and control the movement of products from one country to another.
* Supply chain control: the control of a product from one place to another by means of a pre-exchange control system.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, of 18 April 2018 on the uniform application of certain Union harmonisations on products and repealing Council Directive 2006/41/EC of 17 May 2006 on the Union product compliance network.
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) 2019/966 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 2:
Exercise of copyright and related rights in respect of retransmissions of television and radio programmes originating in other EU countries (Directive 2006/115/EEC on the exercise of intellectual property rights in the field of broadcasting organisations’ retransmission of programmes and the collective management of rights of the same category of rights in that field)
Exemption of rights
The European Union (EU) has adopted a new directive on the exploitation of rights and other protected subject matter relating to broadcasting organisations' retransmissibility of programmes.
ACT
Council Directive 2006/116/EC of 28 December 2006 on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to the exercise, in particular, of the rights of collective management organisations regarding the retransmitment of works of audiovisual works.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It sets out rules on the interpretation and exploitation of copyright (i.e. the right to reproduce a work of art) and other related rights.
It aims to facilitate cross-border provision of online services that are ancillary to broadcasts.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
radio and television programmes transmitted by broadcasting organisations;
online services that originate in other Member States;
replays of television or radio programmes;
the collective management and management of copyright-related rights in relation to retransmits of these rights; and
the rights of a collective management organisation.
Rights of the broadcasting organisations
Radio and television broadcasting organisations transmit daily many hours of television programmes. These organisations have ongoing commercial relations with broadcasting organisations, and as a result are known to operators of broadcasting services.
Radio broadcasting organisations are responsible for the transmission of programmes through direct injection.
The collective management bodies responsible for managing the rights are:
collective management organisations that manage rights for the territory of the EU Member State for which the operator of a retransmitter services seeks to clear rights for a programme;
radio broadcasting organisations which manage the rights for retranscasts of radio programmes transmitted in the Member State in which they originate.
Where a rightholder has not transferred the management of the right referred to in the second subparagraph of paragraph 1 of the directive, the collective managing organisation which manages rights of that category of right for the country of origin of the operator seeking to clear the right for a television programme for which it seeks to grant or refuse the authorisation.
In such cases, it is possible to call upon the assistance of one or more mediators as provided for in Article 11 of Directive 93/83/EC where there is no agreement between the rightholders and the operators of the services seeking to cover the rights. The mediators must ensure that the parties concerned comply with EU law, on limitations, including territorial limitations, to the exploitation or exploitation of their rights. They must also ensure that:
the parties concerned have the right of contractual freedom to limit the exploitation and the rights affected by the principles set out in this directive, provided that any such limitation is in compliance with Union law.
If the parties fail to comply with these principles, they must inform the European Commission and the European Parliament of the reasons for their decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s digital broadcasting directive, which has been amended several times, is one of the most important legal instruments in the fight against copyright infringement in the digital age. It aims to improve the quality of the online content offered by broadcasters and to ensure that there is a diverse content offer, which is also in the interest of consumers.
For more information, see:
Online broadcasting (European Commission).
REFERENCES

Summary 3:
.
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the functioning of the.eu Top Level Domain Name Registry (TLD).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes a registry for the top-level domain name ‘.eu’.
The.eu TLD is one of the largest country code TLDs (ccTLDs) in the world.
It sets out the rules and procedures on the organisation, administration and management of this TLD.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the.eu top level domain name (tLD);
the domain names reserved or reserved at the second level by EU countries;
the registry of.eu domain names.
Designation of the registry
The registry is managed by the European Commission under a contract between the Commission and a designated registrar. The Commission is responsible for:
designating the registrar;
assisting the registry in its work;
supervising the registry’s operation;
ensuring that the registry complies with the principles of non-discrimination and transparency;
providing the Commission with the necessary information and expertise to ensure the proper functioning and operation of the TLD;
monitoring the registry.
Organisation and administration
The Commission must designate a registrar to be responsible for the management and organisation of the domain name. The registrar must:
ensure that domain names are registered in accordance with the eligibility criteria, registration requirements, registration procedures, registration fees and registration fees, as well-defined by the Commission;
rules on the rights and obligations of domain name holders and registrars;
register domain names that are identical or confusingly similar to a name in respect of which a right is established by EU or national law and where it:
has been registered by its holder without rights or legitimate interest in the name;
is being used in bad faith.
Registration and revocation of domain names
A domain name may also be revoked, and where necessary subsequently transferred to another party, following an appropriate ADR or judicial procedure, following a reasonable opportunity to rectify any breach of registration requirements or outstanding debts before the revocation is to take effect.
In addition, the Commission must ensure that:
a domain name is registered by an EU citizen, independently of their place of residence;
a natural person who is not a EU citizen and who is prejudice to arrangements in EU countries regarding their national ccTLD; and
a non-EU citizen who is prejudiced by arrangements in Member States regarding the rules of the national registry regarding the designation and management.
A multistakeholder advisory group may include one representative of stakeholders established outside the EU. It may propose further measures in that regard, if necessary a representative from outside the European Union, and every 3 years thereafter.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
Directive 2014/34/EU establishes the.EU Top Level DNS and the rules on its operation.
This regulation applies from 13 October 2027.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of rules on the operation of.EU TLD, including:
Registry
Decision 2014/35/EU on the establishment of a.eu Multistakeholders Advisory Group (MAAG).
The MAAG is composed of representatives of the stakeholders who have a stake in the operation and management, and whose role is to advise and assist the Commission in the implementation of the regulation.
For more information, see:
‘Registry’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 4:
Multiannual financial framework 2021-2027
Multi-year financial framework (MFF) 2021–2027 — financial support for the transport, energy and digital sectors
Multiannial financial framework(MFF 2021–2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/523 establishing the Multiannual Financial Framework (the ‘MFF’) 2021 – 2027
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union’s multiannual financing framework (the MFF) for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
It sets out the rules for the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the European Social Fund Plus, the European Investment Bank (ESF Plus) and the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFF).
KEY POINTS
The MFF is the framework for the implementation of European Union (EU, Euratom) policies and programmes. It is designed to:
support the development of the transport and energy sectors;
promote and monitor the European Semester of economic policy coordination;
contribute to the creation of a European digital vision in local communities.
The European Commission is responsible for managing, monitoring, evaluating, evaluating and controlling the MFF. It must also ensure that the achievements of the CEF are recognised.
In addition, the Commission must:
coordinate, monitor, evaluate, evaluate and control the work programmes;
ensure that they are implemented in line with the objectives and objectives of the European Commission;
provide information, communication and publicity on the results of the work programme;
identify, monitor and evaluate the results achieved.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLIT APPLY?
From 1 January 2019.
This regulation applies from the date of entry into force.

Summary 5:
Removal of terrorist content on the internet (Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
Removal on the Internet (Regulating (EU))
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation 2010/13/EU on hosting service providers and users’ trust in the online environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the detection and removal of content online that incites terrorist acts.
KEY POINTS
Hosting service providers must act with due diligence and implement safeguards, where appropriate, including human oversight and verifications, to avoid any unintended or erroneous decision leading to the removal of or disabling of access to content that is not terrorist content.
They must apply those provisions in a diligent, transparent, proportionate and non-discriminatory manner.
EU countries must ensure that a contact point is designated or established within the competent authority to handle requests for clarification and feedback in relation to removal orders issued by that competent authority. This contact point should serve only for operational purposes.
The hosting service provider must provide the complainant with the reasons for its decision. The reasons should not imply the disclosure of sensitive information which could jeopardise ongoing investigations.
Complaint procedures must be in place to ensure that the removal order is complied with. The complainant must submit, within 48 hours of receiving either a removal order or information pursuant to Article 11(2), a reasoned request to the competent authorities of the EU country where the hosting services provider has its main establishment or where its legal representative resides or is established to scrutinise the order. The competent authorities must then decide whether to accept or reject the reasoned request.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that the complainant has the right to:
review proceedings, as soon as the grounds for the removal have ceased to exist;
reinstitute access to the content that has been removed or access to which has been disabled, as well as to ensure the reinstatement of that content, depending on the outcome of those proceedings.
Requests for clarification or feedback must be made publicly available.
If a service provider fails to comply with an order, the complainant must have the right of:
an effective remedy;
to be informed of the reason for the decision;
the possibility to appeal the decision.
Jurisdiction and enforcement
The regulation applies to all EU countries except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1), where it applies to the following:
the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland (1);
the Republic of Ireland (2);
Sweden (3);
United States of America (4);
Canada (5);
France (6);
Germany (7);
Iceland (8);
Spain (9);
Portugal (10);
Italy (11);
Austria (12);
Hungary (13);
Romania (14);
Serbia (15);
Bulgaria (16);
Slovenia (17);
Slovakia (18);
Turkey (19);
Ukraine (20);
Vietnam (21);
Yugoslavia (22);
Zimbabwe (23);
Syria (24);
Iraq (25);
Joint Action Plan for Combating Terrorism (JAP-T).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

It has applied since 30 June 2010.
BACKGROUND
    *  *  
For more information, see:
‘Removal’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Terrorist content: any material that is likely to incite or support acts of violence, hatred or violence directed against a person or group of persons, or to undermine the rule of law, public order, public security or international peace and security.

Summary 6:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021-2027
Framework programme for research and innovation 2021-2020
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 on Horizon Europe — the framework programme for R&I
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the European Union’s (EU) Framework programme for Research & Innovation (‘Horizon Europe’) 2021–2027 (the ‘Programme’).
It also lays down the principles and procedures for participating in the programme.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme covers:
research and innovation (R&I) in the fields of science, technology, innovation and applied research (S&A), including:
science and technology applied to health and well-being;
environmental and social aspects of health and wellbeing;
health and wellbeing of the marine environment;
science, technology and innovation applied to the fight against climate change;
research on the environment and sustainable development.
Objectives
The objectives of the programme are to:
promote scientific, technological, economic, environmental and societal impact in pursuit of this general objective and to maximise the added value of the Union's investments in R&Is;
contribute to the sustainable development of the EU and its Member States;
improve the quality of life of people and the environment in the European Economic Area (EEA);
support the development of knowledge-based solutions for the sustainable management and management of the environment, including through the creation, management, management and operation of research institutes;
encourage the creation of networks and networks in the EEA;
support research and development in the areas of health, wellbeing and social determinants of health;
develop innovative solutions to improve the quality and sustainability of life for people, including the environment.
Funding
The European Commission is responsible for administering the programme and for its implementation. The European Investment Bank (EIB) and the Joint Research Centre (JRC) are responsible for managing the budget.
The EIB and the JRC are the main bodies responsible for the implementation of Horizon Europe. The EIB manages the budget for the programme under the auspices of the European Commission. The JRC manages the work programme and calls for proposals for actions under Horizon Europe under the supervision of the Commission.
Partnership and participation
Participation in Horizon Europe is open to any legal entity, regardless of its place of establishment and including legal entities from non-associated third countries or international organisations, provided that the conditions laid down in this regulation have been met together with any conditions set out in the work programmes or call for proposals.
Member States may participate in actions under the programme provided that they respect the following principles:
clear EU added value;
autonomy;
ability to take risk;
efficiency;
effectiveness;
transparency;
compliance with the principle of equal treatment;
accountability.
Procedures for participation and dissemination
The JRC and the EIB must:
apply the principles laid down by the Regulation;
conduct the programme in accordance with the principles set out by the EIC;
ensure that actions are carried out in line with the programme objectives;
take account of the needs of the Member State concerned;
make appropriate use of the financial resources available to the programme;
organise and implement the programme according to the rules laid down under the Regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 18 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
Horizon 2020-2028:
the European Union's Framework Programme for R & I
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATIONS DO?
The Regulation establishes the framework for Horizon Europe (the 'Programme').
It applies to Horizon Europe, the EU's general budget, which is funded by the European Social Fund (ESF), the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development and the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund.
For more information, see Horizon Europe on the Commission's website.

Summary 7:
European Union programme for the development of the space sector
European Union Programme for the Development of the Space sector
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2021/698 establishing a European Union programme to develop the European Union’s space industry
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The European Union (EU) 2020/2021 programme is designed to promote the development and use of space technologies in the EU. It aims to develop space industry capacity across the EU and facilitate collaboration across space industry active in all EU countries.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 182/2011.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme aims to:
promote the development, use and dissemination of European space technologies;
encourage cooperation between EU countries’ authorities in the field of space technology;
ensure a high level of security of systems and services deployed;
contribute to the creation of a European satellite communication system (SST) sub-component;
provide services provided by the European Space Agency (ESA) and the European Geostationary Operational Satellite System (GOVSATCOM).
Objectives
The objectives of the programme include:
developing the European space industry;
promoting the use of the European satellite communications system (GOSPATCOM);
encouraging cooperation between national authorities in this sector;
improving the availability of satellite communication capacities, services and user equipment;
facilitating the exchange of information and expertise;
creating a European space information network;
enhancing cooperation between European space agencies;
informing the public about the capabilities of the EU space industry.
contributing to the establishment of the Copernicus satellite information network.
Organisation
EU countries are to participate in the programme by:
participating in the SST sub-partnership;
assisting the European Agency for Safety and Security in Space (ESA);
providing technical assistance and technical assistance to the European Commission;
cooperating with the Agency and ESA.
The Commission is responsible for implementing the programme. It is assisted by the Security Accreditation Board, which is composed of national experts involved in the security accreditation activities under Chapter II of Title II of the regulation.
In line with Article 346 TFEU, a specific governance of security should be established to ensure a smooth implementation of the Programme. The security board is chaired by the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy. The High Representative is assisted in this by the Administrative Board. The Commission may, where appropriate and in their respective field of competence, cooperate with the Commission and ESA to ensure that the programme is implemented in line with security requirements.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The decision and regulation entered into force on 28 April 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU is one of the most competitive economies in the world and its space sector is already one of its most competitive. However, the sector faces challenges in terms of security, including cyber-attacks. The need to protect the systems and their services against cyber-attack, including by making use of new technologies, should therefore be duly taken into account.
For more information, see:
‘European Union programmes for space technology’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2021/694/CFSP of 28 March 2021 establishing a programme for developing the space industry (OJ L 131, 28.4.2021, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Decision 2021-694/FC have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 8:
The European Digital Innovation Hub 2021-2027 — Strategic Framework Programme
The European digital innovation hub 2021-2020
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2021/2027 establishing the European digital Innovation Hub
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE DECISION?
The regulation establishes the European Digital innovation hub (EDIH) for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
The decision approves the programme for a period of 7 years.
KEY POINTS
Objectives
The programme’s general objectives are to:
promote the digital transformation of areas of public interest in the European Union (EU);
modernise and modernise public administrations;
improve access to and ease of access to services;
strengthen cybersecurity and trust within the digital single market.
Specific objectives
The objectives of the EDIH include:
modernisation of public administrations:
improving the efficiency and effectiveness of public administration;
modernising and modernising public administrations’ information technology systems;
ensuring that information technology (IT) services are accessible to all workers and self-employed people who are in occupation and unemployed people.
Improving access to public services:
increasing the accessibility of public services and improving the quality of services.
Strengthening public administrations' IT systems:
improveing the quality and efficiency of information and communication technology (ICT) services,
increasing access to information and communications technologies (ICT),
modernizing public administration,
modernised public administration's IT systems,
transforming public administration and improving IT infrastructure;
enhancing the digitalisation of information society;
increasing mobility of the workforce;
encouraging the use of exascale and high-performance computing (HPCs) in the public sector;
promoting the sharing of best practices and best practices in the field of high performance computing;
raising awareness of the importance of HPCs in the digital economy;
developing and disseminating innovative products and services that reinforce cybersecurity, trust and trust in the Digital Single Market.
Eligible actions
The actions under Specific Objective 4 shall be implemented primarily through direct management. They shall be funded by the EU budget. The budget for the programme is EUR 7 588 000 000 in current prices.
Implementation and forms of EU funding
EU funding for the duration of the programme shall be based on a multiannual financial framework laid down in Council Regulation (EU) 2020/2093. The financial envelope for the implementation of the Programme for the year 2021 to 2027 shall be EUR 7.5 million.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
 * High performance computing: the use and development of high-performing computing technologies, such as the Internet of Things (IoT) and the internet of things (i.e. smart phones, tablets, smart televisions, etc.).
* Exascale: the ability of the human body to process and process information rapidly and efficiently.
* High-performance computer: a computer that is capable of performing tasks that require a high level of computing power.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Resolution of 14 December 2017 on the establishment, on behalf of the Union, of the European European Innovation Hub (EIAH) 2021–2027 (OJ L 127, 29.12.2017, pp. 1–8)

Summary 9:
European statistical programme
European statistics programme
SUMMEMBER OF:
Regulation (EU) No 223/2014/EU establishing a European statistical programme for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a financial framework for European statistics to provide high-quality, comparable and reliable European statistics, in order to underpin the design, implementation, monitoring and evaluation of all Union policies.
The European Statistical Programme (ESP) aims to meet the challenges of the green and digital transition and of an increasingly competitive and fast-moving environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ESP covers:
the development, production and dissemination of European statistics pursuant to Regulation (EC) 223/2009 of the European Parliament and of the Council;
the establishment of a European statistics network for animal diseases and plant pests that have a substantial impact on the EU;
collecting, analysing and disseminating European statistics on agricultural and forestry products;
monitoring and analysing the impact of EU policies on the environment and the economy;
establishing European statistics for the protection of human health and the environment;
supporting the European Consumer Centres Network.
Objectives
The programme’s objective is to:
improve the quality of EU statistics;
ensure that European statistics are reliable and up-to-date;
contribute to the development, implementation and monitoring of EU policy-making;
provide the European Commission with the necessary information and information on the environmental, economic and social impact of the EU policies and programmes;
promote the exchange of information between EU countries;
facilitate the exchange and sharing of information on EU policies, programmes and actions;
encourage the development of European statistical networks.
In order to achieve these objectives, the ESP is funded by the European Union budget.
Financing
The EU budget covers the costs of actions implementing the specific objective referred to in Article 3(2)(2)(f) of this regulation. The European Commission may finance up to 95 % of the eligible costs of the actions supporting collaborative networks as referred to by Article 15 of Regulation (Euratom).
In addition, the Commission may provide financial support for actions supporting the European consumer centres network, which aims to provide information to consumers about challenges and problems that consumers encounter at local level, which are relevant for EU policymaking and protection of consumers’ interests. The Commission may also finance actions in the field of agriculture, forestry and aquaculture.
Participation
The Commission may, in particular, finance the actions of non-EU countries that are not associated to the programme, but may finance their own participation in the actions that are financed by EU countries.
Sustainable partnerships
The Programme may also be encouraged by providing continuation funding if performance and participation milestones are reached.

Summary 10:
Prohibition of firearms and essential components and ammunition — European Union (EU) rules
Protection of the EU’s internal market against the illegal acquisition and possession of firearms, essential components, ammunition and explosives
Protocols for the protection of the internal market from the illicit trade in firearms and their essential components
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 91/477/EEC on the prohibition of firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It codifies and repeals Council Directive 91/478/EC, which had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 1992.
It aims to protect the EU and its citizens against the illicit acquisition and sale of firearms.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to the following categories of firearms:
firearms classified in category A: semi-automatic firearms;
automatic firearms in combination with a detachable loading device having a high capacity;
guns with a high number of rounds to be fired;
battery-operated rifles;
machineguns, shotguns and assault rifles.
EU countries must take all appropriate measures to prohibit the acquisition and possess of the firearms, the essential components* and ammunition classified in these categories, subject to strict conditions regarding security.
Dealers and brokers must:
ensure that firearms and ammunition are stored separately from each other in a safe;
keep a register of all the firearms they acquire and possess;
notify the competent authorities if they suspect that a firearm is being traded for illicit purposes;
report any suspicious transactions to them;
inform the authorities when they suspect a firearm has been traded for illegal purposes.
In addition to the register, dealers and brokers should also be able to report such transactions to the authorities.
National authorities must keep records of all transactions involving firearms, including the names and addresses of the persons involved in such transactions.
The national authorities must also keep records for a period of 30 years after the destruction of firearms or essential components concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 13 June 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 13 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
  
The legislation is part of the Schengen acquis, the legal framework for the movement of goods within the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Firearms’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Firearms: handguns, shotguns, assault rifles, shotguns or assault rifles classified in the category A category.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 89/852/EEU of 13 June 1989 on the restriction of the acquisition, possession and transfer of firearms (codified version) (recast) (OJ L 29, 13.6.1989, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
 
  Егандарсканка гракован гладана бактарка, годардка  гълакка: горовка (European Firearms Pass)

See consolidated version.
Council Decision 2008/146/EC of 28 January 2008 on the conclusion of the Agreement between the European Union, the European Community and the Swiss Confederation on the implementation, application and development of the agreement relating to the implementation and application of the directive on the security of firearms in the context of the Convention on the Protection of the Internal Market and the Cartagena Agreement (see summary).

Summary 11:
Maritime National Single Window (EMSWe) — reporting formalities for goods to customs and other authorities
Maritime Navigation — Reporting Formalities for Goods to Customs and Other Authorities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the establishment of the maritime National Single Windows
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Maritime Single Window.
It sets out the technical specifications, specifications, quality control mechanisms and procedures for deploying, maintaining and employing the harmonised interface module and the related harmonised elements of the EMSWe.
KEY POINTS
EU countries are responsible for the operation of their maritime national single windows.
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation. The Commission must ensure that the ship identification information, particulars and exemptions that are provided through the national single window are recorded in an EMSWe ship database and are made available for any subsequent port calls within the EU.
In order to enable the reuse of the information provided by declarants, it is necessary to provide for common databases. These include:
a list of dangerous and polluting goods that are to be notified to the maritime N Single Window in accordance with Directive 2002/59/EC and IMO FAL 7, taking into account the relevant data elements from the Convention on the International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES) Convention and Codes;
a common ship sanitation database which is to be established by the Commission.
Each EU country must establish a uniform internet address for the national maritime single window. This address must correspond to the port of destination of the vessel and be accessible to the European Commission and the EU countries’ competent authorities.
A harmonised reporting interface module must be set up for each EU country’s national single portal. It must provide the technical possibility for the providers of port services to make available, separately, to the national authorities a subset of data elements pre-defined at national level.
An EMSWe data set must be established. This data set should contain:
ship identification information;
ship particulars;
common ship sanitation database;
port of destination data elements.
Reporting obligations
The Commission must:
ensure that the information which is provided by the maritime non-governmental organisations (NGOs) is recorded and made available to the competent authorities of EU countries;
take account of the legal acts of the European Union (EU), in particular those relating to the entry and transit of goods, as well as to the protection of human or animal health or against the environment.
Report on the functioning of the regulation:
by 15 August 2027, the Commission must submit to the EU Parliament and the Council an assessment report on its functioning and on the basis of the data and statistics collected. This report must include a description of the measures taken to implement the regulation and its implementation.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 15 August 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the efficiency of maritime transport, including:
the SafeSeaNet system established at national and EU level, which should continue to facilitate the exchange and distribution of information received through the maritime national Single Window between the Member States;
the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA);
the EU Maritime Information System (EMISA).
For more information, see:
‘Maritime Single Window’ (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 952/2013 of 15 July 2013 establishing the Maritime Navigation Single Window and amending Council Regulation (EC) No 139/2013 (OJ L 283, 29.8.2013, pp. 1-8)

Summary 12:
Electronic road tolls: interoperability of the electronic road toll system (EETS)
Electronic traffic management system (ETS)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/680 on the interoperability and operation of the Electronic Road Toll System
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out the principles and procedures for the registration, operation and management of the European Electronic Toll System (ETS)*.
It repeals Directive 2002/58/EC on the electronic toll system.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all new electronic road-toll systems which require the installation or use of an optical identification system (OBE) or satellite positioning* or 5,8 GHz microwave technology.
EETS providers must:
register their OBE with the European Commission;
ensure that the OBE is compatible with other EETS domains and with the existing interoperability constituents of the EETS provider;
provide the Commission with a list of OBEs that are suitable for use in their EETS domain.
The Commission may adopt delegated acts to authorise, authorise or refuse an OBE if it considers that a body notified by another EU country does not meet the criteria provided for in the directive.
Registration of EETS providers
Each EU country must establish a procedure for registering EETS service providers. The procedure should be the same for all EETS users.
An OBE must be installed or used in an EETS operator’s’ domain by a toll charger. The OBE has to be registered with a contact office. The contact office may be a natural person or a public or a private body.
A toll charger must register with the contact office in order to facilitate their contacts with the toll chargers.
When a toll-charging authority receives a request for a toll on a vehicle, it must inform the provider or its authorised representatives within 6 months from the moment it received the request. The provider must inform them of the reasons for the request and provide them with:
a description of the road fee;
the identity of the vehicle;
information concerning the failure to pay a road fee.
In addition, the provider must provide the toll charger with the following information:
the name and registration number of the registered OBE,
the date and place of registration and the name and address of the designated contact office, and
the vehicle registration number.
If the competent authority considers that the provider has not complied with the above requirements, it may issue a decision to revoke the authorisation or to refuse it. The decision must be communicated to the Commission and the other EU countries.
Where a body does not comply with the requirements, the matter is referred to the Electronic Toll Committee referred to in the annex to the directive, which delivers its opinion within 3 months.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
    дантанка бакова гаранда варка  бъларовка (Directive 2016/682/EU) has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
  и
For more information, see:
‘EETS’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Electronic road toll: a system for the payment of tolls on the vehicles of electronic road users by the toll-chargers of road users in the European Union (EU).
* OBE: an electronic identification system that enables the identification of a vehicle when it is detected that it is not paying a toll.
* Mobile communications: the use of mobile communications to communicate with a mobile phone or other mobile device.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  δалкаганца карданска ‘Electronic toll system’

Summary 13:
Safe and well-managed migration: the deployment of immigration liaison officers (Regulation (EU) 2016/679)
Safe and good-managed migratory movements
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 515/2014 on the deployment by the European Commission and the European Agency for Migration and Integration (EASM)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the deployment and management of immigration officers in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The purpose of the regulation is to replace irregular and uncontrolled migratory flows with safe and well managed pathways, through a comprehensive approach aimed at ensuring, at all stages, the efficient management of migration flows in accordance with Chapter 2 of Title V of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU) and the principles contained in the Global Approach to Migration and Mobility (GAMM).
The regulation sets up a steering board to coordinate and coordinate the deployment, management and monitoring of immigration-related officers.
The steering board is composed of representatives of EU countries associated with the implementation, application and development of the Schengen acquis and is chaired by the Commission. It is responsible for:
coordinating and coordinating the activities of the Steering Board;
assisting the Commission in the implementation of the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBSA);
monitoring and monitoring the work of the EASM;
providing information to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions (EEC) on migration policy;
coordination of the work undertaken by the EBSA, the Committee on Migration, Integration and Integration and the Commission’s Migration Policy Directorate-General for Migration Policy.
In addition, the steering board:
provides the Commission with information on the migration-related issues covered by the regulation;
draws up a list of the countries and regions in which the Commission plans to deploy immigration officers;
ensures that liaison officers dealing with migration issues are deployed by different competent authorities and that their mandates and tasks may overlap;
prepares curricula for liaison officers operating within the same third country or region;
organises and disseminates information on liaison officers’ duties and responsibilities;
sets up a secure web-based information exchange platform.
EU countries may receive EU financial support for the execution of the activities referred to in the regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It has applied since 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to combat irregular migration, including:
the creation of a European border and coast guard agency (e.g. the ESSA;
the EASA; and
the European Commission.
For more information, see:
EU migration policy (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Immigration liaison officer: an immigration officer who helps to facilitate and support the networks provided for in this regulation. They are responsible for the coordination and coordination of the actions of the Commission and other EU agencies in the field of migration.

Summary 14:
Biometric data in identity cards and residence cards of family members who are not nationals of a Member State
Biometrics in national IDentity cards and in residence cards for non-EU citizens
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1954 on biometric identifiers in national identity cards
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes minimum security standards and the integration of biometric data into identity cards.
It aims to enhance security and to facilitate the exercise of the rights of free movement by EU citizens and their family members.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that appropriate and effective procedures are in place for the collection, processing and retention of personal data in the context of the application of this regulation.
National IDENTITY CARDS
National identity cards must comply with the specifications set out in part 5 of the International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO) Document 9303 on Machine Readable Travel Documents, which do not serve identification purposes in the issuing Member States, such as the passport card issued by Ireland.
The data elements included on identity cards shall comply with:
security standards/format/specifications;
technical specifications as established by Commission Implementing Decision C(2018) 7767 (see summary);
security specifications as set out by ICAO document 9303.
Family members’ identity cards should comply with this regulation as well as the specifications laid down in the annex to the regulation, including:
the standardised code ‘Family Member EU Art 10 DIR 2004/38/EC’, in the data field;
the number of digits in the card must correspond to the number of members of a family member’s identity card.
Collection of fingerprints
The collection of fingerprints must be collected solely by qualified and duly authorised staff of national authorities, except in the case of applications submitted to the diplomatic and consular authorities of the Member State. Children under the age of 6 years shall be exempt from the requirement to give fingerprints.
Personal data processing
The personal data to be processed must be processed in accordance with the personal data protection principles laid down by the European Union (EU). The personal data must be stored in a secure, unstructured and unalterable format and must be erased as soon as it is erased.
Member States must:
ensure that all personal data are stored and processed in a safe, secure and secure manner;
identify the identity card or residence card of each family member who is not a national of an EU Member State;
use only the standardised codes used by the Member States for the issuance of identity cards to persons incapable of visiting the authorities responsible for issuing identity cards;
inform the European Commission of any new designations or technical specifications that may be introduced in the future.
In addition, Member States must inform the Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee of any changes to the security standards or specifications for identity cards or residence cards that are adopted.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 October 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to improve the security of its internal borders and to ensure the free movement of its citizens.
For more information, see:
EU identity cards (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Identity card: a card or passport issued by an EU country for the purpose of verifying the identity of its nationals.
* Family member: a person whose identity card is issued to his or her family members and whose residence card is valid for a period of 5 years or more.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.e. a document issued to a person who is a member of the EU and whose family members are not.
Council Regulation (EC) No 1030/2002 of 20 September 2002 on the establishment of minimum security specifications for the authentication of national identification cards (OJ L 286, 21.10.2002, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU)* No 1029/2002 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

  ICA OJ L 287, 21 October 2002, p. 1
See consolidated version.

Summary 15:
Interoperability of EU information systems for security, border and migration management
Interoperation of EU data systems for border and immigration management
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/817 on interoperability between the EU Information Systems for Security, Border and Migration Management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the interoperability of the European Union information systems (eu-LISA) for border control, customs, police and judicial matters.
It sets out the conditions for access to the common identity repository for identification (CIR) and the European border and transit information system (ETIAS).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the CIR and the ETIAS central system;
the European Border and Coast Guard (EBCG);
the Schengen Information System (SIS);
Europol.
Access to the CIR or ETIAS Central System
EU countries’ border guards, customs authorities, police officers and judicial authorities have the right to access CIR, ETIAS and SIS databases. They may also access the VIS for consultation. However, they may only do so in accordance with their access rights under EU and national law.
The CIR may only be accessed by authorised persons and staff of external service providers, such as Europol, Eurojust and the SIS.
In cases where Europol has launched a query of the CTIR, it may access the application files stored in the VIS, but only if the reply received reveals that data are stored in CIR.
Ebates
The European border control system (ebates) must keep logs of all data processing operations in the ESP. It must also report to the programme management board, which is composed of representatives of the EU countries and the EBCG.
When a national border guard checks a person’s identity, he or she must indicate in the MID the identity of the person and the colour of the link between the data. The border guard must carry out any additional verification necessary to take a decision on the status and colour of that link.
Where a national authority queries a CIR for information, it must indicate the data referred to in Article 34 of the regulation. The CIR must also indicate the information it has received from Europol and Eurojust.
If a border guard suspects that a person is a threat to public order, he/she must inform the national authorities and the police officers of the country concerned. The national authorities must then inform Europol. The SIS must also notify Europol when it receives a request for information from an EU country.
Measures supporting interoperability
In order to achieve interoperability, the regulation sets out a number of measures, including:
measures to ensure that:
data quality is maintained;
personal data are protected;
data are stored only in a way that is appropriate for the purpose;
there is a clear separation of personal data from other data;
EU information systems should not store DNA profiles or biometric templates obtained from palm print data.
Implementing measures
The Commission has adopted implementing measures to support interoperability. These include:
implementing a communication to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions, the Commission, the Member States and the EU agency concerned;
establishing a European information system interoperability management board.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The EU information system for border security and migration is one of the most important tools used by border guards and customs authorities to prevent, detect and combat illegal immigration and cross-border crime. It is also a key tool in the fight against terrorism.
For more information, see:
Border control and border security (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Common identity repository: a central database containing all the information needed to identify a person or group of people.
* ETIAS: a database containing the information necessary to identify an individual, group or category of persons.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Егаланска на бакова ганта ванка (OJ L 81, 1.2.2019, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, 2019/917 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
last update 14.02.2021



Summary 16:
EU renewable development platform and statistical transfers between Member States
EU renewables development platform
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2010/31/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council on renewable energy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It establishes a European Union (EU) renewable energy development platform.
It aims to encourage investment in renewable energy projects in Member States, including through the use of financial instruments.
KEY POINTS
EU countries have different renewable energy potentials and operate different support schemes at national level.
The directive sets out rules on:
support schemes for renewable energy;
guarantees of origin*;
certification of installations;
EU renewable energy and high-efficiency technologies in their plans and designs.
Support schemes
Member States may establish and make public a list of measures and public bodies, such as fuel suppliers, public or professional bodies, which are to contribute to the average annual increase in the average renewable energy generated by renewable energy installations in their Member States. These measures must comply with the methodological requirements set out in the directive’s annexes.
Guarantee of origin
A guarantee of origin can be transferred, independently of the energy to which it relates, from one holder to another. It is issued in response to a request from a producer of energy from renewable sources, unless Member States decide, for the purposes of accounting for the market value of the renewable energy they receive from a support scheme, not to issue such a guarantee to a producer that receives financial support from such a scheme. A guarantee must be of the standard size of 1 MWh.
A renewable energy producer must be able to prove that the energy it receives from a renewable energy support scheme does not come from a source other than renewable energy sources.
In addition, Member States must ensure that:
the renewable energy produced from renewable energy does not contribute to greenhouse gas emissions;
there is no discrimination between renewable energy producers and those that do not receive financial support;
the energy produced is renewable energy that meets the requirements of the legislation.
EU support schemes
The European Commission must draw up a report every 5 years on the implementation of the directive and on the results of the delegation of power to Member States of the power to adopt delegated acts.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYAPPLY?
 
    бакадарка бълаковка  Екалкацка (Directive 2009/28/EC) has applied since 1 January 2011 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
  Бакданкабка
The EU has adopted a number of measures to promote the development of renewable energy. These include:
Regulation (EC) No 1099/2008 on the recognition of professional qualifications for the European Renewable Energy Agency (ERENA);
Regulations 2001/77/EC and 2003/30/EC establishing definitions for different types of energy and renewable sources;
Council Directive 2009/29/EC on the European renewable energy target (see summary).
For more information, see:
Renewable energy (European Commission).

Summary 17:
Radio Spectrum Management Directive (EU) 2015/1535 — efficient use of radio spectrum (recast)
Radio spectrum Management Directive(EU) 2019/852 — efficient and effective use of the radio spectrum
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the efficient use and effective management of the European Union’s radio spectrum.
It repeals Directive 2002/19/EC, 2002/20/EC and 2002/22/EC.
KEY POINTS
Radio Spectrum management
The directive requires EU countries to:
ensure that radio spectrum* is used effectively and efficiently;
encourage the use of spectrum by operators of wireless network equipment;
provide for the interoperability of radio local area networks (RANs);
establish a procedure for the identification of transnational demand for radio spectrum;
make available to the European Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (ENISA) the necessary economic, technical and market knowledge.
The European Commission must adopt implementing acts detailing the technical and organisational measures referred to in the directive, as well as the circumstances, format and procedures applicable to notification requirements.
EU countries must ensure that:
radio spectrum is used efficiently and effectively;
requests for spectrum are met in a transparent and transparent manner;
the European Commission adopts implementing acts setting out the technical, organisational and financial requirements for the use and efficient management of spectrum; and
the Commission publishes an annual report on radio spectrum management.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Directive (EU)* 2015/1635 has applied since 1 January 2019.
Directives 2002/21/EC (radio spectrum management) and 2002-22/EU (radio-frequency identification) have applied since 31 December 2002.
BACKGROUND
Radio frequencies are essential for the transmission of information and communication technologies (ICTs), including the internet, mobile phones and the internet of things (i.e. the internet). However, due to growing demand, and can be enhanced by integrating or combining them with electronic communications, EU countries must adopt, where appropriate, a cross-sectoral approach.
For more information, see Radio Spectrum Management (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Radio spectrum: a band of electromagnetic spectrum that can be used to transmit, receive and transmit information and communications technologies (e.g. radio signals).

Summary 19:
Solidarity with regard to the supply of gas to EU countries’ citizens in the event of a natural gas supply disruption (SOLIDARITY)
Solidarity between EU countries in the case of a severe disruption in the supply and consumption of natural gas
SOLADARITY
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2017/852 on solidarity between the Member States in the field of energy security
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a system of solidarity between Member States to ensure that gas is supplied to solidarity protected customers in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out the rules and procedures for the coordination of national emergency response plans. These must be updated every 4 years, unless circumstances warrant more frequent updates.
In addition, the competent authorities must conduct a comprehensive national risk assessment and assess natural, technological, commercial, financial, social, political and market-related risks, andany other relevant ones.
The European Commission must submit a report on the results of the assessment to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 2 years.
Member States’ national authorities must consult each other and the Commission on the implementation of the plans and procedures. The Commission must consult the national authorities on the basis of a consultation request within 4 months of receipt of the consultation request.
If a Member State considers that solidarity is not being met, it must inform the Commission of the reasons for its decision.
An emergency response plan must be drawn up for each Member State. It must include:
the name of its competent authority;
the duration of the emergency measures to be taken;
a description of the measures to cope with the situation at alert level;
an estimate of the expected contribution of the measure’s cost;
details of the amount of gas supplied to the affected customers.
A Member State must ensure that solidarity measures are in place to ensure the uninterrupted supply of electricity and gas to solidarity-protected customers in its territory.
Where necessary, Member States must provide the Commission with information on the measures they have taken to ensure uninterrupted gas supply to solidarity customers. This information must be made available to the Commission and the other Member States, including:
information on the number of customers covered by the solidarity measures;
information about the measures taken by Member States;
measures taken by the Commission to respond to emergencies;
assessment of the risks related to:
natural gas supply risk groups and the national risk assessments;
national risk assessments and national contingency plans;
coordination of national and regional emergency responses;
monitoring and monitoring of the situation;
implementation of the national emergency plans.
National emergency plans must include a description of how the measures are to be implemented and how they are expected to be paid for. They must also include information on:
how the measures will be paid;
how they will be funded;
their duration and duration; and
the expected contribution to the measure.
Regional and Union emergency responses
The Commission must:
ensure that Member States:
take the necessary measures, including those agreed in technical, legal and financial arrangements;
provide the necessary information and information to the public and the public authorities;
cooperate with each other to ensure solidarity is met;
make the necessary arrangements for the payment of solidarity charges;
identify the measures that must be taken for the protection of solidarity customers in Member States that are not satisfied with the measures adopted by the Member State requesting solidarity;
draw up a list of the persons and bodies responsible for providing solidarity assistance.
When Member States do not comply with the conditions laid down in the decision, they must inform each other of the reason for their decision and the reasons why.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The Decision has applied since 1 December 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a long history of dealing with natural gas shortages. In addition, a large proportion of such gas is imported into the EU from non-EU countries.
For more information, see:
‘Solidarity’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Natural gas: natural gas supplied by natural gas transmission system operators.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision (EU, Euratom).
-
OJ L 852, 1.12.2017, pp. 1-2

Summary 20:
Energy labelling requirements for energy-related products (Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on energy labelling and information sheets (see summary)
Energy Labelling Requirements for Energy-Related Products
SUMMARY OF:
Decision No 768/2008/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the labelling of energy products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the technical labelling* of energy and non-energy products.
It aims to improve the visibility of products on the market and to promote the use of energy efficiency.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
products placed on the European market or put into service;
suppliers and dealers;
products that are sold in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to energy appliances, appliances, equipment and systems used for heating, cooling, air conditioning, heating and cooling.
Labels
Products must be easily recognisable, simple and concise. They must include information on:
the energy consumption, durability and environmental performance of the product;
the product’s place in the market;
its place in service; and
how it is used.
The labelling must be displayed on the product packaging and in the product database.
Suppliers must ensure that the label and information sheet are clearly visible and clearly visible.
Distributors and dealers must provide consumers with information on the performance of a product, such as:
its energy efficiency, durability, environmental performance and energy efficiency;
energy efficiency* and its place in use;
how the product is used;
whether the product complies with the legislation on energy efficiency and its environmental performance.
Consultation forum
The European Commission must consult the Consultation Forum on energy labels, dealers and consumers on a regular basis. The Commission may also set up campaigns to encourage consumers to buy energy-efficient products. It must support cooperation and the exchange of best practices in relation to those campaigns, including through the recommendation of common key messages.
In addition, the Commission may adopt delegated acts to establish specific product groups of products in accordance with a set of specific criteria and in order to establish product-specific labels.
A product database must be established for each of the categories of products covered by the regulation. The database must:
be user-friendly and cost-effective;
avoid redundancies;
be free from redundant registration;
not be deleted.
Disputes between suppliers and dealers
Dealers must inform the Commission and the market surveillance authorities of any non-compliance of a supplier or dealer with regard to a product. If the Commission considers that a non-compliant product is not restricted to their national territory, they must inform it and the other Member States of the results of the evaluation and the action which they have required the dealer to take.
If a dealer or supplier is found to be in breach of the regulation, the dealer must withdraw the product from the market. The dealer must then inform the European Commission of the reasons for the withdrawal and inform the consumer of the reason for the action.
Where a product does not comply with the regulation and the national measure is considered to be justified and the non-national measure is not justified, all Member States must take the measures necessary to ensure that it is withdrawn from their market.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 21 October 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Energy labelling: a label or information sheet on the packaging of an energy product.
* Class ‘E’, ‘F’ or ‘G’: products that fall into the category ‘energy-related’ and which are classified according to their performance in terms of their energy efficiency or environmental performance, i.e. whether they use electricity, heat, cooling or other sources of energy, and whether they are used for domestic, commercial or industrial purposes.
Class ‘A’ products: products which fall in the category of ‘non-energy’.

Summary 21:
European Data Protection Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the processing of personal data (personal data protection regulation)
Eurodata Protection Regulation
EuroData Regulation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 181/2012 on the protection of the personal data of natural persons, economic operators and public authorities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Data Protection Board (EDPB) to ensure that personal data are protected in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the rules and procedures for the processing, storage and use of the data.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
the processing of all personal data concerning health, scientific or historical research purposes or statistical purposes;
the storage and processing of certain types of personal information;
processing of certain categories of data relating to health, in respect of specific needs, in particular where processing of such data is carried out for certain health-related purposes by persons subject to a legal obligation of professional secrecy.
It does not apply to processing of other types of data, such as those relating to financial transactions.
Consultation and consent
Consent must be given by the data subject before the processing can be carried out. It must be as easy as to withdraw as to give consent. The consent must cover all processing activities carried out on the same purpose or purposes.
The consent must be valid for at least 6 months from the date of processing.
If the consent is not given, the processing must be terminated.
When processing does not require identification, the person concerned must have the right to have their personal data transmitted directly from one controller to another. The controller must keep a record of the processing activities under its responsibility.
In addition, the controller’s representative, who is the person in charge of data protection, must be able to identify the person whose personal data has been processed.
Transfers subject to appropriate safeguards
The data subject must be informed of the risks, rules, safeguards and rights in relation to their processing. They must be provided with a copy of their consent, including:
a description of their rights and obligations;
an explanation of how the processing is being carried out;
a list of the persons whose data have been processed, including their names, addresses, contact details and contact details;
their rights to have access to official documents;
access to the European data protection supervisor, who must be available to the public, to the Commission and to the EDPB.
EU countries must ensure an adequate level of protection equivalent to that ensured within the EU.
Where personal data is processed in a non-EU country, the EU country concerned must ensure that it is protected in line with the rules of the EU and that the data are processed in accordance with the EU data protection law.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
    Еганка бакова гакка  года
It has applied since 1 July 2012 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
  Мбанълакта  
The European Union has a comprehensive data protection system. It is designed to protect the rights and interests of individuals, businesses and the public.
For more information, see:
‘Personal data protection’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 22:
Harmonised index of consumer prices (HICP)
Harmonomically based on observed prices, the HICP is used by the European Commission and the European Central Bank (ECB) to assess price stability in the European Union (EU).
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 701/2006 of 25 April 2006 laying down detailed rules for the implementation of Regulation (EU) No 2494/95 as regards the temporal coverage of price collection in the harmonised index [Official Journal L 122 of 9.5.2006].
SUMMARY
The European Commission (now the European Economic and Social Committee) uses the Harmonised Index of Consumer Prices (HicP) for its assessment of price stability. It is based on observations of prices, which include taxes on products.
HICPs are based on:
the OOH price index and the Household Price Index (HPI) accounting for less than one part in a hundred of the total owner-occupier housing expenditure and total purchases of dwellings, respectively;
the FISIM price index accounting for 0.1 part of a thousand of total expenditure;
sub-indices of the OOH index and of the HPI accounting for no more than 0.01 part of one hundred of total expenditures;
other financial services, such as charges by banks and post offices;
services of carpenters.
The Commission, the ECB and Eurostat must:
produce, where available, electronic records of transactions and at the level of detail necessary in order to produce harmonised indices and to evaluate compliance with the comparability requirements and the quality of the indices.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 11(2).
In addition, the implementing acts shall:
require the statistical units to:
provide the Commission with the electronic records and the data it needs to produce the indices;
make available to the Commission, Eurostat and Euroforecasts the inventories and the deadline for providing these inventories to Eurostat;
require Eurostat to submit a report every 3 years to the European Parliament and the Council on the harmonisation of the consumer price indices and on the quality and comparability of the index data;
allow Eurostat, Euroforecast and Eurojust to submit periodic reports to Eurojust on the status of the HicP.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
   
The HICPs have been used for over 20 years to assess the price stability of the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Hicp’ on Eurostat’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Regulation (EEC) no 802/2006
of 26 April 2006 establishing detailed rules concerning the temporal cover of price-collection in the Harmonized index of Consumer prices [Official J L 122, 9.6.2006]
