Summary 23:
Anti-dumping Regulation (EU) No 952/2013 on the protection of the EU’s internal market against dumping
Anti-Dumping Regulation(EU) no longer codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 182/2011
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the protection against dumping and injury caused by imports of certain agricultural products from non-EU countries.
It codifies the rules on dumping, injury and countervailing measures adopted by the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Importers of agricultural products must register with the European Commission before they can export them to the EU. The Commission then investigates whether these products are subject to dumping and/or injury.
Imports must not be made subject to registration for a period longer than 9 months.
Dumping and injury
The aim of the regulation is to prevent the dumping or injury of products originating in the EU by means of imports from countries which are not members of the European Economic Area (EEA).
The regulation does not apply to imports originating in non-EEA countries. However, it may be applicable to imports from the EU's major trading partners.
Provisional or definitive measures
The European Commission may impose provisional or definitive anti–dumping duties on imports originating from countries outside the EEA if it considers that there is a threat of dumping or a causal link between the imports and the dumping of a product and the harm caused by the imports.
The Commission may decide to suspend or reduce the duties if there is sufficient to justify it.
If the Commission considers that the threat of injury is more likely to be realised if the dumped imports continue.
Expiry of the measures
An expiry review may be initiated where there is evidence that the expiry of measures would be likely to result in a continuation or recurrence of dumping.
Duration, reviews and refunds
The exporter may submit a voluntary undertaking offer to revise its prices or to cease exports at dumped prices, if it is satisfied that the injurious effect of the dumping is thereby eliminated.
In addition, the Commission may, in accordance with the advisory procedure referred to in the regulation, accept satisfactory voluntary undertaking offers submitted by any exporter to revise or cease exports.
Where the Commission concludes that the importer has not complied with the conditions laid down in this regulation, the exporter must refund the amount of the duty. The amount of refund must not exceed 2 % for individual exporters and they remain subject to the proceedings and may be reinvestigated in any subsequent review carried out for the country concerned pursuant to Article 11.
EU countries may refuse to grant exemptions to producers of the product concerned that can show that they are not related to any producer subject to measures and that are found not to be engaged in circumvention practices as defined in paragraphs 1 and 2 of this regulation. Where the circumventing practice, process or work takes place outside the EU, exemptions may be granted to producers if they are found to be not related the Commission has completed its analysis thereof.
Amendments to the regulation
Amending the regulation has been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
Regulation (EU)* No 852/2014 has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Dumping’ (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Countervailing measure: measures to protect the internal market from the adverse effects of imports of goods which are likely to cause it to be harmed.
* Prohibited import: measures which are intended to prevent or reduce imports of products which are liable to cause harm to the internal economy.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  барканска гракта бълакова (codified version)
Commission Implementing Decision of 29 November 2014 on the codification and repeal of Council Regulation (OJ L 343, 29.12.2014, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
 
 банка  глагакца (Codified version), Euratom Implementing Regulation (JHA) No 552/2011 of 29 October 2011 laying down implementing measures for the Protection of the Internal Market against the adverse impact of imports originating outside the Union and repealing Council Regulation No 182(2011) No 609/2011, and Commission Implementing Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 1/2014 and (EC) 2015/852 of 29 September 2011 on the Protection against the Anti-Countervailing Measures against the Illicit Trade in Agricultural Products of the Member States and repealing Commission Regulation (EC), No 605/2004 (O J L 343 of 29.11.2011, pp 1-2)




Summary 24:
Implementing Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on subsidising imports of agricultural products
Implementating Regulation (U) No 181/2011 laying down the rules on subsidised imports of certain agricultural products (codified version)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 184/2011 — subsidising agricultural imports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (EC) No 1216/2009 laying down rules on the trade arrangements applicable to certain goods resulting from the processing of agricultural agricultural products.
It aims to improve the transparency of the EU’s internal market for agricultural products and to protect the interests of producers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to imports of:
agricultural products originating in or exported from the European Union (EU);
foodstuffs originating in, exported from or imported from non-EU countries;
animal feed;
seeds originating in and exported from outside the EU;
sugars and other agricultural inputs.
The European Commission may launch investigations into the subsidisation of agricultural imports if it considers that these imports are causing injury to producers or are likely to cause injury.
Complaints
A complaint may be submitted to the Commission or to a Member State, which forwards it to the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD).
The complaint must be submitted in writing. It must include:
the product and countries concerned;
the name of the producer or exporter or country;
a description of the type of the product and the type and quantity of the inputs to be substituted in the production process.
the type of products and quantities of inputs substituted.
If the complaint is found to be causing injury, the Commission may decide to open an investigation into the allegations of subsidisation and/or to impose a countervailing duty on the imports of the affected products. The Commission may also impose a provisional or definitive duty on these imports. If the definitive duty is lower than the provisional duty, the duty must be recalculated.
In the event that an investigation is opened, the European Commission must inform the Member States of its analysis of the complaint normally within 21 days of the date on which the complaint was lodged with the Commission.
A committee composed of representatives of Member States and chaired by the Commission is responsible for the management of the investigation. It may request information and may exchange views in the committee or directly with the European Parliament and the Council.
An investigation may be terminated without measures if the Commission concludes that the allegations are not justified. The investigation may continue for a period of 5 years, or be terminated in the event of a finding of injury or if the investigation concludes that there is no evidence that the subsidies are causing harm to the producers or the exporters.
Reviews and refunds
Member States may request a review of the measures in force to determine whether and to what extent a refund is justified. If a review, carried out in accordance with the the provisions applicable for such reviews, is justified, a refund may be issued.
Where the Commission considers that a refund has been justified, it must notify the parties concerned of the reasons for its decision.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to protect producers’ interests in the field of agriculture, including:
Directive 2014/34/EU on the harmonisation of the laws and regulations on trade in agricultural products between Member States;
Imposition of Regulation (Euratom) 2018/852 on agricultural subsidies.
For more information, see:
‘Subsidised imports’ on the EAFRD’ website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (UE) No 185/2011 of 21 December 2011 laying down a system for the subsidising of imports of some agricultural products by Member States (Codified version).
Successive amendments and corrections to the regulation have been incorporated in to the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 25:
Recovery and resilience facility
Resilience Facility
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1999 on the establishment of a Recovery and Resiliency Facility
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union’s recovery and resilience fund.
It aims to provide effective and significant financial support to step up the implementation of sustainable reforms and related public investments in the Member States.
KEY POINTS
The facility is designed to support projects that respect the principle of additionality of EU funding. It is open to all Member States, including the European Commission and the European Economic Area (EEA).
It is funded by the EU budget and is managed by the European Stability Mechanism (ESM).
Objectives
The Facility supports the following objectives:
recovery and resilience of the economy and society in the face of economic and social challenges;
promotion of sustainable and inclusive growth and jobs;
improvement of the quality of living standards of the most vulnerable in the EU;
improving the competitiveness of the labour market;
supporting the sustainable development of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) in the context of climate change and the green transition;
encouraging the development of new technologies;
fostering innovation in the field of information and communication technologies (ICTs);
promoting the sustainable use of renewable energy;
ensuring the sustainable management of natural resources;
contributing to the creation of jobs and growth in Europe.
Specific objectives
The European Commission is assisted by a steering committee composed of representatives of Member States and the private sector.
The Commission, the European Parliament and the Council of the EU. The Commission is responsible for the management of the facility. The committee is chaired by the Commission and comprises representatives of the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the EEA countries. The EIB manages the ESM and its financial resources.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 February 2020.
BACKGROUND
The ESM was established under Regulation (EC) No 1467/97 on the European Recovery and Reintegration Facility (see summary).
For more information, see:
‘The European Union Recovery and Innovation Facility’ on the ESI's website.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition into force:
1.1.2020

Summary 26:
Technical support instrument for green and digital transitions
Technical support instruments for green, digital and sustainable transitions
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/241 establishing a Technical Support Instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a technical support instrument (TSI) for green technologies and sustainable development.
It aims to provide technical support to the European Commission and Member States in the field of green technologies, sustainable development and environmental and social challenges.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out a list of technical support projects that are eligible for technical support from the Commission and the Member States.
The instrument’s objectives are to:
foster green technologies;
support the sustainable development of the European Union (EU);
contribute to the creation of a more sustainable and inclusive economy;
improve the quality of life of EU citizens;
promote the sustainable use of natural capital, preserving biodiversity and the circular economy and the energy transition;
encourage the development of sustainable technologies.
To achieve these objectives, the Commission must:
ensure that technical support actions are coordinated and coordinated by the Commission;
provide information, communication and publicity to the public, the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic Area (EEA) countries;
make available final studies or reports produced as part of eligible actions.
In addition, the instrument must provide the Commission with:
a single online public repository through which it may, subject to applicable rules and on the basis of consultation with Member States concerned, make available final reports and studies and reports produced by eligible actions;
a list of approved projects;
regularly inform Member States of projects in which it has received a request for support;
assess the urgency, breadth and depth of the challenges identified, support needs in respect of the policy areas concerned, analysis of socioeconomic indicators and institutional and general administrative capacity of the EU countries concerned.
Implementation
The Commission is responsible for:
monitoring and evaluating the technical support provided for in the regulation;
coordinating and coordinating the work of the Commission, Member States and the private bodies and entities legally established in:
Member States;
European Free Trade Association countries which are party to the EU Economic Area Agreement, in accordance with the conditions laid down therein.
Transparency and sound financial management
The European Commission must ensure that:
technical support projects are carried out in line with the principles of transparency, equal treatment and financial management;
the Commission is able to make available the results of its investigations into technical support activities on the ground;
all technical support is protected by means of proportionate measures, including measures relating to the prevention, detection, correction and investigation of irregularities, including fraud, to the recovery of funds lost, wrongly paid or incorrectly used, and to the imposition of administrative penalties.
Information, communication, publicity and publicity
In order to ensure the proper functioning of the instrument, it should be able to engage in communication activities. It should also be possible to publish information on its website and on its social media channels.
Coordination and complementarity
The technical support instruments must be coordinated and complemented by:
the European Parliament and the Council, including the Committee of the Regions, the Committee on the European Semester, the Social Committee and the Committees of the States, the Economic and Social Committee, the committee on the environment and the committee of the regions, the committees on the social committee and the committees of the states and the bodies responsible for the implementation of the instruments; and
the Committee on Europe and the Southern Neighbourhoods, which is composed of representatives of Member States’ representatives.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat climate change and climate change-related challenges in recent years. These include:
Implementing the Green and Sustainable Development Agenda (GSA) (see summary);
the Green Agenda for Europe (see overview);
The Green Strategy for Europe, which aims to improve the quality and sustainability of life in the EU by 2030.
For more information, see:
‘Green technologies’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Technical support instrument: an instrument that provides technical support for actions that foster green technologies. It is an instrument designed to support Member States' efforts to develop green technologies in order to achieve the objectives of the Green Agenda.

  Ебракова городна на баркодска (Technical Support Instrument for Green Technologies) (OJ L 81, 1.1.2021, pp. 1-8)

Summary 27:
Rail passengers’ rights and obligations — Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007 on rail passengers‘ rights and responsibilities
Rail passengers' rights and requirements
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1201/2013 on rail passenger rights and obligation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the rights and conditions for passengers travelling by rail in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve rail passenger’s rights.
KEY POINTS
Passengers have the right to:
have their tickets and reservations confirmed;
be informed of the availability of tickets, reservations and through-tickets and re-routing;
have access to information during the journey;
receive compensation for any loss or damage caused by the carriage of their luggage;
make a request for a temporary replacement equipment or device.
Railways and, where possible, ticket vendors and tour operators must provide the passenger with at least the information set out in Annex II, Part II. This information must be sufficient to have continuous access to the information they provide.
The regulation does not apply to the carriage charge, which must be paid by the railway undertaking or to the person with disabilities or persons with reduced mobility. The carriage charge must not exceed €4 per ticket.
Payment and refund of carriage charge
The carriage charge may be waived or refunded if:
the carriage is subject to an examination by the carrier or carriers;
a statement that the carriage charges are subject to certain conditions;
the passenger is not entitled to compensation for damage or loss;
there is a delay in delivery;
it is not the responsibility of the passenger.
Compensation for damage and loss of articles, hand luggage or animals
The passenger is responsible for the total or partial loss of, or damage to, articles or hand luggage and animals the supervision of which is the passenger's responsibility, unless it is caused by a fault of the carrier.
A person with disability or a person who is reduced in mobility may be entitled to a compensation of up to €10,000 for the loss of a hand luggage, or for the damage caused to a handbag or handbag. The compensation may be reduced to €5,000 if it is the fault of a person with a disability or reduced mobility, or if the carriage is not subject to the conditions laid down in the regulation.
In addition, the regulation sets out a number of other rights, such as:
access to information;
availability of tickets and reservation;
transport;
information on the conditions for the transport of EU countries;
travel arrangements.
Passenger rights
Railway undertakings must:
ensure that:
passengers are informed of all information they need during their journey, including:
ticket prices;
ticket information and reservations;
ticket information and reservation information on travel arrangements;
conditions for the re-arrangements of services;
exclusions from certain obligations as regards the provision of through tickets and rerouting.
They must also:
provide passengers with information on:
tour operators’ services and information on information to be provided pursuant to this regulation;
visits and events;
services for passengers with disabilities and persons who have reduced mobility;
service for persons who are not physically able to travel by rail.
Transport arrangements for persons with disabilities, persons with a reduced mobility and persons with physical disabilities must be:
comprehensive and consistent with the rules laid down by the COTIF;
consistent with the uniform rules of the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on rail safety and security (COTIF);
comparable to those of the United Nations Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities and Persons with Reduced Mobility (UNESCO Convention on Railways).
The European Parliament and the Council may adopt measures, in accordance with the principle of subsidiarity, to improve the rights of rail passengers. These measures may include:
improving the conditions of transport for passengers;
establishing a European Union Railways Information System (ERISA);
introducing a European Rail Safety Information System;
implementing measures to improve passenger information on the rail system.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 23 February 2013 and had to become law in the EU countries by 23 February 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU has adopted a series of laws on rail transport since 2007. These have been amended several times, most recently in 2013.
For more information, see:
European Railways (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Carriage charge: a charge charged by a railway company to cover the cost of transporting a passenger. This charge is calculated on the basis of the distance between the carriage and the destination of the railway station. It does not exceed 8 000 units of account.



Summary 28:
External assistance for the Western Balkans
External aid for the western Balkans
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/947 — framework for planning and delivering external assistance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework for the European Union’s external assistance programme for the period between 2021 and 2027.
KEY POINTS
The European External Action Service (EEAS) is the main instrument for providing assistance to the Western Balkan countries in preparation for accession to the EU.
The aim of this regulation is to:
promote sustainable development in the region;
ensure the sustainable development of the region and its economic, social and cultural development;
support the efforts of the European Green Deal;
contribute to the implementation of the United Nations Instrument for Pre-accession Assistance (IPA III) and the European Peace and Security Instrument (PSI III);
support regional cooperation in the field of peace, security and peace;
provide the European Commission with information, communication and visibility on the progress made by the beneficiaries of the EAS.
In order to maintain the EU's effectiveness in external action, a framework is set up for 2021-2027. The EAS is based on a performance-based approach and the fair share principle, ensuring progress in all beneficiaries listed in Annex I.
It also includes a strategic planning framework and general principles, based on the indicators referred to in Article 6 of the IPA III.
A strategic board advises the Commission on EAG operations. The strategic board is assisted by a committee for the instrument for Preaccession Pre-Accession Assistance, which is composed of representatives of the EU countries and the Commission.
EAG operations are financed by the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the European Social Fund for Rural Development (EFSD+) and regional cooperation.
EU countries contribute to the provisioning needs in respect of the operations to the benefit of the beneficiaries. The European Commission, in particular, contributes to the financing of the actions implemented and managed by the EAGs.
Implementing rules
The Commission adopts implementing rules establishing uniform conditions for implementing this regulation. These apply to the structures and instruments that have proved their worth in the pre-accessional process.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 7 September 2021 and had to become law in the Member States by 27 March 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Western Balkans are one of the countries which have applied for EU membership and are eligible for financial assistance under the EU external action programme.
For more information, see:
‘Western Balkans’ (European Commission).

Summary 29:
External Action Guarantee (EAG) 2021-2027 — European Union External Action Instrument (EU-EFSD+)
External Action Grant (EU–EFSD+) 2021 - 2027
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Decision (EU) 2021/2027 on the European Union’s external action guarantee
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE INITIATION?
The European Union (EU)-EFSD* Instrument aims to ensure consistency between the different areas of its external action and between these and its other policies, as well as to work for a high degree of cooperation in all fields of international relations.
The decision and the instrument are part of a package of measures designed to ensure that the EU is consistent with its foreign and security policy objectives.
KEY POINTS
   
The EAG is a multiannual instrument designed to:
contribute to the implementation of the Paris Agreement and to achieve the UN for-profit independent and non-violent structures, through which people organise the pursuit of shared objectives and ideals, whether political, cultural, religious, environmental, social or economic;
promote human rights, democracy and good governance, security and stability, climate change and the environment, and oceans, and to challenges related to migration and forced displacement and their root causes;
support the United Nations Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs);
support international organisations, such as the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY) and the Organisation for the Prohibition of Chemical Weapons (OJ Cotonou);
provide technical assistance to developing countries in the field of nuclear energy and nuclear energy;
cooperate with non-governmental organisations, including the International Maritime Organisation (IMO);
ensure the protection of human rights and the rule of law in the area of international law and human rights violations;
encourage international cooperation in the fields of education, training, human rights law, gender equality and the fight against violence;
contribut to the prevention and eradication of human trafficking and the eradication and elimination of child sexual exploitation.
Joint programming
The EU-EFDS+ Instrument covers a number of thematic programmes, including:
a programme covering African, Caribbean and Pacific countries under points (b), (c) and (d) of the first subparagraph and a programme covering non-EU countries under the second subparagraph;
a joint programme covering the Middle East, North-East Asia and South-East Europe under the third subparagraph and an action programme covering South-east Asia under the fourth subparagraph.
Cooperation between the EU and its Member States, on the one hand, and partner countries, in the other, shall be based on and shall promote the development effectiveness principles, where applicable, across all modalities, namely: ownership of development priorities, a focus on results, inclusive development partnerships, transparency and mutual accountability. Joint programming should build on the partner countries’ engagement, appropriation and ownership.
In line with the Consensus, the Union and its EU countries should enhance joint programming to increase their collective impact by bringing together their resources and capacities in the relevant fields of cooperation.
Eligible persons and entities
EU countries and territories are eligible for the EAG. The European Investment Bank (EIB) is the main source of funding for overseas countries, territories and EU countries and the European Economic Area (EEA). The EIB is also responsible for providing financial support to the European Commission, the European External Action Service (EEAS), the European Agricultural Fund for Reconstruction and Development (EAFRD) and other international organisations. The EEA and the EAFRD are also eligible for financial support from the European Social Fund (ESF).
The Commission may request any relevant information from eligible counterparts about their non-EESD+ operations. The Commission may also request information from the EEA, the EAS, the EU countries or the European Atomic Energy Community (EAC) on the activities of the EIB.
EU funding
The budget for the period 2021 to 2027 is up to € 53.49 billion. This includes:
€ 53.4 billion from the EU budget and up to EUR 53.9 billion for the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF);
€ 1.8 billion from EASD;
€ 2.5 billion from Euratom.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Decision 2011/852/EU has applied since 1 January 2021 and the EU-EASD Instrument has applied from 1 January 2027.
BACKGROUND
External actions are often implemented in a highly volatile environment requiring continuous and rapid adaptation to the evolving needs of EU partners, to global challenges to human rights (democratic and good Governance, security, stability, environment and oceans) and to the causes of forced displacement.
For more information, see:
External action guarantee (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* External action guarantee: an instrument of the EU



Summary 30:
Protection of whistleblowers: EU competition law breaches
Protection against breaches of Union competition law
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/865 on the protection of whistleblowers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
It aims to protect whistleblowers* who report breaches of EU competition laws that are harmful to the public interest.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that whistleblowers are protected against any adverse effects resulting from their reporting. These include:
reputational damage, such as denial of provision of services, blacklisting or business boycotting;
disputes over compensation for actual and future financial losses, including lost past wages, but also for future loss of income, costs linked to a change of occupation;
legal expenses and costs of medical treatment, and for intangible damage such as pain and suffering.
Reporting persons
The directive applies to any person who:
reports a breach falling within the scope of the directive;
is the subject of an investigation by national authorities or judicial proceedings;
acts as a whistle-blower in the context of investigations by EU or national authorities, judicial proceedings or investigations triggered by the report.
The person concerned may be subject to a number of conditions, including:
the confidentiality of the identity of the reporting person during the reporting process and investigations;
the protection of the person’s rights of defence;
protection from any other adverse effects arising from the reporting persons.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that the person concerned is provided with appropriate legal advice;
notify the European Commission and the other EU countries of the results of the investigations and judicial proceedings.
If the Commission receives a report on a breach of EU law that is harmful to public interest, it must inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee on the Functioning of the European Union (EEU).
The Commission must also:
keep a record of the reports it receives;
make a report available to the media, elected officials, civil society organisations, trade unions, or professional and business organisations.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU has a long history of protecting whistleblowers. In particular, the prudential framework applicable to credit institutions and investment firms, Directive 2013/36/EU establishes a system of protection for whistleblowers.
For more information, see:
‘Whistleblower protection’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 31:
European integrated border management
European integrated borders management
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/399 on the European Border and Coast Guard Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European border and coast guard agency (EBCG)* and sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the EBCG.
KEY POINTS
   
The European border management is central to improving migration management. It aims to detect, prevent and combat illegal immigration and cross-border crime. It covers land, sea and air border surveillance and border checks.
It is based in Warsaw, Poland.
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Ireland and the United Kingdom (1), which do not take part in the regulation.
EU countries’ participation in the standing corps through long-term secondment is voluntary. The European Union’s external border management (EU-wide border management) consists of the following:
a standing corps composed of officers from each EU country;
a liaison officer based in a non-EU country and liaison officers based in an EU country.
Agency management board comprising representatives of the EU countries and the European Commission.
Operational tasks
The ECBG is responsible for:
monitoring, detection, identification, tracking, prevention and interception of unauthorised border crossings for the purpose of detecting, preventing and combating illegal immigration;
detecting, detecting, identifying, tracking and preventing and preventing illegal border crossings;
checking of third-country nationals and the acquisition of travel documents.
the management board is composed of representatives of EU countries;
the executive director is appointed by the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Council of the European Union (EEU) and is assisted by a management board.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCEA
 
It entered into force on 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The purpose of the regulation is to lay down the rules and procedures for the functioning of the agency.
This regulation constitutes a development of provisions of the Schengen acquis in which the UK does not participate.
For more information, see:
European border management and border control (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Standing Corps: a standing corps consisting of officers and technical crews from at least 2 EU countries.
* Liaison officer: an officer assigned to a country by the EU country concerned or at the request of that country. The term of office of the liaison officer is 4 years.

Summary 32:
Information and communications technology (ICT) underpins the complex systems which support everyday societal activities, keep our economies running in key sectors such as health, energy, finance and transport, and, in particular, support the functioning of the internal market.
ACT
Directive (EU) 2018/1972 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 28 June 2018 on cybersecurity in the digital single market
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It establishes the European Network of Information Security Assurance Institutions (ENISA).
It aims to improve the cybersecurity of ICT products, ICT services and ICT processes in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Ensuring a high level of cybersecurity in ICT is essential to protect the EU’s economic, social and cultural interests.
To achieve this, ENISA:
provides advice, issuing guidelines and exchanging best practice on cybersecurity to EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies;
encourages the use of cybersecurity products, services and processes;
ensures that cybersecurity products and processes are certified and that they comply with the relevant security requirements.
The European cybersecurity certification framework (ECCG) is based on a number of principles, including:
the use of the appropriate cybersecurity certification and associated security requirements by the users;
the security requirements of a specific cybersecurity certification scheme.
ENISA is composed of the following:
a management board composed of representatives from EU countries, in collaboration with the European Cybersecurity Certification Group (ECCG);
an advisory committee composed of national representatives of EU countries;
a stakeholder cybersecurity certification group;
an independent cybersecurity certification body.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive applies from 28 June 2021.
It repeals Directive 2004/105/EC and its subsequent amendments.
BACKGROUND
The cybersecurity sector is one of the most important challenges facing the EU and its economic and social interests. This is why it is essential for the EU to take action to improve cybersecurity.
For more information, see:
‘Cybersecurity’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Cybersecurity: attacks performed by persons who have significant skills and resources.

Summary 33:
Quality and comparability of European Union (EU) gross value added (GNI) data
Quality and comparable quality and comparable GNI data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 608/2014 on the transmission of data subject to statistical confidentiality to the European Union’s statistical office
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European system of own resources (ESA) for the collection and transmission of statistics on the quality, reliability, comparability and exhaustiveness of the European Gross Value Added (GVA) aggregates.
It sets out the procedures for the transposition of GNI aggregates and their components.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on the sources, their uses and the methods used to produce the aggregates by 1 October each year. These data must be reliable, exhaustive and comparable.
The Commission must verify the sources and methods in the inventory of sources, the methods and the sources of the sources used and sources of sources. The inventory must be consistent with ESA 2010 and duplication and overloading must be avoided.
Eurostat must publish a report on the methodology used for producing the data for previous years at the end of each verification cycle. That report must detail the methodology and sources used for the previous years.
Before 1 October every year, Member States must provide Eurostat, in the context of national accounting procedures, with figures for GNI aggregate and components, in accordance with the definitions referred to in Article 1 of the regulation.
GVA aggregates must be comparable and reliable.
Each year, Eurostat must submit a report to the Commission on the reliability, exhaustiveness and ex-post quality of the data it has received. This report must describe the methodology, sources, methods and sources for the GNI and its sources.
In order to ensure that the data are reliable, exhaustible and comparable, the Commission must adopt delegated acts in respect of the list of issues to be addressed in every verification cycle in order to:
ensure the accuracy, reliability and completeness of the information provided by Member States;
identify the sources that are reliable and exhaustive;
verify the methods, sources and sources that have been used in the past verification cycles;
assess the quality of data provided by each Member State;
make necessary adjustments, where applicable, to avoid gaps and double counting.
Delegated acts
The delegated acts conferring powers on the Commission for a period of 5 years from 18 April 2019. A decision to revoke the delegated acts will put an end to the delegated powers.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval procedures for vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units
EU type–approval rules for vehicles and systems
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1628 on the placing on the market of new vehicles, vehicles, parts, components, separate technical unit and systems of the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedures for the granting of EU type‑approval* for vehicles* and systems*, components*, separate technical* units* of the vehicles of the type covered by the withdrawal agreement between the EU and the United Kingdom (U.K.).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
vehicles, systems* and components* of type-approved vehicles;
separate technical units of type approved by the EU;
vehicle systems, systems and components which have been approved by an EU type‐approval authority.
It does not apply to vehicles which have not been type‐approved by an authority in the EU Member State of the same Member State and which are subject to the same type‐conditions as those of vehicles approved in the Member States of the EU under Directive 2007/46/EC.
The regulations apply to the following types of vehicles:
end-of‐series vehicles, including:
cars,
crossovers,
trucks,
motorcycles,
battery-electricity vehicles,
trailers, and
cargo vehicles.
Vehicle type‐type-approvals are also held by manufacturers established in Member States other than the United Nations (UN) Member States.
Member States must notify the European Commission of the vehicle type‐certificate of conformity and the number of certificates issued by the competent authority in relation to the vehicle.
When manufacturers make use of the procedures set out in this regulation, their UK type‐approval may become invalid before EU type-authorisation legislation ceases to apply to and in the UK due to granting of the Union type‐authorisation for the same vehicle. However, Member States may decide to continue to grant the UK type‑certificate until the vehicle’s type‐car type‐conformity has become invalid.
If Member States consider that the withdrawal of the UK from the EU is likely to have a significant impact on the functioning of the single market, they may adopt measures to ensure equal treatment of manufacturers affected by the UK withdrawal from the Union and those holding type‑approved vehicle type certificates.
In this respect, the regulation sets out:
the number of Member States that must inform the Commission when a vehicle, system, component or separate component has been granted EU type•approval;
the date on which the type‑authorisation certificate and the type-certificate have become invalid;
where a Member State has notified the Commission that a vehicle has been given EU type approval, it must inform that Member State that the vehicle has not yet been put on the EU market, registration, sale or entry into service of vehicles conforming to a type whose UK type–authorisation has not become valid before the day the regulation ceases to be applicable.
Where Member States decide that the regulation does not comply with the principle of subsidiarity, the Commission must adopt measures in accordance with Article 27 of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU) to ensure that Member States continue to have the greatest possible freedom to choose the new Union type–type‐authority.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 30 March 2019.
BACKGROUND
The United Kingdom withdraws from the European Economic Area (EEA) on 1 March 2019 and the EU has to conclude a bilateral agreement with the EU on type‐recognition of vehicles and their systems.
For more information, see:
Vehicles and systems (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Vehicle type: a vehicle of type ‘e.g.’: an engine, transmission, chassis, suspension, differential, separate electrical system, separate mechanical unit, separate electronic component and separate electrical unit.
* System: a system, system or separate electrical component that allows a vehicle to be driven without any steering or suspension input.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.e. the regulation on the typeapproval procedure for vehicles (OJ L 157, 30.3.2016, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU)* 2016/216 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 35:
European Police Office (Europol) — rules on the protection of personal data
European Police Officer (EPSO)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (JHA) No 552/2009 on the establishment of a European Police Office
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes rules for Europol, the European Union’s (EU) police office.
It aims to ensure operational effectiveness and to protect personal data.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the management board of Europol;
the Executive Director;
data protection officers;
national supervisory authorities;
international organisations;
other EU bodies, authorities of non-EU countries and international organisations.
The Executive Director is appointed by the European Parliament and the Council of the EU and has a term of office of 5 years. The Executive Director may be replaced by a staff member other than the Executive Director.
Europol may make use of seconded national experts.
Management Board
The management board consists of representatives of EU countries and the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF). It meets at least once a year. Each representative has a voting right. It is chaired by an EU country and consists of one representative from each EU country.
Executive Director
The executive director is assisted by a Data Protection Officer (EDPS). The EDPS is responsible for the management of the Europol data protection system and the management, including:
ensuring that personal data are protected;
keeping detailed records of all personal data transfers;
monitoring and safeguarding;
personal data protection;
assisting the management board and the EDPS in the performance of its tasks.
Data Protection Officer
The EDPS must be in a position to perform his or her duties and tasks independently and effectively, and should be provided with the necessary resources to do so.
EU countries must ensure that:
data subjects have the right to have their personal data collected, processed and stored in a secure and confidential manner; and
the protection of their data is ensured.
In the event of a data breach, Europol must communicate the personal data breach to the data subject without undue delay. It must also inform the data protection officer of the reasons for its objection.
Exchanges of data with private parties
The Europol may directly retrieve and process information, including personal data, from publicly available sources, including the internet and public data. It may also directly exchange all relevant information, with the exception of information relating to personal data*, with entities referred to in Article 19(2) of this regulation, in so far as such an exchange is relevant for the purpose of the organisation of its functions.
When Europol receives a request for information from a private party, it must inform the private party of the reason for the request. The private party must then inform Europol of its reasons for objecting to the request and provide Europol with a copy of its consent. This consent may be withdrawn at any time.
If a personal data subject objects to the processing of their own personal data by Europol in the course of its work, they must be given the opportunity to explain why. If they do not comply with this request, they will be informed of the consequences of their objection and of the legal remedies available to them.
Information exchange
The European police office may directly exchange information with other EU bodies and authorities of third countries, international organisations in accordance with Article 19 (2) and without prejudice to Article 67 (3) of the Convention based on Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union, on establishing a European police Office (the Europol Convention).
In addition, it may exchange all information with entities listed in the Protocol on the Protection of Personal Data with the United Kingdom, the United States of America and the United Nations Security Council (the United Nations Special Committee on Combating Terrorism).
The Management Board is composed of representatives from the Member States and the EU countries. It meets twice a year at the request of the European Commission.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The EPSO was set up in 2003.
For more information, see:
‘Europol’ on Europol's website.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition into force

1.1.2010

Summary 36:
EU financial assistance for COVID-19 outbreak in the European Union (COVID-20)
EU financial aid for the EU in the light of COVID–19 outbreak
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 on financial assistance to Member States experiencing exceptional difficulties caused by exceptional occurrences beyond their control
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It enables the Council to grant EU financial assistance* to a Member State that is in difficulties or is seriously threatened with severe difficulties due to an exceptional occurrence beyond its control.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to any Member State which:
is experiencing difficulties or serious threats to its financial stability due to the COVID‑19 outbreak;
is seriously threatened by the effects of the outbreak on the labour markets of its citizens;
has not been able to pay its social security contributions.
The Member States concerned must:
provide evidence of actual or planned expenditure directly related to short-time work schemes and similar measures, as well as, where appropriate, to relevant health-related measures, in the Member State requesting financial assistance which are linked to the exceptional occurrence caused by COVID‐19;
be able to demonstrate that they have sufficient financial resources to cover the costs of these measures;
have the capacity to repay the loans.
In addition, the Member States must provide the European Commission with:
a description of the actual and planned expenditure related to the short-term work schemes or similar measures that may be financed;
a list of the national short-temperature work schemes, as defined in the regulation’s;
evidence of the costs related to these schemes;
the costs of the relevant health measures.
If financial assistance is granted, the Commission must verify that the expenditure relates to the measures that are funded by the financial assistance. The Commission must also verify compliance with the prudential rules laid down in Article 9.
Beneficiary Member States may open a special account with the European Central Bank (ECB).
The European Commission may grant loans to the beneficiary Member States in the form of a loan granted by the Union to the EU. The amount of loans granted to the three Member States representing the largest share of the loans granted shall not exceed 60% of the maximum amount referred to in Article 5.
Administration of loans
The Commission must establish the necessary arrangements for the administration of loans with the ECB. In addition, it must inform the ECB of the conditions for the payment of loans. The ECB must also ensure that the loans are not subject to any financial irregularities.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
The Council of the EU adopted Regulation (EU) No 122/2002/TFEU (see summary) on the application of the Treaty on European Union to exceptional occurrences.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union financial assistance instrument’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Short-temporary work scheme: a scheme for income replacement for the self-employed.
* Health-related measure: a measure designed to prevent or reduce the impact of an outbreak on public health.
Short-term working scheme: an arrangement designed to reduce or eliminate public expenditure on social security and social protection schemes.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Euratom) 2019/852 of 30 March 2019 on financial aid to Member State experiencing exceptional circumstances caused by the exceptional occurrences caused by CoVID‐20 outbreak (OJ L 332, 31.3.2019, pp. 1-12)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) No 232/2011 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 37:
Recovery and resolution of failing financial institutions: rules on insolvency and resolution
Resolving insolvencies and resolution arrangements on financial institutions
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1095/2010 on the resolution and recovery of failing credit institutions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the resolution of failed credit institutions (CCPs) in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all credit institutions, including credit institutions such as banks, insurance companies, investment firms, pension funds and credit unions. It does not apply to credit institutions.
Resolution
The resolution of a failing credit institution (CCP) must be carried out by a resolution authority. The resolution authority must:
make a valuation of the assets or liabilities of the failing CCP;
prepare a business reorganisation plan;
identify any measures that may adversely affect the resolvability of the CCP.
It must submit a recovery plan to the resolution authority, which must be approved by the resolution college, which is composed of the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA), the European Central Bank (ECB) and the European Insurance and Financial Services Authority (EIFS).
The recovery plan must include:
a description of the causes of the failure;
the assets, rights, obligations or liabilities that the CCP has;
a recovery plan for the assets and liabilities;
details of the recovery plan.
A resolution authority’s powers and responsibilities;
procedures for the transfer of assets, obligations and liabilities to the original owners.
If the resolution authorities consider that the resolution plan does not meet the requirements of the resolution regulation, it must communicate this to the ECB and the EIFS for joint decision.
Cooperation between authorities
In the event of a failure of a credit institution, the competent authorities must cooperate with each other in order to ensure that:
the resolution authority:
identifies any measures which may adversely impact the resolution resolution;
ensures that any measures taken by the CCP may be effective in resolving the failure of the credit institution;
coordinates with the other authorities in the area of financial stability;
examines the recovery plans and identifies any measures to be taken in the event that these measures could seriously harm the resolution process.
The ECB and ESMA must cooperate between themselves in the case of failure of credit institutions and with other competent authorities.
When a CCP fails, the ECB is responsible for appointing a resolution committee. The committee:
is composed of representatives of the shareholders and the board of the CCP;
is chaired by the ECB;
has the power to:
approve or reject a resolution proposal;
make recommendations to the European Commission;
recommend or reject the resolution proposal.
Where the resolution committee recommends that a resolution procedure be adopted, the European Banking Authority adopts a resolution decision. The decision must be submitted to the EFSMA and the ECB for approval or consent.
Once the resolution procedure has been adopted and the resolution decision has been taken, the EAFS and EFSSA must communicate the resolution decisions to other authorities, including the European Financial Stability Board (EFSB).
If a resolution plan is rejected by the ESSB or the ECB, the resolution body must communicate its reasons for its rejection to the competent authority.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 14 February 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
  The resolution of financial institutions is a key element of the EU's financial stability. It is essential for the stability of the financial system and the protection of financial markets.
For more information, see:
Financial institutions and resolution (European Commission).

Summary 38:
European Year for Railways 2021 - promoting the European Union rail network and reducing emissions of greenhouse gases (European Green Deal) (European Year 2021 - European Year 2021)
European Year FOR Railways
The European Green Deal is a new growth strategy that aims to transform the Union into a fair and prosperous society, with a modern, resource-efficient and competitive economy where there are no net emissions of carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions in 2050 and where economic growth is decoupled from resource use. To achieve climate neutrality, a 90 % reduction in transport emissions is needed by 2050. The European Year for Railway Transport 2021 is the first full year where the rules agreed under the Fourth Railway Package will be implemented throughout the EU.
ACT
Decision No 552/2014/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 December 2014 on the European Year of Railways for 2021.
SUMMARY
This Decision establishes a European year for rail transport. The year 2021 will be important for the Union rail policy, since it will represent the 1st full year of the implementation of the fourth railway package. It will also be the first year where rules will be adopted on the opening of the market of domestic passenger services, on reducing costs and administrative burden for railway undertakings operating across the EU and on reducing technical barriers. The measures to be taken to achieve the objectives set out in Article 2 shall be closely coordinated with on-going activities promoting rail transport, including:
a study on the feasibility of creating a European label to promote goods and products transported by rail so as to encourage businesses to switch their transport to rail;
a feasibility study with a rail transport to compete with other modes of transport;
an evaluation of the level of integration achieved through the use of services on the rail network;
the creation of a European Rail Traffic Management System (ERTMS);
the realisation of an extended, modernised and interoperable railway infrastructure, including a European rail traffic management system, terminals offering modal shift options, as well as modernised rolling stock.
In addition, the Commission shall consider initiating, during the European year, studies on:
the feasibility of establishing a European Label to promote good and services by rail,
the introduction of a rail connectivity index,
an improvement of the efficiency of the rail freight corridors.
The Commission shall inform the Council at European level of the measures taken to promote rail transport during the year, including the participation of persons’ organisations and representatives of international organisations in the activities of the Year.

Summary 39:
Exchange of young officers in the field of cyber security and defence: European External Action Service (ESDC) (European External Action service) (OJ L 201, 3.7.2018)
Exchange in young officers: European external action service (ESSD)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2016/2382/CFSP establishing the organisation and functioning of the European External Agency for the Exchange of Young Officers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes the organisation of the ESDC, a European Union (EU) body that provides training and education for young officers (e.g. students) in the fields of cybersecurity and defence.
KEY POINTS
The ESDC’s tasks are to:
promote the European initiative for the exchange of young Officers;
to promote PhD-level research in domains related to CSDP;
provide EU countries’ and EU administrations with knowledgeable personnel familiar with EU policies, institutions, procedures and best practices in the area of cyber-security and defence;
help promote professional relations and contacts among training participants.
Organisation
The decision sets out the structure of the organisation, including:
a steering committee composed of senior representatives from the civilian and military institutes and other actors identified by EU countries to support the conduct of ESDC activities and of the Director of EUISS or the Director's representative;
a network of national experts seconded to the European Union institutions, the EEAS and EU agencies;
an advisory board composed of representatives of the civil and military organisations;
the head of the Steering Committee.
a secretariat.
The steering committee meets at least 4 times a year. It is chaired by the Director-General of the EUISS and composed of the director-general of the EEA, the director of the Directorate-General for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy and representatives of EU agencies.
A steering committee is composed of:
members of the organising and conducting institutes;
representatives of the associate network partners;
exchange of views and views of the participants;
participants from the EU institutions, EU agencies and bodies.
Management
The head is appointed by the steering committee. The head is responsible for:
the organisation and running the organisation;
training and education activities;
organising and conducting the network;
coordinating the activities of the board of directors;
contracted staff;
assisting the head in his or her tasks.
Staff
The staff comprise:
staff seconded by EU institutions or EEAS or EU agencies,
staff trained by national experts;
national experts trained by the EEAs or EU countries;
staff contracted by the head.
Membership
The board is composed:
of senior representatives of national institutes or bodies identified by Member States to support ESDC;
of national experts trained and trained by EU agencies or bodies;
members from the European Institute for Security and Defence Policy (EIDP) and the European Defence Agency (EDA).
The director-General chairs the board and is assisted by a member of the Secretariat. The board may delegate this task to one of the Chairpersons of the Board configurations or to a member who is an active observer. The Board draws up the rules and arrangements governing the creation, functioning of these configurations to be agreed by a Steering committee.
Financing
The budget for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2021 is EUR 2 055 156. The financial reference amount intended to cover the expenditure of the organisational activities during this period is € 2.5 million. This includes:
Contributions in kind to training programmes;
recipients’ contributions in kind in kind;
voluntary contributions;
revenue from the sale, rental or transfer of equipment, materials, software, software and services.

Summary 40:
Recovered water: environmental and health standards
Recovered waters: environmental standards
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2004 on water reuse
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the reuse of urban waste water by reclamation facilities.
It aims to ensure that waste water is reused whenever appropriate.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the responsibilities of the reclamation facility operator and obligations regarding water quality, traceability of reclaimed water and transparency.
Reclamation facility operators must:
ensure that the water is in compliance with the minimum water quality requirements set out in Annex III to Directive 91/271/EEC;
perform routine monitoring to verify the water’s compliance with these requirements;
keep records of the results of these monitoring;
use the water for agricultural purposes.
The European Commission, the European Environment Agency and the European Centre for Disease Prevention and Control have access to the data sets.
EU countries must ensure that the Commission publishes, publishes and updates, on a regular basis or following a request from the Commission, a Union-wide overview of the EU-wide re-reused water situation.
Permits are issued by the operator of a re-water facility. Operators must have a permit, which must be valid for a maximum period of 5 years from the date on which it is issued. The permit must include information on:
the risks to the environment and to human and animal health so that any risks are of an acceptable level;
the validity of the permit;
a description of the water treatment options;
how the water has been treated and the conditions for its use.
In addition, the permit must contain a description of how the water was treated and how it has been used for agricultural irrigation.
If the operator does not comply with the requirements, they may be subject to a fine or to a ban on using the water. The fine or ban must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
Where the operator fails to comply with these rules, the authority responsible for issuing the permit may decide to issue a ban or ban on the use of that water.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
The legislation applies from 25 June 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 25 June 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle waste water pollution. These include:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 on the re-use of municipal waste water (see summary);
Directive 2014/34/EU on the water reuse of agricultural products and repealing Regulation (EC, No 1774/2002 on animal by-products and derived products not intended for human consumption.
For more information, see:
Reused water (European Commission).

Summary 41:
EU blue card for third-country nationals — mobility within the European Union
EU blue-card for third countries
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/67/EU on the EU blue card
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to facilitate the mobility of non-EU nationals who wish to work legally in the EU and to take up residence in different European Union (EU) Member States.
It sets out the rules for the issuing of the blue card.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to non-European Union citizens who are not citizens of the Member States of the European Economic Area (EEA). It does not apply to:
non-EU citizens who have been granted international protection;
third country nationals who have obtained a higher education qualification no more than 3 years before submitting the application for an EU Blue Card; or
third-country national nationals who do not meet the criteria set out in Directive 2003/109/EC on the right of free movement of persons.
The blue card is valid for a period of 5 years. It can be renewed up to 3 years after the end of the 5-year period of validity.
Rights and conditions of mobility
The card allows the holder of the card to work in different Member States regardless of whether the person concerned is an EU citizen of reference or not. It also allows for short-term mobility between Member States and the right to stay for up to 2 years.
Member States must ensure that:
the holder of a blue card has the same rights as a Member State’s’ citizens, irrespective of whether they are citizens or not;
they have the same right to live and work in the same Member State;
there is no discrimination on the basis of sex, race, colour, ethnic or social origin, genetic features, language, religion or belief, political or any other opinion, membership of a national minority, property, birth, disability, age or sexual orientation, as required by Council Directives 2000/43/EC and 2000/78/EC (7) in particular.
In implementing this directive, Member States are not to discriminate on the grounds of sex*, race*, colour*, sex*, gender*, genetic features*, language, belief, nationality*, membership of any national minority*, property*, birth*, disability*, age and sexual orientation.
Sanctions against employers
In the case of employers who fail to comply with the directive, they must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive. They may also be subject to sanctions based on the gravity and nature of the misconduct.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2015. It had to become law in the European Member States by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU blue cards are a key element of a comprehensive labour migration policy and for better integration of migrants.
For more information, see:
‘Blue card’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Third country: a non-member of the EU who is not a citizen of an EU Member State.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2014/66/EU of 21 December 2014 on the granting of the Union blue card to third countries and repealing Council Directive 2001/43 (EC) No 1030/2002

Summary 42:
European Asylum and Migration Fund
European asylum and migration fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1060 establishing a European Asylum and Migrant Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It establishes the European Asylum & Migration Fund (EAMF).
It sets up the EAMF with the aim of providing financial support to EU countries’ actions in the field of migration and asylum.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The fund’s main objectives are to:
ensure that EU countries:
receive, resettle and accept refugees and stateless persons as beneficiaries of international protection;
provide emergency assistance to vulnerable persons;
promote the integration of asylum seekers into the labour market;
encourage the exchange of information between EU countries;
facilitate the exchange and sharing of information on the implementation of EU policies on migration and migration.
It is funded by the EU budget and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
Funding
EU countries must:
contribute to the overall budget of the European Union (EU);
make use of the thematic facility;
use the European Social Fund Plus (ESF+) to implement projects.
The European Commission decides the co-financing rate and the maximum amount of support from the Fund for the types of action covered by the contribution referred to in paragraphs 1 to 6 of the regulation.
Cofinancing
The Commission decides on the overall amount to be made available under the annual appropriations of the Union budget.
under the European budget, the amount of financial support for each EU country must be:
5 %;
5% for resettlement and humanitarian admission actions;
10 % for international protection actions
10% for humanitarian assistance actions
20% for integration measures
15% for reception actions.
Actions must have clear EU added value. They must be implemented in accordance with the rules of the programming period under which they receive funding.
Each individual phase of the project should be implemented according to the rules laid down in the regulation and the rules set out in the implementing programme.
Projects may be implemented under shared, direct or indirect management by EU countries or by the European social fund.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EAMFs are part of a package of measures designed to improve the quality of life of migrants and asylum seekers in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Migration and asylum’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Refugee: a person who has been effectively resettled or admitted and who is entitled to international protection under international law.
Humanitarian assistance: measures to help vulnerable persons.
International protection: measures aimed at helping people who are in danger of being returned to their country of origin or who are at risk of being displaced.

Summary 43:
Family leave: flexible working arrangements for parents and workers with childcare responsibilities (Directive 2010/18/EU)
Family leave - flexible working conditions for parents, carers and employers
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/1821 on flexible working and parental leave
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the rights of parents and carers who are parents, or carers.
It repeals and repeals Directive 2006/54/EC.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that workers who exercise their right to take leave or to request flexible work arrangements are protected against discrimination or any less favourable treatment on the ground that they have applied for, or have taken, leave provided for in Articles 4, 5 and 6 or time off from work.
The directive applies to:
maternity leave;
paternity leave.
Flexible working arrangements.
Employers must:
ensure that workers have the right to return to the original working pattern before the end of the agreed period where justified on the basis of a change of circumstances;
provide for urgent family reasons in the case of illness or accident making the immediate attendance of the worker indispensable.
Allow the worker to request a flexible working arrangement if they are:
injured or ill;
unwell;
visibly ill or injured;
in need of urgent medical attention.
Workers must be able to specify the period of notice to be given by the employer when applying for parental leave and should be able decide whether the parental leave is subject to a certain period of service. Employers must provide reasons for any refusal of such a request or for any postponement of such arrangements. The employer must also provide the worker with the opportunity to request the return of the working pattern if justified by a change in circumstances.
Member States must take the necessary measures to prohibit discrimination on the grounds that the worker has applied for or has taken leave or has exercised the rights covered by this directive.
In implementing this directive, Member States are not required to rename or otherwise change the different types of family-related leave that are provided for under national law or collective agreements and which count towards compliance with this Directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 20 August 2018.

Summary 44:
Criminal offences and sanctions in the area of fraud affecting the EU’s financial interests (Directive 2015/23/EU)
Criminal acts and sanctions relating to fraud affecting EU‘s financial interest (DirectIVE 2015/24/EU).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It establishes minimum rules relating to the definition of criminal offences* and sanctions* in the field of fraud* affecting the European Union (EU) financial interests.
KEY POINTS
Misappropriation, when committed intentionally, constitutes a criminal offence. The intentional nature of an act or omission may be inferred from objective, factual circumstances.
The Court of Auditors and the auditors responsible for auditing the budgets of the EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies should disclose to the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and to other competent authorities any fact which could be qualified as a crime under this directive, and Member States should ensure that national audit bodies within the meaning of Article 59 of Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 966/2012 do the same, in accordance with Article 8 of the regulation.
Limitation periods for criminal offences affecting the Union's financial interests
The limitation period should be at least 5 years from the time when the criminal offence was committed. This period may include extensions of the limitation period arising from interruption or suspension.
Sanctions
Member States should provide for certain types and levels of sanctions when criminal offences defined in this directive are committed. In cases of a maximum sanction of at least four years of imprisonment, the maximum sanction should be:
€10 000 or an advantage of less than €10 000;
€20 000 or more.
Penalties should not go beyond of the financial interests throughout the Union by means of measures which should act as a deterrent.
In addition, Member States may provide for sanctions other than criminal sanctions.
WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 6 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The Convention drawn up on the basis of Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union, on the protection of the European Communities’ financial interests of 26 July 1995, including the Protocols thereto of 27 September 1996, of 29 November 1996 and of 19 June 1997 (the ‘Convention’).
For more information, see:
‘Fraud’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Misappropriation: the intentional misappropriation of funds by an individual or group of persons for purposes other than the proper management of the funds.

Summary 45:
Interchange fees for consumer debit card transactions: rules for issuers, acquirers and consumers
Interchange fee fees for consumers debit card payments: rules on issuers and acquirers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on interchange fees for card-based payment transactions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out rules on the interchange fees* charged by payment card issuers* and by payment service providers* for the processing and storage of payment card transactions.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
debit card transactions carried out with universal cards.
Payment cards are the most frequently used electronic payment instrument for retail purchases in the European Union (EU). They are also the most widely accepted payment instrument in the world for online transactions. However, they have been subject to excessive interchange fees. This has a negative impact on merchants and consumers and prevents innovation.
In order to ensure that interchange fees are set at an economically efficient level, taking into account the structure of domestic debit card markets, the possibility to express interchange fee caps as a flat rate should be maintained.
To acknowledge the existence of implicit interchange fees and contribute to the creation of a level playing field, three party payment card schemes using payment card providers as issuers or acquirers should follow the same rules as four party schemes and should comply with the regulation, whilst transparency and other measures related to business rules should apply to all providers.
Co-badging and choice of payment brand or payment application
The European Banking Authority (EBA) is responsible for issuing payment cards must ensure that:
payment cards are issued by the same issuer or acquirer as those issued by national banks;
payment services are provided by a single issuer or a single acquirer, irrespective of the issuer’s national or cross-border status;
the payment service provider must inform the payee of the amount of the merchant service charges, interchange fees, scheme fees and payment card fees applicable to each category and brand of payment cards, unless the paymentee subsequently makes a different request in writing.
The EBA must submit those draft regulatory technical standards to the European Commission by 9 December 2015. The Commission must adopt those standards and submit them to the EBA within 9 months of the submission of the draft technical standards.
EU countries must designate competent authorities that are empowered to ensure enforcement of this regulation and that are granted investigation and enforcement powers. The bodies must be independent from the parties.
Member States should lay down rules on penalties applicable to infringements of the regulation and should ensure that those penalties are effective, proportionate and dissuasive and that they are applied.
Amendments to Regulation (EC) No 651/2014
In addition, the regulation has been amended by:
Directive 2011/83/EU on e-commerce (see summary) and Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 852/2013 on the electronic payment instruments and services which can be off-line, on-line or mobile.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
It has applied since 16 September 2014. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 16 September 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been working towards an integrated market for electronic payments for more than a decade. This regulation aims to ensure the proper functioning and completion of this market.
For more information, see:
‘E-commerce’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Interchange fee: a fee charged by a payment card issuer or an acquirer to facilitate the payment of a debit card to a consumer.
* Scheme fees: the fees charged by the payment card company to facilitate a payment transaction between two or more payment card companies.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (European Union) No 529/2014 of 15 September 2014 on the approximation of the laws and regulations of the Member States relating to interchange fees (OJ L 136, 16.9.2014, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation No 529 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
