Summary 97:
Border surveillance at sea: rules for border surveillance
Bilateral border surveillance: rights and principles for border guards and other staff
Border surveillance is to prevent unauthorised border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to apprehend or take other measures against those persons who have crossed the border in an irregular manner.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 656/2014 — rules on border surveillance in the area of the Schengen acquis
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the application of the EU’s border surveillance rules.
It aims to ensure that border surveillance is not limited to the detection of attempts at illegal border crossings but equally extends to steps such as intercepting vessels suspected of trying to gain entry to the EU without submitting to border checks, as well as arrangements to address situations such as search and rescue that may arise during a border surveillance operation at sea and arrangements intended to bring such an operation to a successful conclusion.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all measures taken by EU countries or the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Eurojust) to prevent, detect or apprehend persons attempting to cross the borders of the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to:
illegal border crossing attempts;
illegal crossing of the borders;
unauthorised crossing of borders in the contiguous zone;
the interception of vessels attempting to enter the territory of an EU Member State without complying with border checks.
Rights and principles
EU countries and Eurojust must:
ensure the protection of fundamental rights and the principle of non-refoulement and non-discrimination;
protect victims of trafficking in human beings;
assess and rescue persons who are in a phase of uncertainty;
use all means to identify the intercepted or rescued persons, assess their personal circumstances, inform them of their destination in a way that those persons understand or may reasonably be presumed to understand and give them an opportunity to express any reasons for believing that disembarkation in the proposed place would be in violation of the principle.
The operational plan must contain details adapted to the circumstances of the sea operation concerned. It must be updated as necessary.
Participation in border surveillance operations
Member States and the Eurojust Agency must ensure that:
the operational plan is drawn up in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation;
members of the participating units are trained and equipped;
border guards, other staff participating in a sea operation are provided with appropriate training;
operational plans are drawn up according to the operational plan;
participation in a maritime operation is coordinated through the International Coordination Centre (ICC);
the participating units must communicate with each other and with the International Maritime Organization (IMO).
The participating units may also use all means available to them to identify, assess and rescue the persons on board, including:
pregnant women or children on board;
victims of trafficking;
persons who have been reported missing or overdue;
people who have failed to make an expected position or safety report.
If a Member State suspects that a vessel is attempting to illegally cross its borders, it may approach the vessel and take measures to prevent it from crossing the border. If the flag State is unwilling or unable to do so either directly or with the assistance of the Member State to whom the participating unit belongs, that Member State may request authorisation from the EU country to take any of the measures laid down above.
Interception
Member countries and the Agency may:
intercept a vessel suspected of attempting to breach the border;
take measures against the vessel;
cooperate with the IMO and the ICJ in the event that the vessel is found to be in a state of uncertainty or in danger of being in danger;
interrogate the vessel or the persons in danger.
In addition, Member States must cooperate with other Member States in the field of border surveillance to identify persons who may be in need of assistance or who may have been involved in illegal border crossing.
Implementing Decision 2014/852/EU
The Member States may, without prejudice to Article 3 of Decision 2008/146/EC on the implementation, application and development of the agreement between the European Community and the Swiss Confederation on the Swiss association with the implementation of the implementation and application and the Scheunen accords (see summary), adopt measures to protect the rights of persons who fall within the area referred to in Article 1, point A, of Decision 1999/437/EC read in conjunction with Articles 3 and 4 of Council Decision 2009/849/EC.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 April 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
The European Commission adopted Implementing Decision 2010/252/EU establishing a European Union border surveillance system.
For more information, see:
‘Border surveillance’ on Eurojust�



Summary 98:
Financial rules for EU actions, emergency and technological assistance
Financial rules on EU actions and emergency and technical assistance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 966/2012 on the financial rules for Union actions, emergencies and technology assistance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for implementing the European Union’s (EU) measures, measures and technologies (EU actions, EMERGENCY and Technical assistance) for the period from 1 January 2014 to 31 December 2020.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the Member States’ actions, measures, technologies and measures for the prevention and control of migration and asylum applications;
the management of the external borders of the Union, including actions implemented in non-EU countries;
measures for the protection of human rights and international and human rights law, including measures implemented in third countries.
The Regulation and the Specific Regulations apply to the following:
EU actions and measures to prevent and control migration, asylum, integration and internal security (Migration, Integration and Internal Security Funds);
measures to protect human rights, international law and international humanitarian law;
implementing measures for protection of the environment and security in the context of the migration crisis.
Implementation
The European Commission and the Member States are responsible for the implementation of the regulation and the specific regulations.
Beneficiaries
The Commission and Member States must:
ensure that beneficiaries are fully cooperate with the Commission;
carry out the tasks set out in the regulation;
allocate the necessary resources to implement the programme objectives;
make the necessary financial contributions to achieve the objectives of the programme.
make all the necessary payments to cover the costs of implementing the programme;
provide the Commission with information, communications, communication, monitoring, evaluation, verification, evaluation and reporting.
Each Member State must submit an annual report to the Commission on the progress made in achieving its programme objectives. This report must be published in the Official Journal of the EU.
Member States must ensure that the Commission has access to all the information it needs to carry out its tasks, including information on beneficiaries, the beneficiaries’ rights and obligations.
Monitoring and audits by the Commission
Each year, the Commission carries out an annual audit of each national programme. This audit is carried out in accordance with the rules laid down in the Regulation. The Commission is assisted by the Asylum, Integration & Internal Security Fund Committee, which is composed of representatives of the Commission, Member States and the European Border and Coast Guard. It is responsible for:
monitoring and assessing the implementation and effectiveness of the national programmes;
assisting the Commission in monitoring and evaluating the effectiveness of its tasks;
checking that national programmes comply with the principles of sound financial management, in particular value for money and cost-effectiveness.
Information, communication and publicity
Each national programme must have its own information and publicity system. This system must include:
information, communications and publicity;
information and publicity about the programme and its activities;
publication of the programmes and their activities; and
information about the beneficiaries.
In addition, each programme must be accompanied by:
a statement of its objectives and objectives,
a description of the measures taken to achieve them;
a list of beneficiaries and their rights and responsibilities;
an annual report on the programmes’ progress made.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
It has applied since 30 June 2012 and had to become law in the Member State concerned by 30 June 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU is facing a major migration crisis and must adopt an integrated approach to questions arising from the pressure of asylum applications and regarding the management of its external borders.
For more information, see:
‘Migration and asylum’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Migration: the movement of people, goods, services and capital through the internal borders of an EU country.

Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, of 30 May 2012 on the Financial Rules for Union Actions, EMERGY AND TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE (OJ L 127, 30.5.2012, pp. 1-8)

Summary 99:
European Return Fund
European Refugee Fund
SUMMigration and migration — European Return Fund (ERF)
The European Refugee Fund is a European Union (EU) instrument designed to support the return of people who have been refused international protection and the resettlement of refugees in the region of Eastern Africa/Great Lakes.
ACT
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 514/2014 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 23 May 2014 establishing the European Refugee and Migration Fund for the period 2008 to 2013 as part of the General Programme ‘Solidarity and Management of Migration Flows’ (OJ L 144, 6.6.2014, pp. 45-52)
SINCE WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
   
 
It applies from 1 January 2011 and 31 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s migration policy has been made increasingly flexible and coherent, in terms of both scope and applicability, to support policy developments in the field of asylum and migration.
For more information, see:
European Migration Fund (European Commission).
KEY POINTS
Objectives of the ERF
The ERF is designed to:
contribute to the establishment of a common European asylum system;
receive and asylum systems in EU countries;
ensure the effective implementation of the Common European Asylum System (CES);
support the transfer of applicants and beneficiaries of international protection transferred from one EU country to another with the support of the Fund;
the establishment of common EU resettlement priorities;
provide financial support to the European Migration Network (EMN);
National Contact Points (NCPs);
contain representatives of EU countries’ national authorities.
The EMN is a network of national authorities responsible for the management of the CES and the coordination of the activities of the EMNPCs.
In addition, the ERF supports actions that take place in the framework of consistent strategies, taking into account the integration needs of third-country nationals at local and/or regional level.
Eligible actions
The ERF may finance transnational actions or actions of fair and transparent distribution of resources among the specific objectives set out in Article 3(2) of the Regulation. Actions must be carried out in accordance with Article 6 of Regulation (EU), in line with the principles and mechanisms set out by the Commission. Actions may not be transferred to other actions under the national programme.
Resources for eligible actions in the Member States
Resources are divided between the basic amount and an amount calculated on the basis of the average of 2011, 2012 and 2013 allocations for each in mind of the context in which they are used. The basic amount is composed of a minimum amount and a maximum amount. The maximum amount is based on the average number of applicants, beneficiaries and international protection transfers from one Member State to another and the number of cooperation projects with other Member States, in particular with regard to those most affected by migration and in particular those most in need of assistance.
National authorities must ensure that actions are carried out with the consent of the Member State which granted them international protection or is responsible for examining their application to another interested Member State where they will be granted international protection.
EU actions must be implemented according to the rules laid down in the regulation and must comply with the procedures laid down by the European Commission. The European Commission may, at its initiative, adopt measures to combat illegal immigration or the circumventing of existing legal migration rules, thereby safeguarding the integrity of Member States’ immigration systems. The Commission may also take measures to prevent illegal immigration and to combat the illegal immigration of non-EU nationals.
Implementation of the EFR
In order to achieve its objectives, the Fund supports the following actions:
the return of persons who have not been given international protection;
transnational actions and actions of transnational nature;
coordination of the actions of the national authorities;
reciprocal support for actions of regional and local authorities in order to ensure the proper functioning of the EU migration network;
participation of the Commission in the implementation of national actions;
cooperation with other EU bodies and programmes in the area of migration and migration policy;
support for the establishment, management and coordination of activities of national contact points;
assistance for the activities and activities of EMN;
financial support for the European migration network.
References
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition into force (Official Journal)

Official Journal L 144 of 6.7.2014

Summary 101:
Conservation and management of the stock of common interest: measures against countries allowing non-sustainable fishing (Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on measures against non-Sustainable fishing)
Convention on sustainable fishing
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2012/852/EU on measures to combat non-conservation of the common interest of fish stocks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the type of measures that may be taken with regard to countries allowing sustainable fishing and establishes general conditions for the adoption of such measures, so that they are based on objective criteria and are equitable, cost-effective and compatible with international law, in particular with the Agreement establishing the World Trade Organisation (WTO).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to non-EU countries that:
have not taken measures to conserve or manage the stock in question;
are not taking measures to prevent, deter and eliminate illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing;
allow high-yield fisheries on the stock under their control.
The measures must be:
related to the conservation of the species;
made effective in conjunction with restrictions on fishing by EU vessels, or on production or consumption within the EU, applicable to fish and fishery products made of or containing such fish of species for which the measures have been adopted;
proportionate to the objectives pursued;
compatible with the obligations imposed by international agreements to which the EU is a party and any other relevant norms of international law.
the measures must not lead to the stock being in an unsustainable state solely due to measures adopted by others.
National authorities must notify the European Commission of the reasons for the identification of a country as a non-compliant fishery management country. The Commission may adopt implementing acts determining whether the conditions laid down in the regulation have been complied with and, where necessary, providing that the measures adopted in respect of the country concerned cease to apply.
In addition, the Commission must adopt implementing measures determining whether, where appropriate, the measures taken by the country in question have been proportionate to objectives pursued and are compatible with the WTO obligations imposed on the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLYEAR INCE?
   
It entered into force on 31 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
Fisheries management in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Fishery management’ on the Commission’s website.
KEY TERMS
* Sustainable fishing: the management of a stock within which the stock can replenish itself with high probability while allowing high yield fisheries on it.
* Non-consistent fishing: fishing that is not sustainable.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2012/884/EU of 31 November 2012 on measures in response to the identification by the European Parliament and of the Council of non-European Union countries of a third country allowing sustainable sustainable fishing (OJ L 112, 31.12.2012, pp. 1-8)

Summary 102:
Bilateral investment agreements between EU countries and non-EU countries (Regulation (EU) No 1201/2013)
Bilateral Investment agreements between European Union countries and third countries (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 602/2013 on bilateral investment agreements with third countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the conditions under which EU Member States are empowered to amend or conclude bilateral agreements with countries outside the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to agreements signed between the Member States between 1 December 2009 and 9 January 2013.
The Member States must notify the Commission of the entry into force of the bilateral investment agreement and of any subsequent changes to the status of such an agreement. The notification must include a copy of the agreement.
Notification to the Commission
The Commission may request further information from the Member State concerned. Where the information transmitted by the EU country is not sufficient for the purposes of authorising the opening of formal negotiations in accordance with Article 9 of the regulation, it may request additional information. The Member State and the Commission must fully cooperate and take all necessary measures to ensure an effective defence which may include, where appropriate, the participation in the procedure by the Commission;
participation of the Commission in negotiations;
the participation of the European Commission in the negotiations concerning investment between the EU and the third country.
Where the Commission grants an authorisation to conclude the agreement, it must keep the Commission informed of the progress and results of the negotiations throughout the different stages and may request the Commission’s participation in those negotiations.
Authorisations are granted by the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee for Investment Agreements. The Commission may refuse to grant authorisations if:
the agreement does not constitute a serious obstacle to the negotiation or conclusion by the Union of bilateral investments with non-European Union countries; or
the provisions of the agreements do not constitute serious obstacles to the conclusion of such agreements.
Amendments to the agreement
Member States must ensure that the amendments to the agreements notified pursuant to Article 2 of this regulation do not:
contain provisions that are contrary to Article 3 of the Treaty on European Union;
are not incompatible with the objectives of the common commercial policy;
do not constitute, or are likely to constitute, an obstacle to negotiations or conclusion of agreements with the EU.
If the Commission decides to grant a Commission authorisation, the agreement must be amended or to conclude. The agreement must include:
a description of the terms and conditions of the investment agreement;
an explanation of the reasons for the decision;
details of the conditions for the Commission's involvement in the agreement's conclusion;
a copy of any amendments or to conclusion.
In addition, the Commission may:
request additional information from a Member State;
request the Commission to examine the legal basis for its decision; and
request an explanation for any failure of Member States to fulfil their obligations under EU law.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 9 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has exclusive competence in the field of economic and monetary policy with respect to the common economic policy of non-euro area countries.
For more information, see:
Investment agreements (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Bilateral investment agreement: an agreement between an EU country and a third country concerning investment.
* Common commercial policy: the policy of the EU with regard to economic, monetary and fiscal policy.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Resolution of 9 December 2009 on the conclusion, on behalf of the Council, of the Agreement on the signing and provisional application by Member States of bilateral agreements between Member States concerning investment (OJ L 183, 9.12.2009, pp. 1-8)

Summary 103:
Beneficiaries of the European Union’s common commercial policy (GSP) — rules on preferential trade in goods between the EU and non-EU countries and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) countries
Benefiars of the common commercial strategy (GPS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2013 on the European Community’S common commercial and trade policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the rules on the EU's common commercial policies (‘GSP’).
The GSP is a system of preferential trade measures designed to encourage the free movement of goods between European Union (EU) countries.
The European Commission is responsible for administering the GSP.
It applies to goods originating in the EU from:
the EU countries;
the European Economic Area (EEA);
the countries of the Western Balkans and the former Yugoslav Republic of Macedonia;
Burma/Myanmar.
KEY POINTS
EU countries eligible for GSP
A list of eligible countries is established in Annex I. The list includes countries for which GSP preferences may have been temporarily withdrawn or suspended, or for which the tariff preferences may be temporarily withdrawn, suspended or temporarily withdrawn.
A GSP beneficiary country may be excluded from the list of GSP beneficiaries if it:
has not met the conditions set out in Article 21 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU;
is a candidate for membership of the World Trade Organization (WTO);
is not a member of the EEA;
does not meet the conditions laid down in Article 22(1) of the TFEU;
has a high level of economic or social development; or
is subject to serious economic, social or environmental problems.
Annex II sets out general safeguards for the protection of intellectual property rights of EU citizens and businesses;
may be amended by delegated acts adopted by the Commission in the light of evidence received from the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2014.

Summary 104:
EU rules on the export of firearms to and through non-EU countries
Umbrella Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on the exporting of firearms
EU rules governing the exporting and importation of firearms and their parts and components
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EEC) No 529/2014 — export controls and controls and measures relating to firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the export and import of firearms from the customs territory of the EU to or through third countries.
It aims to prevent, combat and eradicate the illicit manufacture, import and trafficking of firearms.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
the competent authorities of EU countries have effective control over the manufacturing, marking, import, export and transit of firearms;
the export authorisation and import authorisation are issued in accordance with the United Nations Firearms Protocol (UN Firearms Protocol);
the European Firearms Pass (EFP) is valid throughout the EU.
Exports
The regulation applies to:
exports of firearms for civilian use within the EU;
transshipments of firearms between EU countries;
imports from EU countries of firearms, their parts or components and ammunition.
Importers must:
provide the exporter with:
an export licence or import authorising document;
a description of the item to be sent out of the country in which the item is to be shipped;
information on the item’s condition of import and transit;
an explanation of the items to be provided in advance to the third countries of transit.
The exporter must also provide the importing and transit countries with information on the items intended for export.
the importing country must notify the exporting country in writing that they have no objection to the transit of an item out of its territory.
Export authorisation
The exporting country must issue a weaponisation, a registration or a registration of a component of a weapon or ammunition, as well as an authorisation for the sending out of their territory of a firearm.
Before the competent authorities in an EU country grant an export authorising, they must notify, annul, suspend, modify, revoke or refuse to grant it, and share the relevant information with the authorities of the other EU countries. They must also inform the European Commission of any objections it or they may have to the granting of such authorisation, which shall bind the EU country in whose territory the application has been made. The Commission may adopt delegated acts to adopt delegated decisions in the light of these objections, subject to the conditions laid down in this regulation.
In order to ensure that this regulation is properly applied, EU countries should take measures giving the competent authority appropriate powers. These powers should be effective, proportionate and dissuasive. They should also be ensured by means of diplomatic channels.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It has applied since 3 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
The UN Firearms Protocol, the purpose of which is to promote, facilitate and strengthen cooperation among parties in order to prevent and combat the illicit manufacturing, importing, exporting and trafficking in firearms, ammunition and spare parts, parts and component parts and ammunition, entered into force on 3 July 2005. It requires parties to put in place or improve administrative procedures or systems to exercise effective control of the manufacture, marking and import and export of weapons.
For more information, see:
‘The EU Firearms Protocol’ on the European External Action Service's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/852/EU of 3 June 2014 laying down the rules on export controls, procedures and controls and measures related to firearms exported within the European Union (OJ L 157, 4.6.2014, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 105:
European situational picture and common pre-frontier intelligence picture (EUROSUR)
Europol: European situational picture
European situational image and common intelligence picture
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 establishing the European situational image
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It establishes a European situational awareness and reaction capability at the external borders of the Member States of the European Union (‘external borders’) for the purpose of detecting, preventing and combating illegal immigration and cross-border crime and contributing to ensuring the protection and saving the lives of migrants.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets up a communication network between the national coordination centres of EU countries and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EurosUR).
The communication network consists of:
a common situational picture (e.g. a map of the EU’s external borders);
a European border surveillance and border control picture (a map of external border crossing points);
common pre-border intelligence picture, a picture of all the information that is relevant for air border surveillance, checks and checks at external border crossings points.
The European border control coordination centre (EBCC) is responsible for:
coordinating the national situational picture;
sharing information between national border control centres and the EBCC;
monitoring and analysing the situational picture.
National coordination centres must regularly inform the agency of the measures taken at national level pursuant to point (calls for information;
regularly exchanging information on national situational pictures;
checking the security and border controls;
coordination systems;
exchanging information on the status of national border controls.
EURoSafiliations between national coordination centre and the agency.
EU countries’ competent national authorities must:
ensure that the communication network is open to the public;
share information on their situational picture with the agency;
cooperate with neighbouring countries, on the basis of bilateral or multilateral agreements and regional networks as referred to in Article 20 of the Convention against the Smuggling of Migrants by Land, Sea and Air, the Convention Relating to the Status of Refugees, the International Convention for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms and other relevant international instruments.
In addition, the European Commission must ensure that:
the communication network has a high impact level where there is a moderate impact on border security;
the incidents related to illegal immigration occur at the relevant border section have a moderate to high impact;
there are high impact levels where there are high numbers of incidents involving illegal immigration.
Agency responsibilities
The agency is responsible:
for:
identifying, analysing, monitoring and assessing the situation at the EU external borders;
collecting and disseminating information on illegal immigration;
assisting the competent authorities in the prevention, detection and control of illegal immigration, and combating the smuggling of migrants by land, sea and air;
ensuring the protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms of migrants;
providing information and assistance to border control authorities and national authorities of non-EU countries in the Mediterranean region.
It must also:
assist in the coordination of border control and border management at the border;
act as a contact point for the competent border control authority in the Schengen area;
facilitate the exchange and exchange of information between the competent national border management authorities and the Agency.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat illegal immigration in recent years. These include:
Directive 2008/46/EC on border control (see summary);
Council Decision 2002/192/EC establishing a European border management system for the internal borders (See summary).
For more information, see:
External borders (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* External borders: external borders: the area of the internal border of an EU Member State where a Member State has a border with another Member State.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 107:
Protection of designations of origin and geographical indications (designated names, symbols and indications) in the European Union — protection of traditional specialities and traditional products
Protected names, symbol and indications in the field of agriculture
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on the protection of designated names and symbols of origin
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) system of protection of protected names*, symbols* and designations* of origin*.
It aims to ensure that the names and indications on goods and services are protected against the misuse, imitation and evocation of the registered names on goods as well as on services in order to ensure a high level of protection and to align that protection with that which applies to the wine sector.
KEY POINTS
Protection
The regulation applies to:
traditional specialities* and designated names* on agricultural products;
designated geographical indications* on wine;
traditional products* which are produced in a non-EU country.
It does not apply to products that are produced outside the EU.
The European Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts, in accordance with Article 56 of the regulation, establishing the Union symbols. These may be optional on the labelling of traditional products.
Designated names
The Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the use of the name in trade and, if necessary, informing the competent authorities;
checking that the name is used fairly and in order not to mislead consumers;
ensuring the authenticity of the product’s traditional character.
registering a protected name
checking the name, symbol or designation of origin.
assessing whether the name or designation complies with the conditions of use.
Applying for protection
The EU countries must apply to the Commission for the registration of names or designations. The Commission must:
assess the quality of the names or designation;
check that the designations or indications comply with the requirements of the protection scheme;
identify products that meet the conditions for protection.
Application for registration
An optional quality term may only be used to describe products that comply with certain conditions. The term ‘mountain product*’, for example, must meet the following an examination procedure and must be accompanied by a description of the method by which the product is prepared, the key elements establishing the product's traditional character and the characteristics of the traditional product.
Registration of names
A protected name or designation of origin must be registered in the territory of an EU country. It is the sole responsibility of the EU country concerned.
A European Union symbol (EU symbol) must also be registered. The symbol must be optional, except where it is used on products produced in countries other than those covered by the regulation.
EU countries may encourage the formation and functioning of groups on their territories by administrative means. These groups are responsible for ensuring that the appropriate information is communicated to the consumer.
In addition, the Commission must ensure that:
the European Union symbols are used fairly;
the symbols are accompanied by the information contained in the specification referred to in the regulation;
they comply with all the conditions laid down in Article 56.
If the Commission considers that an EU symbol does not comply with these conditions, it must inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the reasons for its omission or omission;
EU symbols must be replaced by a new EU symbol.
Examination procedure
An examination procedure must be carried out by a body designated by the Commission. The body must examine all applications for registration. This scrutiny must not exceed a period of 6 months. If no agreement is reached, this information must be provided to the European Commission and the European Agricultural and Rural Development Agency (EARDA).
If an application for registration is rejected, the body must submit a report on the application and the results of the examination to the EARDA. The report must be sent to the Council and the Commission within 6 months of receiving the report.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts must be adopted by 4 January 2023. The regulation repeals Regulation (EC, No 509/2006 and repeals Directive 2008/95/EC.
References

Entry into force - Date of expiry

Summary 108:
Single-hull oil tankers: accelerated phasing-in scheme for the application of MARPOL 73/78 design requirements to single- and double-decked oil tanker standards
Single-Hull oil tanker rules: accelerated phase-in mechanism
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 2099/2002 establishing a scheme for applying the requirements of the International Maritime Organisation’s Regulations on maritime safety and the prevention of pollution from ships
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a scheme to apply the rules of the international maritime organisation (IMO) on the design and construction of double hulls and equivalent design requirements for single and double decked oiltankers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation repeals Regulation (EC, No 2098/2002, which had to become law in the European Union (EU) countries by 31 December 2002.
It repeals and replaces Regulation (EEC, Euratom) No 596/2004.
The purpose of this regulation is to establish an accelerated phased-in system for the implementation of the requirements applicable to the design of the double hull and the equivalent design of single-and-double deck oil tanker specifications.
This regulation does not apply to:
single-deck oil tanker (e.g. a tanker carrying heavy grade oil);
double-deck* oil tanker;
cargo tankers;
tankers of Category 2 or Category 3 which are equipped only with double bottoms or double sides not used for the transport of oil and extending for the whole length of the cargo tank, or with double sides.
*;
heavy-duty oil tanker which is equipped with a double bottom or a double side.
EU countries must notify the IMO if it allows, suspends, withdraws or declines the fact that they continue to operate under the flag of a third country in accordance with Regulation 20.5 of Annex I to MARPOL 1973/78.
Exemptions from the regulation
Member States have the right to grant exemptions from certain provisions of that regulation. However, they must ensure that these exemptions do not exceed the limits set out in the regulation.
In addition, Member States must prohibit the transport to or from ports of the Member States of heavy-duty-carrying oil in single-deck tanker.
A delegated act adopted pursuant to Article 10(1) as soon as it adopts a delegated act, the Commission must notify it simultaneously to the European Parliament and to the Council. The Commission has the power to revoke the delegated act if it considers that the regulation is not being applied correctly.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 31 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a communication on a common policy on safe seas.
For more information, see:
European Commission communication on the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA).
KEY TERMS
Double hull: a vessel designed to carry more than 10,000 gross tonnes of oil.
Equal design: a ship designed and built to carry a greater number of crew than the number of passengers.

Summary 109:
European Financial Supervisory Authority (EFSA) (European Financial Stability Mechanism)
European Financial System Mechanism (European financial stability mechanism)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 223/2009 on the European Financial Supervision Authority (ESSA)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European financial supervisory authority (ESAs) to assist national supervisory authorities in the consistent interpretation and application of EU rules and to contribute to financial stability necessary for financial integration in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that the EU’s financial stability is maintained and that the European financial system is sound.
KEY POINTS
The ESAs are accountable to the European Parliament and the Council of the EU. The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation.
The purpose and tasks of the Authority are to:
assist competent national financial authorities in applying and applying EU rules;
ensure that financial institutions comply with the rules of the Union;
provide advice and guidance to national authorities on the application and interpretation of EU financial rules; and
assess and monitor the financial stability of the financial markets and institutions in the EU;
contribute to the prevention and resolution of systemic risks.
In addition, the ESAs:
have a permanent capacity to respond to systemic risks;
have the capacity to assess and assess the risk posed by cross-border financial institutions and groups of financial institutions;
make recommendations to the national authorities;
coordinate and coordinate their activities with regard to the prudential requirements of the other EU financial authorities.
Establish an independent supervisory bodies and bodies.
have an independent board of supervisors and a management board.
are chaired by an independent director, who is appointed by the European Commission and has a term of office of 5 years and may be replaced by an alternate if he is prevented from attending a meeting of the Board of Supervisors;
are independent from national authorities and have the right to appeal to the Court of Justice if they consider that a national authority has not complied with its responsibilities;
take decisions in cases where the national authority in question has failed to comply with its obligations;
meet at least 5 times a year and at least twice a year with a minimum of 3 members of the supervisory board;
report to the Commission every 2 years on the implementation of the regulatory technical standards and implementing technical standards that it has prepared;
prepare guidelines and recommendations on the technical aspects of financial regulation;
cooperate with the European Banking Authority and the European Insurance and Reinsurance Stakeholder Group and the Occupational Pensions Steering Committee.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The European financial stability Mechanism is designed to ensure the soundness of the internal market for financial services and the stability of financial markets. It is based on a number of principles, including:
the need to ensure consistency and consistency with EU rules on financial supervision;
the right of the addressees of the decisions to be heard and to be fully respected.
For more information, see:
Financial stability in Europe (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
Financial institutions: any financial institution that is subject to EU financial supervision.
Regulatory technical standards: any regulatory technical standard that applies to financial institutions.
Implementing technical standard: any technical standard, guideline or recommendation that is adopted by the Commission.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU) No 225/2009 of 11 March 2009 on the establishment of a European Financial System Management Authority (EFSM) (OJ L 157, 12.4.2009, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC)* No 225 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 552/2014 of the European Central Bank of 11 December 2014 on the harmonisation of the rules on the supervision of European financial institutions, amending Council Regulations (EC and Euratom), (EC), (Euratom) and (EURO) No 2533/98 of 23 November 1998 concerning the collection of statistical information by the ECB and on the coordination of the statistical information of the ECB;
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/852/EU of 25 November 2014 concerning the harmonization of the technical standards for the European banking system and amending Commission Implementing Regulations (EU and Euratom).

Summary 110:
Equal treatment of workers and their families moving between EU countries
Equality and freedom of movement of workers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/68 on the equal treatment of nationals of a Member State and of workers employed in the territory of another Member State
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) no 848/66, which had to become law in the Member States by 8 November 1968.
It aims to ensure that workers who are employed in another Member States enjoy equal treatment as regards membership of trade unions and the exercise of rights attaching thereto, including the right to vote and to be eligible for the administration or management posts of a trade union.
The regulation does not apply to conditions of registration with employment offices or to recruitment of individual workers.
KEY POINTS
Eligibility for employment
A national of one Member State who wishes to pursue the same activity as a national of another member State must not be excluded from taking part in the management of bodies governed by public law and from holding an office governed by the public law of the Member State in which he or she is employed.
Employment and equality of treatment
A worker who is employed in an employer’s territory who is not employed in that Member State must enjoy the same social and tax advantages as national workers. This includes:
the right to participate in trade unions;
membership of the management or administration posts of the trade union;
participation in the elections of representatives of the employers’ associations;
equality of treatment with regard to working conditions and working conditions.
Recruitment
The engagement and recruitment of a national for a post in an EU country must not depend on medical, vocational or other criteria which are discriminatory on grounds of nationality by comparison with those applied to nationals of the other Member State.
EU countries must forward to the services of the first Member State the details of suitable applications. These details must be brought up to date regularly.
An Advisory Committee and a Technical Committee are set up by the Council. Each Member State appoints as member of the Advisory Committee one of the members who represent it on the Technical Committee. The advisory committee and the technical committee meet at least twice a year. They adopt all measures necessary for this purpose.
They communicate all developments on the Union labour market; such facts are communicated to the specialist services of Member States and to the advisory committee referred to in Article 21 and the Technical committee.
In addition, the advisory and technical committees cooperate with each other and with the departments of the Commission.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
It entered into force on 1 January 1969.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Equality of treatment’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Member State: any Member State of the European Union (EU) where there is a large population of workers, and where the labour market is open to workers from other Member States.

Summary 111:
Regulation (EC) No 2037/2010 on the placing on the market of substances for essential laboratory and analytical uses and the placing of biocidal products on the European Union market (Recast)
Recast
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It repeals and repeals Regulation (2000/2037/EC, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2010.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all substances, including hydrochlorofluorocarbons, which are used in essential laboratory or analytical uses.
It does not apply to substances used in industrial processes, such as those used in the manufacture of pharmaceuticals, pharmaceuticals and medical devices.
EU countries must ensure that the substances listed in the annexes to the regulation are listed in accordance with the Annexes to Directive 91/414/EEC and Directive 98/8/EC.
The European Commission may, at the request of the competent authority of an EU country, grant derogations from end dates for existing applications or cut-off dates for new applications, provided that no technically and economically feasible alternative is available.
Imports of controlled substances or of products and equipment containing or relying on controlled substances must comply with the regulation’s export control rules.
Exports of substances to the EU must be registered with the Commission, indicating the substances being used, the purpose, the estimated annual consumption and the suppliers of those substances, and any purchases from and sales to other undertakings in the Community.
In addition, the Commission must keep a register of the quantities imported under other customs procedures, separately identifying the customs procedure and the designated uses, and quantities of used substances imported for recycling or reclamation.
Export controls must be in place to ensure the safety and security of the substances and products imported.
Any undertaking using controlled substances other than those listed in Annex I must register with the European Commission the substances it uses, the reasons for using them, the quantities used and the quantities of stocks it holds.
If the Commission considers that the regulation does not comply with its annexes, it may adopt measures to amend them.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECAST APPLACE INTO FORCE?
 
   
It applies from 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to protect human health and the environment against the adverse effects of chemicals. These include:
the implementation of the Montreal Protocol (see summary);
the European Union (EU) Convention on the Protection of Life and Health of Animals and Plants;
the Convention on Biological Diversity.
For more information, see:
Chemical substances (European Commission).

Summary 113:
Eco-management and audit scheme (EMAS)
Eeco-management & audit scheme
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on the European Union’s Eco-Management and Audit Scheme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) scheme to encourage organisations to publish rigorous and independently verified environmental or sustainable development performance reports.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules and procedures for organisations to register with the European Commission and the responsibilities of environmental verifiers.
It also establishes a system for the verification of organisations’ environmental statements.
Organisations must:
register with the Commission;
ensure that their environmental statements are validated;
publish an environmental statement every 2 years;
verify their environmental performance and practices of contractors, subcontractors and suppliers;
assist the competent authorities in the application and enforcement of legislation relating to the environment;
provide information to the competent enforcement authorities on the applicable legal requirements relating to environmental matters.
The European Commission is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of the Member States and the European Environment Agency (EEA).
The Commission is responsible for:
assisting organisations relating to compliance with environmental requirements;
establishing an audit system;
monitoring the environmental performance of organisations;
checking that organisations comply with the requirements of the legislation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

Summary 114:
Spirit drinks: rules on the definition, description, presentation and labelling of spirit drinks and on the protection of geographical indications of certain spirit drinks (Regulation (EC) No 1791/2006)
Spirit drinks
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EU) No 596/2006 on the rules applicable to the definition and description, description and presentation and the labelling, as well as on the protected geographical indications, of spirit beverages
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the rules governing the definition of ‘spirit drinks’.
It also lays down the rules concerning sales denominations and geographical indications.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to spirit drinks which meet the definition laid down in Annex II of the regulation. It does not apply to drinks falling within CN codes 2203, 2204, 2205, 2206 and 2207, which are defined as spirit drinks.
The definition of a spirit drink is based on:
the composition, purity and alcoholic strength of the spirit drink;
the quantity of alcohol;
any other natural and/or nature-identical flavouring substances or flavouring preparations that may be used to flavour the drink; and
the sales denomination ‘Spirit drink’ which must be shown clearly and visibly in a prominent position on the label and must not be replaced or altered.
Specific rules concerning the sales denominations
Sales denominations
A spirit drink may only be sold under the name of any predominant flavour with the word ‘vodka’ or ‘mirabelle’, ‘mistrà’ and ‘pomace’ in addition to the sales denomination.
Sales denomination must be accompanied by the following information:
a description of the product’s characteristics;
an anethole content of not less than 1 gram and no more than 2 grams per litre,
the maximum hydrocyanic acid content of 5 grams per hectolitre of 100% vol.
A description of a product must contain:
an indication of the country of origin or the place of production;
a sales denomination;
descriptions of the products’ characteristics and their presentation;
description of the ingredients used to make the product.

Summary 115:
Common rules for safeguarding civil aviation against acts of unlawful interference with civil aircraft (common basic standards on aviation security)
Common rules on safeguarding Civil aviation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 on common basic standards for protecting civil aviation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes common European Union (EU) rules on civil aviation security.
It aims to protect persons and goods travelling within the European Union against unlawful interference*.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must draw up a national security programme for civil aviation. This programme must describe the methods and procedures which are to be followed by airport operators and other entities in order to comply both with this regulation and with the national aviation security programme of the EU country in which the airport is located.
Air carrier security programme
Air carriers must:
ensure that passengers and their baggage are screened and that they are not mixed with arriving passengers, unless:
the passengers arrive from a Member State that does not have the same level of security standards as that of the Member State concerned;
they arrive on flights from non-EU countries that have aviation security standards equivalent to those laid down in this regulation.
The airport operator must also ensure that:
passengers and their cabin baggage have been screened and have been trained in accordance with the common basic security standards;
airport security staff are properly trained;
security procedures or exemptions from security controls are granted;
technical specifications and procedures for approval and use of security equipment are approved and used;
requirements and procedures concerning potentially disruptive passengers are laid down.
air carriers must inform the European Commission of any deficiencies identified.
An annual report must be presented by the airport operator to the Commission every year. It must set out the measures taken to remedy any identified deficiencies and the penalties to be imposed for them.
In addition, the Commission must submit an annual report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every year on the implementation of the regulation. This report must include:
a description of the security procedures and procedures in place at the airport;
the number and type of personnel responsible for the screening of passengers and cabin baggage;
measures taken to prevent prohibited articles from being introduced into security restricted areas and on board an aircraft.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDITION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Civil aviation (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Unlawful interference: an act that jeopardises the security of civil aviation that threatens the safety of persons or goods.
* Passengers: passengers who are not passengers but who are passengers of an air carrier.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, ECSC, Euratom) No 550/2006 of 21 December 2006 establishing common rules for the safeguarding of Civil Aviation Against Acts of Lawful Interference with Civil Aviation (Common Basic Standards on Aviation Security)
Successive amendments to Regulation 2006/552/EC have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 117:
EU naval operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAVFORCE FOR MEDIACINI 2020/472
European Union naval operation on Libya
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020/473 on a European Union military operation in Libya
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the European Union (EU) to launch a military operation on the Mediterranean in order to combat human smuggling and trafficking in Libya.
It sets out the operational plan for the operation.
KEY POINTS
The operation is led by the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy, Federica Mogherini. The High Representative, assisted by the European External Action Service (EEAS), is the primary point of contact with the UN, the authorities of the countries in the region, and other international and bilateral actors, including NATO, the African Union and the League of Arab States.
The High Representative is responsible for:
coordinating the EU’s response and coordination with the United Nations (UN) and the countries involved in the stabilisation efforts in Libya;
monitoring the implementation of the UN arms embargo on Libya with aerial, satellite and maritime assets.
Training of the Libyan Coast Guard and Navy
ensuring that the Libyan coast guard and navy are equipped and equipped to combat illicit exports of petroleum from Libya, in accordance with UNSCR 2509 (2020), thereby contributing to situational awareness in the area of operation and in the Area of Interest.
Ensuring the protection of human trafficking and smuggling and human smuggling.
Safeguarding human trafficking.
Monitoring human trafficking, smuggling and smuggling, including crimes related to the security of the operation;
collecting, storing and transmitting data on human smuggling, and crimes relevant to the operation, which it may transmit to the relevant law enforcement authorities of EU countries and to competent EU bodies.
Coordination of EU military operations in Libya with the EU Military Committee (EUMC). The EUMC is chaired by the high representative of the EU. It reports to the Political and Security Committee (PSC).
The PSC may invite the EU Operation Commander or the EU Force Commander to its meetings, as appropriate. The PSC is in charge of the political control and strategic direction of EUNAFOR MED IRINI. It may decide to prolong the operation if the deployment of maritime assets in the operation produces a pull effect on migration on the basis of substantiated evidence gathered according to the criteria set in the operations plan.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The decision has applied since 31 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
On 19 January 2020, a Conference on Libya was held in Berlin on Libya. The Council authorised the EU to launch an operation in response to the situation in Libya and to support the efforts of the international and regional actors to stabilise the country.

Summary 118:
The FADO system for the exchange of information on authentic documents and on known methods of falsification between EU countries’ law enforcement authorities
The fado system for exchanging information on authenticity of documents
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — the European Agency for Delegated Regulation (FADO)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a system to exchange information between the European Union’s Member States’ authorities on authentic and false documents.
KEY POINTS
The European Agency (EASA) is responsible for the processing of personal data relating to authentic travel documents and for detecting false ones.
The EASA is a body set up by the European Parliament, the Council and the European Commission to:
ensure that only limited information related to an identified or identifiable person should be stored in the FadO system;
facilitate information exchange between the competent authorities of the Member States;
assist the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) in its work in the field of anti-fraud;
provide the Agency with information on false documents and methods of detecting them, as well as statistics on detected false ones;
make recommendations on effective ways of detecting and detecting them.
In addition, the Agency may transmit to the Agency the information on the documents referred to in the second and third subparagraphs of the regulation.
Processing of data by the Agency
The Agency:
processes personal data only to the extent strictly necessary for the purpose of operating the system in line with the principle of data minimisation in a way which does not permit the identification of any natural person without using additional data.
Any processing by Member States in the context of this regulation should be in accordance with the regulation or Directive 2016/680 on the protection of the personal data of EU citizens.
Member States must ensure that personal data are only processed in the manner strictly necessary to operate the FADSO system. The ownership of the existing data should then be transferred to the agency.
Delegated acts
The Commission may adopt delegated acts supplementing this regulation by establishing measures granting access to the actors listed in paragraph 4 of this article. The Commission must consult experts designated by each Member State and their experts systematically have access to meetings of Commission expert groups dealing with the preparation of delegated acts.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.

Summary 119:
Access to, or the pursuit of, regulated professions: non-discrimination and proportionality (Directive 2014/34/EU on access to, pursuit of and freedom to provide services)
Access to and pursuit of:
access to, and the freedom of providing services
The freedom to choose an occupation, as well as the freedom to conduct a business, is guaranteed by the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union (‘the Charter’).
The Charter also guarantees freedom of establishment and of the exercise of the right to provide a service.
In the absence of specific provisions harmonising the requirements on the access to a regulated profession or on the pursuit thereof laid down in EU law, it is a Member State’s competence to decide whether and how to regulate a profession within the limits of the principles of non-discrimination, proportionality and equality.
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It lays down rules on the conditions of access to and access to regulated professions.
It aims to ensure that:
the freedom of education or training consists of activities which are remunerated;
the right of establishment is guaranteed;
services are provided free of charge.
KEY POINTS
Member States must:
ensure that the regulation does not discriminate on the basis of nationality or residence;
require that the rules are proportionate and do not lead to a disproportionate burden on service providers nor should they hinder or render less attractive the exercise by service providers.
The Commission must assess the proportionality of the national measures in relation to the nature, the content and the content of the rules.
requirements concerning insurance cover or other means of personal or collective protection with regard to liability, language knowledge requirements, advertising requirements, insurance cover and insurance cover, and liability.
If the Commission considers that a national measure is disproportionate, it must inform all parties concerned and give them the opportunity to make their views known. The Commission should facilitate that exchange.
National authorities must ensure that those measures are not directly or indirectly discriminatory based on the nationality of persons or residence.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLACE INTO FORCE?
Directive (EU) 2015/24/EU countries had to incorporate it into their national law by 30 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to ensure the free movement of persons and services within the EU, on consumer choice and on the quality of the service provided.
For more information, see:
‘Access to the regulated professions’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
   
Council Directive 2014/35/EU of 30 June 2014 on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to the access of persons to and to the professions and repealing Council Directive 2005/36/EC.
SUMMARY
The aim of this Directive is to ensure:
that the conditions for access to or pursuit of regulated professions are based on public interest objectives;
that these objectives are met by ensuring that the requirements are not disproportionate and that they do not go beyond what is necessary to attain them.
This Directive applies to the following.
Requirements on access:
to qualifications, qualifications and training;
to professional qualifications;
professional training and training requirements;
education or training requirements.
Regulation:
procedures for the establishment and establishment of a profession;
rules for the qualification, establishment or establishment of an organisation or organisation;
requests for the recognition of qualifications, training or organisation of organisations or organisations;
exchanges of information on the qualifications and organisation of bodies and bodies of the profession; and
rules on the recognition and recognition of bodies or bodies of organisations of the regulated profession.
Exemptions from certain requirements:
exceptions to certain requirements, such as those relating to insurance cover (i.e. insurance cover is not covered by insurance cover) and liability;
insurance cover, liability and liability requirements, including for the protection of the health and well-being of the person concerned;
language knowledge requirements and the extent necessary to practise the profession, including the extent to which the profession is covered by the language knowledge requirement, advertising and advertising.

Summary 120:
Management of stocks of common interest in the North Sea and in its adjacent waters
Management of species of common interests in the north sea and in their adjacent waters.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the management of stock of species in North Sea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a multiannual management plan for the stocks of species which are subject to catch limits and to which this regulation applies.
It sets out objectives, quantifiable targets with clear timeframes, conservation reference points, safeguards and technical measures designed to avoid and reduce unwanted catches.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
fishing opportunities;
by-catch stocks;
the management of mixed fisheries.
Fishing opportunities
The European Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts in accordance with Article 16 of this regulation and to supplement this regulation by specifying details of that obligation as provided for in points (a) to (e) of Article 15(5) of the regulation.
The Commission’s delegated acts must:
be based on scientific advice provided by the International Council for the Exploration of the Sea (ICES);
contain the objectives and objectives set out in the plan;
be consistent with the objectives of the European Union fisheries policy;
include the target fishing mortality (FMSY) that corresponds to the objective of reaching and maintaining MSY as ranges of values which are consistent with achieving MSY.
By-catch management
The plan must: cover:
catch limits;
target fishing mortality;
management of by-catch;
preventing discards;
avoiding and reducing, as far as possible, unwanted catches;
ensuring that the stock size is maintained at levels consistent with MSY;
provide the Commission with the information it needs to draw up and implement the management plan.
In addition, the Commission may:
adopt delegated acts adjusting the geographical distribution of stocks if the scientific advice given by ICES indicates that a change in the distribution of the relevant stocks is likely to lead to a premature closure of the fishery;
adjust the fishing opportunities between consecutive years to not more than 20% of the total number of fishing opportunities.
Monitoring and monitoring
The management of stocks must be based on:
scientific advice;
monitoring data;
assessment of the impact of fishing activities on the marine ecosystem; and
assessments of the effects of non-fishing activities on marine ecosystems.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU fisheries policy is based on the sustainable management of fish stocks. It aims to restore fish stocks, in the shortest time feasible, at least to levels that can produce MSY, as determined by their biological characteristics.
For more information, see:
Fisheries policy (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Fishing opportunities: the number of fish that can be caught by a fishery.
* By-catch: the catch of fish which is not intended for commercial purposes.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 3/2014 of 21 December 2013 on the implementation of the common fisheries policy of the Union and repealing Regulation (EC) No 676/2007 and amending Regulations (EC, EFTA, EU, USA, Japan, Iceland, Norway, Switzerland and the United Nations Convention on the Law of Sea (OJ L 347, 23.12.2013, pp. 1-8)

Summary 122:
Controls on cash entering and leaving the EU
Countries must ensure that cash is declared to the competent authorities of the EU country through which they are entering or leaving the Union and make it available to them for control.
Categories of cash
The European Union (EU) has introduced a system of controls with respect to cash entering or departing the EU to complement the legal framework for the prevention of money laundering and terrorist financing laid down in Directive 2015/849.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the declaration of cash to the authorities of EU countries when it enters or leaves the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
carriers who carry cash on their person, in their luggage or in their means of transport;
unaccompanied cash;
cash making up part of a consignment without a carrier;
criminal activity;
financial intelligence units (FIUs) established in an EU country for the purposes of implementing Article 32(2) of Directive (EU).
The EU’s financial intelligence unit (FIU) is the entity established in a Member State for the purpose of preventing money laundering or terrorist financing.
The obligation to declare cash must be subject to a threshold of €10 000 or more. Carriers must also declare amounts suspected to be related to criminal activity. In order not to restrict free movement unduly or overburden citizens and authorities with administrative formalities, the obligation should not exceed € 10 000.
In addition, it should also be possible to share information relating to sub-threshold amounts with the authorities in other Member States if there are indications of criminal activity, in other EU countries.
EU countries must:
ensure that the information obtained by the authorities is covered by the duty of professional secrecy;
make a disclosure declaration, systematically or on a case-by-case basis, in accordance with national procedures.
impose sanctions on persons who fail to comply with this obligation. These sanctions should include:
temporary detention of cash by competent authorities;
suspension of cash for up to 6 months;
restrictions on the movement of cash in and out of the European Union.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION APPLY?
Regulation (EC) No 1889/2005 has applied since 16 October 2005 and had to become law in the Member States by 16 October 2006.
BACKGROUND
The reintroduction of illicit proceeds into the economy and the diversion of money to finance illicit activities create distortions and unfair competitive disadvantages for law-abiding citizens and companies, and are therefore a threat to the functioning of the internal market. In light of these developments and on the basis of the evaluation of existing EU legislation, Regulation (EC), No 89/2005 needs to be amended.
For more information, see:
‘Carrying cash’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Carriers: any natural person who is a carrier of goods or people. The definition of ‘carrier’ should be understood as excluding those carriers who undertake the professional conveyance of goods and people.
Unaccompanied cash: cash made up of part of the consignment of goods.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, Euratom of 25 October 2005 on the introduction of controls on cash (OJ L 309, 26.11.2005, pp. 1-9)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom, EJ L 308, 26 November 2005, pp 1-2)
last update 04.02.2018

Energy Union objectives and targets — 2020-2026

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/842 on the Energy Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the energy union’s objectives, targets and targets for the period 2021-2027.

KEY POINTS
The Energy Union aims to:
ensure that energy security is maintained;

decarbonisation;
reduce greenhouse gas emissions;
increase renewable energy;
contribute to climate change.
The objectives cover five dimensions: energy security; the internal energy market; energy efficiency*; decarbonisation*; and research, innovation and competitiveness.
Long-term strategies
These include:
energy security;
energy efficiency* (including smart grids, smart grids* and energy storage);
renewable energy* (i.e. renewable energy);
research, innovation* and competitiveness;

take action to address any remaining collective gap.
If the Commission concludes that an EU country is not meeting its objectives, its targets and contributions, its reference points, its energy efficiency target or its renewable energy targets, or that it is not implementing its integrated national climate and energy plan, it must issue recommendations to the EU country concerned pursuant to Article 34.

Summary 124:
POPs — Regulation (EU) 2021/841
POPS — Regulation(EU) 2020/831
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 841/2014 on POPs
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the maximum concentration limits for certain substances, mixtures and articles that may be present in mixtures or articles in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to substances that are unintentional by-products of industrial processes, such as polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs) and polychlorinated biphenyls (PCDs). It does not apply to waste.
The maximum concentration limit is set at 500 mg/kg (mg/kg).
The European Commission will review the regulation by 16 July 2021. This review will assess, inter alia, all relevant impacts with regard to the sum of the concentration of those substances up to a maximum concentration of 500 mg /kg where they are present in a mixture or article, subject to review and assessment by the Commission.
Exemptions from control measures
Certain substances may be exempt from the regulation’s control measures if they meet certain conditions. These conditions include:
they do not pose a risk to human health or the environment;
they are not a threat to public health or public safety;
the substances are not hazardous to the environment.
EU countries must monitor the use and management of notified stockpiles.
In addition of substances to the list of POPs is prohibited.
they must ensure that they are only used for the purpose of preventing, limiting or limiting the release of substances.
They must also ensure that the substances are used only for the purposes of preventing or limiting their release.
National authorities must:
monitor the use, management and disposal of stockpiles of substances and mixtures;
ensure that the quantities of substances in stockpiles are not exceeded;
assess the effects of the substances on human health, the environment and the economy;
take appropriate action to prevent, limit or limit the quantities and quantities of chemicals in the stockpiles;
monitor and assess the impact of the chemicals on the environment, the economy and the health and safety of the public.
For matters relating to waste, the Commission must be assisted by the Committee established by Article 39 of Directive 2008/98/EC.
Penalties
The Commission must impose effective, proportionate and dissuasive penalties for infringements of the above rules.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 16 July 2014 and had to become law in the EU countries by 16.7.2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat POPs, including:
Protocol and Convention on the Elimination of Pollutants from Chemicals (Protocol)
Convention on Pollutant Substances (Convention)
Protection of substances from Chemtrafficking in Hazardous Substances
International Action Plan for POPs (POP)
European Union Framework Convention on Polluter-Related Substances and Protocol on the Protection of Health and Safety of Animals (PAP)
EU Action Plan on the Prevention of Pollution from Chemical Substances.

Single-used plastic products : reducing waste AND pollution
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) No 1025/2012 on the reduction of plastic waste
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce the quantity of waste and pollution from single-use products* resulting from the increasing use of plastic in short-lived applications, which are not designed for re-use or cost-effective recycling.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all products, including personal care products, household products and fishing gear, which can be placed on the market in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to:
personal care products;
personal hygiene products; and
fishing gear containing plastic.
It does not cover:
single-use items, such as balloons, balloons and balloons containing plastic;
balloons containing plastic for personal care;
professional care products.
Producer responsibility
Producers of these products are responsible for the costs of cleaning up litter. They must:
cover the costs associated with the collection and disposal of litter;
ensure that products are made available free of charge at the point of sale to the final consumer;
make available alternatives to the products listed in Part A of the directive’s annex to the end-users’ information sheet.
Implementing acts
The European Commission must adopt implementing acts laying down the format for reporting data and information to the European Parliament, the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Committee on the collection, disposal and recycling of marine litter.
