Summary 151:
Statistics on the prevention of illegal entry and stay (2008-2013) (Eurostat Regulation) (European Commission)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 343/2003 on the collection and analysis of Community statistics in the field of migration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection, analysis and dissemination of statistics on migration and asylum in the European Union (EU).
It repeals Regulation (EEC) 311/76.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries and to Eurostat (the European Union’s statistics office).
EU countries must provide Eurostat with all information necessary to produce statistics on:
the number of persons illegally entering and staying in the EU;
the numbers of requests for taking back or taking charge of an asylum seeker.
These statistics must relate to reference periods of one calendar year and must be supplied to the European Commission within 3 months of the end of the reference year.
Data sources and quality standards
Eurostat must:
ensure the quality, reliability, objectivity, scientific independence, scientific confidentiality, cost-effectiveness and statistical confidentiality of the data it receives;
assess the quality and reliability of the information it receives and the quality of the statistical sources it uses;
specify the statistical methods used to produce the statistics.
The European Commission must ensure that the statistics it receives are:
accurate, reliable, objective, scientific and statistical accurate;
consistent with EU law and practice;
conform with the statistical standards set out in Council Decision 89/382/EEC of 19 June 1989 establishing a Committee on the Statistical Programmes of the European Communities.
In addition, Eurostat must ensure:
data sources are reliable and reliable;
data quality standards are met; and
the data are collected and analysed in accordance with the European Statistical Code of Practice (ESCP).
The Commission must also:
analyse the data provided by the EU countries;
check that the data are reliable, accurate, scientific, methodological and statistical standards have been met;
make recommendations to EU countries on how to improve the reliability and accuracy of the statistics they provide;
draw up a report on the implementation of the regulation and on the results of its implementation.
References
Reference Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 552/2008
The first reference month for the regulation was January 2008. The second reference month was January 2009. The third reference period was January 2011. The fourth reference year was January 2012.
Reference Year 2008
The reference year 2008 was the first reference year for which Eurostat was able to collect statistics on the number of illegal immigrants and asylum seekers. The first reference period for the second reference period (January 2011) was the second.
For the reference period 2008, the statistics supplied by EU countries were:
total number of people illegally entering the EU and staying illegally;
total numbers of asylum seekers;
number of requests to take back or take charge of a non-EU national;
recipients of a visa or immigration status granted instead of a residence permit.
This regulation does not cover estimates of the number illegally resident in the Member States.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Council Regulation (EU) No 344/2009 has applied since 20 August 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Migration and asylum statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Illegal immigration: the illegal entry or stay of a person into or staying illegally in an EU country.

Summary 152:
Auditing of statutory auditors and audit firms in the European Union (EU) (Regulation (EC) No 561/2011)
Auditing and audit firm selection in the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation No 551/2011/EC on statutory audit
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure for the selection of statutory auditor or audit firm.
It sets out the principles of independence and professional secrecy for statutory audit firms.
KEY POINTS
The selection procedure is carried out by an audit committee composed of representatives of the European Parliament, the Council, the European Commission and the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA).
The committee is responsible for:
the selection of the statutory auditor;
the audit firm; and
the quality assurance, market monitoring, and transparency of the competent authorities.
The audit committee is chaired by a European Parliament and Council representative. The chair is elected for a 4-year term. The CEAOB is composed of a representative from each EU Member State and one representative from the European Economic and Social Committee of the Regions.
Auditors are appointed by the audit committee on the basis of a recommendation from the audit firm’s board of directors. The audit committee may make a recommendation to the auditor for an audit engagement. The recommendation must be justified and contain at least two choices for the audit engagement and the committee must express a duly justified preference for one of them. The committee may also express a preference for a third party if the audited entity were to enter into a contract with a non-audited entity or if the auditor was to perform audit work for an EU institution or entity with special relations with an EU country.
A sound engagement quality control review of the work carried out in each statutory audit engagement should be conducive to high audit quality. This review should be carried out every 2 years.
An audit report must be in clear and unambiguous language. It must be prepared by an independent audit firm and must contain the following information:
a description of the audit work;
a report on the overall results of the quality assurance system on an annual basis;
an audit report on any irregularities that may have occurred during the engagement;
any other information that may be relevant to the audit report.
In addition, the committee should:
identify any irregularities and take appropriate measures to deal with such irregularities and to prevent any recurrence of such irregularities;
ensure that the auditor and the auditor firm are informed of any threats to their independence and the safeguards applied to mitigate those threats;
make an annual report on their work on the quality of their work.
Quality assurance and transparency
Audits must comply with the following requirements:
they must be independent and transparent;
their work must be performed in accordance with the principles laid down in Article 2 of Directive 2006/43/EC;
they should be independent of any persons who work or who have worked for the bodies involved in the selection procedure.
If an audit firm is employed by a statutory auditor, the obligations under this regulation are incumbent on the auditor. The statutory auditor must ensure that its work complies with the requirements laid down by this regulation. The auditor must also ensure that their work is independent and that they are not involved in any conflicts of interest or conflicts of interests.
Regulatory cooperation between competent authorities
The regulation sets up the European European Auditors’ Association (EGAOB). It is the body responsible for the coordination of statutory audits and the establishment of supervisory convergence with non-EU countries. It is also responsible for establishing the European Supervisory Network of Auditors (ESNAA) and for the setting up of a European Audit Network of Supervisory Authorities (EASA). The EASA was established by Directive 2005/909/EC (see summary).
It is assisted by the European Financial Services Authority (EFSA), the European Bank for Reconstruction and Development (EFSDA) and the Committee on Auditors and Supervising Auditors.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to strengthen the independence of statutory audit bodies and to improve the audit quality of audit firms performing statutory audits.
For more information, see:
‘Audits and audits’ on the EEAOB’ website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 530/2011 of 21 December 2011 on statutory auditor selection and selection procedure (OJ L 127, 24.12.2011, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 530/11 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 153:
Conservation of marine biological resources and fisheries targeting them (CFP) (PDF)SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 847/96 — common fisheries policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the conservation and management of the marine environment and the fisheries targeting it.
It repeals and replaces Council Decision 2004/585/EC.
KEY POINTS
The CFP is the European Union’s (EU) fisheries policy. It aims to protect and restore the marine ecosystems of the Mediterranean, Black Sea and North Atlantic. It also aims to ensure that aquaculture and fisheries activities avoid the degradation of these ecosystems.
The scope of the CFP includes:
the conservation of marine ecosystems and the management of fisheries targeting these ecosystems;
the sustainable fisheries partnership agreements (SFFAs);
regional cooperation on conservation measures.
Fishing opportunities
EU countries must use transparent and objective criteria when allocating the fishing opportunities available to them, including those of an environmental, social and economic nature. They must inform the European Commission of the allocation method and the reasons for it. The Commission must encourage the exchange of information and best practices among EU countries and facilitate the coordination of national measures foreseen in the multiannual national strategic plan.
When non-compliance is detected, EU countries must take appropriate measures. Such measures must be proportionate to the nature, gravity, duration and repetition of the non–compliance.
Sustainable fisheries partnerships agreements (SSAs)
SDFAs aim to:
ensure that sustainable fisheries activities are minimised;
promote the sustainable management of marine resources;
encourage the development and implementation of conservation measures;
improve the protection of marine biodiversity.
Regional cooperation
EU Member States must:
cooperate with each other on the implementation of SDFAs;
coordinate their actions with the aim of:
promoting sustainable fisheries management;
ensuring that sustainable fishing activities are implemented in accordance with the principles of ecosystem-based fisheries management.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 25 March 2010 and had to become law in the Member States by 31 March 2015.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing measures, including:
rules for the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA);
rules on the European Fisheries Management Agency (EFMA);
the European Fisheries Protection Agency (EFPA); and
rules of the European Council for the Common Fisheries Policy.
For more information, see:
Fisheries policy (European Commission).

Common organisation of the markets in fishery and aquaculture products (CMO) (summary) Common organisation of markets in fish and shellfish
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 laying down common rules for the common organisation of market information in fisheries and aqua-fishery products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the common rules and procedures for the marketing of fishery products in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The CMO is an integral part of the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) and should contribute to achieving the objectives of the CFP. It aims to:
ensure fair competition in the fishery sector;
improve consumer information;
provide market intelligence.
The regulation sets out the following elements: professional organisations;marketing standards;consumer information;competition rules;market intelligence. fisheries products are marketed in accordance with the CMO’s rules;
the products are labelled according to the relevant EU legislation; and
the prices triggering the storage mechanism are set at prices which do not comply with this regulation.

The Commission must, by 1 or marked prior to 13 December 2014, report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) on the products marketed in the EU and how they are labelled. The report must include:
the name of the product;
a description of the products;
an indication of the stocks of fish, shellfish or shellfish that have been used up.

Summary 155:
Securities settlement systems (CSDs) — common prudential requirements
Securities settlements systems (SSDs)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on securities settlement systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the establishment of a common framework for the securities settlement system* (CSD)* in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the safety, efficiency and competition of the EU’s securities settlement markets.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all CSDs that are authorised to provide securities settlement services in the EU. It does not apply to non-banking-type ancillary services, such as credit information services, which are regulated by the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA).
Authorisation
A CSD must be authorised by the competent authorities of an EU Member State to provide services to an issuer. The authorisation must specify the core services listed in Section A of the annex to the regulation, as well as the non-bank-type services permitted under Section B of the Annex, which the CSD is authorised to offer.
The competent authorities must consult each other regarding the approval of a CSD link and, where necessary, withdraw the authorisation if it becomes aware of a serious risk to the security of the securities market or of the financial stability of the host Member State. The CSD may refuse to provide a service if:
the issuer has not been informed of the reasons for its refusal;
the CSD has not complied with the conditions of its authorisation;
there is a risk of cross-subsidisation by an issuer;
an issuer is not satisfied with the quality of the services provided.
the relevant authorities must immediately inform the relevant authorities and, if applicable, the competent authority on the necessity to withdraw an authorisation.
In order to ensure an appropriate level of safety in the provision of CSD services by third-country CSDs, such CSDs must be subject to recognition by ESMA where they intend to provide certain services or to set up a branch in the Union.
Compliance with prudential standards
The European Commission must adopt technical standards for CSDs in the event of a significant risk of disrupting operations. These standards must be adopted by 18 June 2015. The Commission must submit these technical standards to the European System of Financial Supervision and Coordination (ESMSC).
The Commission must also adopt the implementing technical standards in accordance with Articles 10 to 14 of the regulation in the case of events that threaten to disrupt operations.
Regulatory conduct of business rules
CSDs must publicly disclose prices and fees for their services. These fees must be clearly disclosed to the participants. They must maintain analytical accounting for their activities and maintain a reconciliation of the results of any infringements.
They must keep a register of the activities of their subsidiaries and branches in the host EU country, which must be made public.
An EU country may impose sanctions on an authorised CSD for infringements of the rules laid down in the regulation. These sanctions must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
EU countries must ensure that CSDs comply with the requirements laid down by the regulation and that they are subject to the supervision of the ESMA.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 18 June 2011 and had to become law in the Member States by 18 July 2015.
BACKGROUND
The securities settlement process is one of the most important aspects of the functioning of the European financial system. It is a key component of the overall financial system and the main means by which securities are settled.
For more information, see:
The securitisation of securities (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Securities settlement system: a system that is used to settle securities transactions between issuers and counterparties.
* Credit information services: a set of services that are provided by a centralised centralised financial institution.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 181/2011 of 21 May 2011 laying down rules on the establishment, operation and supervision of a system for the settlement of securities securities (Securities Settlement Systems) (Codified version) (OJ L 127, 22.5.2011, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 181/11 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

  The European Parliament and the Council adopted Regulation (EC) No 1095/2010 of 22 May 2010 laying down prudential rules for market operators and for securities settlements systems.

Summary 156:
Cohesion Fund for transport infrastructure — environmental and trans-European networks in the area of transport infrastructure (Cohesion Regulation)
Cohesion fund for transport infrastructures — environment and transport infrastructure
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 establishing the European Cohesion Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and the European Transport Fund (ETF).
It sets out the rules for the EU’s investment priorities for projects in the fields of environment, transport infrastructure and public administration.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1084/2006.
KEY POINTS
Objectives
The ERDF and the ETF aim to:
promote environmental protection;
reduce greenhouse gas emissions;
improve public administration and public services;
enhancing institutional capacity of public authorities and stakeholders and efficient public administration through actions to strengthen the institutional capacity and the efficiency of public administrations and services related to the implementation of the EDF.
The EDF is responsible for:
supporting transport infrastructure projects of European added value (i.e. projects that contribute to the creation of a European transport network) and for the sustainable development of the European transport market;
promoting environmental protection, including:
reducing air pollution;
improving noise-reduction measures;
encouraging the use of renewable energy;
ensuring the efficient public transport systems.
In order to achieve these objectives, the European Commission has adopted a list of common output indicators (e.g. These include:
polluter-friendly transport projects;
investment to achieve the reduction of greenhouse gas emission emissions from activities listed in Annex I to Directive 2003/87/EC;
environmental protection, i.
Public administrations and stakeholders
The European Commission must ensure that, in promoting risk management investment, specific risks at regional, cross-border and transnational level are taken into account. In order to concentrate efforts in that regard, priority should be given to projects of common interest as defined in Annex II.
Investment priorities
The investment priorities set out in Annex III of the regulation are based on the investment priorities identified by the European Parliament and the Council in the framework of the Connecting Europe Facility (see summary). These investment priorities should set out detailed objectives, which are not mutually exclusive, to which the Fund is to contribute. They should be aligned with the thematic objectives set out by the ERDF. In addition, they should take account of the economic, social and environmental aspects of the transport sector, in particular:
the environmental impact of transport;
the social and economic aspects of transport.
Funding
The Fund is funded by the EU budget for the period 2014-2020. It has a budget of € 10,000,000.
Eligible projects
Projects eligible for support from the Fund are:
trans-European transport projects under the European Network of Regional Development Funds (ENRF);
transport infrastructure projects funded under the ETSF;
projects financed under the EU Transport Fund;
transnational transport projects financed by the EEF.
Specific provisions concerning the type of activities which can be supported by the Fund, in order to contribute to these investment priorities, are laid down in the annexes to the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2014 and had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
The Cohesion fund was established in 2013. It is one of the main instruments of the ‘connecting Europe’ programme, which aims to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the EU transport system.
For more information, see:
‘The European Transport Network’ website.

Summary 157:
Sustainable urban development — European Regional Development Fund
Sustainability — European regional development fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 on the sustainable urban development fund (ERDF)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the structure and rules for the European regional Development Fund.
It aims to reduce disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reduce the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
Scope of support
The ERDF’s main objective is to:
contribute to the development of sustainable urban areas in the European Union (EU);
reduce disparities between regions in terms of the level of development;
improve the quality of life of people in the least developed regions;
promote a low-carbon economy.
The European territorial cooperation goal is one of the main objectives of the ERDF. The other objectives are:
promoting regional cooperation;
reducing disparities between regional development levels;
contributing to the creation of a sustainable urban environment;
encouraging the sharing of facilities and human resources across borders;
supporting innovative actions in the area of urban development.
In order to reinforce capacity-building, networking and exchange of experience between programmes and bodies responsible for implementing urban development strategies and innovative actions, it is necessary to establish an urban development network at EU level.
the ERDF may also support the following actions:
urban development strategies;
urban planning and urban planning;
sustainable urban management;
research and innovation, information and communication technologies (ICT), small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) and promoting a low carbon economy;
social economy enterprises.
Funding
The funding is allocated pro rata to the different categories of regions based on their relative shares of the total population of the EU Member State concerned. The ERDF is managed by a managing authority. The managing authority may retain the right to undertake a final verification of eligibility of operations before approval.
Specific objectives
Depending on which category of regions is supported, the support under the Investment for growth and jobs goal should be concentrated on research and innovation (R&I) and SMEs;
information and communication technology (ICT);
sustainability;
environmental protection;
smart cities;
transport;
health and well-being;
education and training;
science and technology (S&T);
science, technology and innovation;
agriculture and forestry;
energy efficiency;
the environment and climate change;
public transport;
infrastructure;
economic development and social inclusion.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND

Summary 158:
European territorial cooperation — 2014-2020 (ERDF Regulation)
Eastern territorial cooperation – 2014-20
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 on the European territorial cooperation goal
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
   
It sets out the general principles and objectives of the European Territorial Cooperation (ERD) programme for the 2014–2020 period.
It also sets out rules for the specific character of cooperation programmes.
KEY POINTS
The ERDF’s goal is to contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to rural areas, areas affected by industrial transition, and regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
To achieve this goal, the ERDF aims to strengthen cooperation by means of actions conducive to integrated territorial development linked to the EU‘s cohesion policy priorities, and to the development of territorial cooperation not only in the areas covered by transnational cooperation programmes, but also in other areas such as maritime cooperation, interregional cooperation and maritime cooperation.
The regulation sets out:
the general principles of the ERD;
the specific objectives for territorial cooperation, i.e. to reduce disparities between regions, to improve the quality of life of the most disadvantaged regions and the most in need of assistance, particularly in rural areas;
transnational cooperation, which aims to enhance the integration of regions into the European Union (EU),
the development of regional cooperation in the field of territorial development,
the promotion of maritime cooperation and
the improvement of maritime relations between the EU countries and territories.
In addition, the regulation sets up a monitoring committee, composed of representatives of the participating EU countries, the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Fund (ESF).
The monitoring committee is responsible for:
monitoring the implementation of the programme;
assessing the progress made by the participating countries in the programme implementation;
checking that the programme is being implemented correctly;
ensuring that the expenditure is being reported to the lead beneficiary;
setting out the criteria for the selection of the lead beneficiaries;
establishing the monitoring committee and its responsibilities.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European territorial development goal is one of the main aims of the EU's cohesion policy. It aims to improve territorial development and to reduce the development gap between the regions of the different EU countries.
For more information, see:
European territorial development (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Territorial cooperation: cooperation between countries, regions and territories which are part of the Union.
* Regional cooperation: the cooperation of countries, territories and countries’ territories with which the EU has signed an agreement on territorial cooperation agreements.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1301/2013 of 13 December 2013 establishing the European Regional Development Fund and amending Council Regulation (EC) No 1201/2014 on the territorial cooperation objective for the period 2014–20

Summary 159:
Trans-European energy infrastructure — permit granting process
Trans-Eastern energy infrastructure – permit granting procedure
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 347/2013 — streamlining and improving permit granting processes
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to streamline and improve permit granting procedures for the construction of new energy infrastructure in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to projects of common interest (i.e. projects that are considered by competent authorities as being in the public interest).
The European Commission must ensure that the decision with regard to the overriding public interest of a project is taken within the time limit set out in Article 10(1) of this regulation.
Permits must be granted to projects that:
contribute to the development of the EU’s energy infrastructure;
improve the efficiency of the electricity grid;
reduce greenhouse gas emissions and the environmental impact of electricity grid infrastructure.
Projects must meet the following criteria:
level of sustainability;
environmental impact;
cost-effectiveness.
The Commission and the competent authority referred to in Article 9 of the regulation must ensure:
that the decision to grant a permit is taken in the interest of the public;
the public interest is taken into account in the decision-making process;
there is no conflict of interest between public and private interests;
public interest related to its territory.
In addition, the Commission must:
monitor the implementation of projects in the context of the European energy infrastructures;
ensure that projects are implemented in line with the objectives of the Europe 2020 strategy;
make recommendations to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions;
draw up a report every 2 years on the experience gained from the monitoring of implementation of these projects;
assess the impact of the projects on the environment and on public health;
take the necessary measures to ensure that projects comply with the requirements laid down in Regulation (EC) No 714/2009 on energy infrastructure and the European Energy Agency (EEA);
make proposals for the financing of energy infrastructure projects.
Implementation of the Regulation
EU countries must submit a report to the Commission every two years on how the regulation is being implemented. The Commission then transmits the report to EU countries and the EEA, which then forwards it to the EU countries, the EEA and the regional groups concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU has adopted a number of measures to modernise and modernise its energy infrastructure. These include:
the European energy strategy (see summary);
the Energy Agency, which is responsible for the coordination of the regulatory framework for the energy sector in the EU.
For more information, see:
Energy infrastructure (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Permit granting process: the procedure for granting a permit to construct an energy infrastructure project.
* Energy infrastructure: the construction, operation and maintenance of electricity transmission, distribution and distribution networks, including the transmission of electricity.

Summary 160:
Alternative dispute resolution (ADR) for online sales and service contracts (ODR platform)
Alternative dispute Resolution (ODR)* for online selling and services contracts (OLR platform).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on alternative dispute resolution for e-commerce transactions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the Online Dispute Resolution* (OLDR) platform to resolve disputes between traders and consumers over online sales or service contracts.
It establishes the ODR platform, which is managed by the European Commission.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to online sales* and service* contracts where the trader, or the trader’s intermediary, has offered goods or services through a website or by other electronic means and the consumer has ordered those goods and services on that website.
EU countries must ensure that traders and their intermediaries:
ensure that consumers have access to an electronic complaint form made available on the platform;
provide an electronic translation function which enables the parties and the ADR entity to have the information which is exchanged through the platform and is necessary for the resolution of the dispute translated, where appropriate.
The European Commission must:
monitor the use and functioning of ODR and draw up reports on its operation;
assess the results of the testing and take the appropriate measures to address potential problems in order to ensure the effective functioning of the platform.
In addition, the Commission must ensure:
that the information referred to in the annex to this regulation is accurate, up to date and provided in a clear, understandable and easily accessible way;
that consumers have the right to have their personal data processed in accordance with the rules on the protection of personal data.
ADR entities:
may use a case management tool to settle disputes between consumers and traders;
may communicate with consumers about ADR procedures.
If a dispute cannot be resolved by a dispute resolution procedure, the parties may:
agree to use an online dispute resolution* system;
agree that the dispute should be settled by an online ADR* system.
Disputes may be settled via a dispute settlement* system, if the parties agree to do so.
Complaints may be submitted to an ODR entity, which automatically and without delay transmits the complaint to the trader that the parties have agreed to use. The trader may also send a complaint to a third party, such as an online seller or a service provider. The third party may then send a response to the complaint.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
    баркова градка  
It has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
  нарга дантанка is one of the European Consumer Centres (ECCs).
For more information, see:
Online Disputes Resolution (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Alternative dispute resolution: a procedure that allows traders to resolve a dispute with consumers without resorting to a court of law.
* Online sales contract: a sales contract where a trader offers goods or service to a consumer.

Summary 162:
Support programme for the smaller Aegean islands
Support Programme for the small islands
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1122/2009 on the European Union’s support programme for small and medium-sized islands
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) support programme to supply the smaller islands with agricultural products and to support local agricultural production.
It sets out detailed rules for the implementation of the programme.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all the islands except Crete and Evia.
The aim of the support programme is to:
support the development of agricultural production and local agricultural activities;
encourage farmers to supply quality products;
promote trade between the islands;
improve the marketing of quality agricultural products.
Measures to assist local production
The European Commission must adopt implementing acts to establish the measures supporting local production. These measures must be consistent with other EU policies and with the measures taken under such policies.
Specific supply arrangements are established for the EU's agricultural products listed in Annex I to the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU, which are essential for human consumption, for the manufacture of other products or as agricultural inputs. These supply arrangements must be based on a forecast supply balance and must not lead to diversions of trade in the products concerned, nor should the economic advantages of the specific supply arrangements lead to diversion of trade.
In addition, the Commission must establish a register of operators and a system for the production of quality products. The Commission must also establish a system of checks to ensure that payments are made only on condition that they are actually passed on, and appropriate checks are carried out.
A derogation may be granted from the Commission's consistent policy of not authorising aid to the islands if the measures are not being carried out in line with the requirements of the islands and the strategy proposed. In such cases, Greece may submit to the Commission duly substantiated proposals for amendments to those measures within the context of the financial allocation referred to in Article 18(2) and (3) of the regulation.
Amendments to the regulation
The Commission may adopt amendments to the measures adopted under the regulation in respect of supplementing or modifying certain non-essential elements of this regulation. These amendments must be approved by the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC).
The measures adopted by the Commission are subject to the same conditions as those laid down in the regulation on support for rural development by the EAFRD.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 November 2009 and had to become law in the EU countries by 30 November 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
The smaller islands constitute an essential tool for supplying those islands with goods and services and for supporting local agriculture.
For more information, see:
Smaller islands (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Smaller islands: those whose population is less than 1 000 people.
* Medium-sized and small-scale agricultural production: the production and marketing of agricultural products, including by the insularity, small size and distance from markets of the smaller smaller islands.

Summary 163:
Road transport statistics: road vehicle and goods statistics (Regulation (EC) No 596/1999)
Road transport Statistics: road transport statistics (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation No 598/1999 on the collection of statistics on the carriage of goods by road
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and transmission of statistical data relating to:
vehicle;
journey;
goods.
It repeals Regulation (EC, No 597/98) which had to become law in the European Union (EU) countries by 31 December 1999.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must compile statistics relating to the following areas:
the road vehicle for the transport of goods;
the journey; and
the goods road transport vehicle.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 3 years on the implementation of this regulation. The report must evaluate in particular the quality of the statistical data transmitted, the data collection methods and the administrative burdens on the EU countries and on the respondents. It must also assess the structure and content of the results to be disseminated.
Road vehicle: the road vehicle used to transport goods.
Journey: the journey to and from the destination of the destination.
Goods: the goods transported by road.
Load capacity: the maximum weight of goods declared permissible by the competent authority of the country of registration of the vehicle. The maximum permissible weight, in 100 kg, is set at 100 kg.
Where a road vehicle is a road train made up of a lorry with a trailer, the maximum permissible load is 100 kg (100 kg).
Road tractor and semi-trailer: the number of axles for combinations of vehicles: road tractor or semi-tractor with trailer.
Successive configurations of road transport vehicles: a road lorry used alone, namely without a trailer throughout the survey period, or in combination with another road vehicle;a road tractor with trailer;a combination of road tractors or semi tractors with trailer and a road trailer; anda road train consisting of 2 lorries with trailer, a road tractor or a road hauler.
Uniform nomenclature of goods for transport statistics.
Nomenclatures: the NST classification of goods in use, based on the 20 divisions derived from the European Standard for Transport Statistics (NST classification).
The most frequently-used codes are given in the table in point 2(b) of the annex to the regulation. These codes are: ‘goods’, ‘vehicle’ and ‘journey’; ‘load capacity’.

Summary 164:
Serving of judicial and extrajudicial documents in civil and commercial matters in the European Union (EU) (Regulation (EC) No 1348/2000 on the service in the Member States of judicial, extrajudicial and commercial documents in Civil and Commercial Matters)
Serving in the Community of judicial or extrajudicial matters in civil or commercial matters
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1201/2006 on the Service in the EU of judicial* and other extrajudicial* documents to be served in civil matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European system for the transmission of judicial documents to other EU countries.
It repeals and replaces Council Regulation (EU, EEC) 1348 (2000).
KEY POINTS
Each EU country must designate the public officers, authorities or other persons, public authorities, public bodies or other bodies, who are competent for the transmitting and receiving agencies for the service of judicial (or extrajudicial) documents.
The European Judicial Network in Civil Matters (EJNIC) is a network of public bodies and bodies responsible for the exchange of information between EU countries on judicial and other judicial matters. It is based on the Convention on the application of the Protocol on the position of the United Kingdom and Ireland, annexed to the Treaty establishing the European Community (Article 3 of the Treaty on European Union).
The regulation applies to the following:
the service of a document on the party’s authorised representative in an EU country where the proceedings are taking place regardless of the place of residence of that party;
the use of a particular method of service.
When a document is refused, the addressee has the right to refuse it.
A receiving agency must send a receipt to the transmitting agency by the swiftest possible means of transmission using the standard form set out in Annex I.
If the request for service cannot be fulfilled on the basis of the information or documents transmitted, the receiving agency shall contact the transmitting agencies by the fastest possible means in order to secure the missing information or document.
In the event that a document cannot be served within a particular period, the date to be taken into account with respect to the applicant should be that determined by the law of that EU country.
Refusal to accept a document
The applicant may refuse the service on the grounds of:
costs occasioned by recourse to a judicial officer or to a person competent under the law in the country of the EU country addressed;
use of a specific method of services.
Requests for service
The requesting agency must communicate the information and documents it has received to the requesting agency within 7 days of receiving the request.
Where the request is not fulfilled within the 7-day deadline, the applicant must inform the judicial officer of the reasons for the refusal. The judicial officer may order, in case of urgency, any provisional or protective measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
The EJNic was established under Article 3(2) of the Lisbon Treaty. It aims to establish an area of cooperation between the EU countries where judicial cooperation is needed for the proper functioning of the internal market.
For more information, see:
European judicial cooperation (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Judicial: the legal process in which a person is brought before a court of law for the purpose of obtaining a judicial or other judicial decision.
* Extrajudicial document: a document which has been served on the person who is the party to the proceedings.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2006/512/EC of 21 December 2006 on the Regulation (Euratom), Euratom, on the establishment of a European judicial network in Civil matters (OJ L 183, 22.12.2006, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Decision 2006 /512/EU have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
last update 04.02.2016



Summary 165:
Regulation (EC) No 726/2004 on advanced therapy medicinal products for human use (see summary)
Regulation [Official Journal C 202/2004/EC]
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) framework for the marketing, labelling and labelling of advanced therapies* for the treatment of diseases and dysfunctions of the human body.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the manufacture, procurement, testing, processing, preservation, storage and distribution of human tissues and cells. It does not apply to products containing or consisting exclusively of non-viable human or animal cells and/or tissues and which do not act principally by pharmacological, immunological or metabolic action.
It applies to:
combined advanced therapy* medicinal products* and investigational medicinal products (i.e. products containing and consisting of cells or tissues that have been combined into a single therapeutic product;
products containing and containing active implantable medical devices;
advanced therapy products that are not intended to be used for the diagnosis or treatment of a disease or disorder.
Authorisations
The European Medicines Agency (EMA) is responsible for assessing the quality, safety and efficacy of these products and for authorising their marketing. It must prepare a draft opinion on each advanced therapy for final approval by the European Committee for Medicinal Products for Human Use (ECMU). The Committee for Advanced Therapies advises the European Commission on the quality and safety of the products. It also draws up detailed guidelines on good clinical practice specific to advanced therapy medicines.
The Commission must ensure that these guidelines are implemented in accordance with the technical requirements laid down in Directive 2001/83/EC on the principles and guidelines of good manufacturing practice in respect of medicinal products.
Post-authorisation requirements
The applicant for a marketing authorisation must:
provide a description of the product’s characteristics and the package leaflet;
describe the measures to be taken to ensure the follow-up of efficacy and adverse reactions;
state the details of the patient and product traceability systems;
set up and maintain a system for the patient, institution or private practice where the product is used;
ensure that the authorisation application is made available to interested parties without delay.
In addition to the requirements for pharmacovigilance, the applicant must detail the measures envisaged to ensure that the product and its starting materials are traceable. The Commission must also ensure that relevant information on envisaged measures is made publicly available.
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2021/852 amends Regulation (EC, Euromodern) No 626/2003 to adapt Annexes I to IV of the regulation in order to adapt them to scientific and technical developments in the field of biomedicine and to reflect the specific nature of those products. The regulation also repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 1/2002/EC.
Amendments to Regulation (CE) No 852/2003
The amended regulation repeals and replaces Regulation No 541/2004.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Combined advanced therapies: products that combine or consist of cells, tissues or organs that are capable of providing a therapeutic effect.
* Investigational medicinal product: a medicinal product that is not intended for use in the clinical development of new medicines and which is intended only as a medical device.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.e.:
Commission Regulation (European Union) No 1907/2004 of 21 December 2004 on the marketing authorisations for advanced therapies (OJ C 202, 21.12.2004, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments and corrections to Council Decision (EC), Euratom, have been incorporated in to the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

KEY POINTS
EU type approval
The regulation sets out the obligations of manufacturers, importers and importers to comply with the EC type approval rules.
Manufacturers’ type approval obligations include:
access to vehicle repair and maintenance information and to the information referred to in the regulation;
proof of compliance with the regulation relating to access to vehicle maintenance information;
the use of defeat devices that reduce the effectiveness of emission control systems.
The European Commission is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of EU countries and the European Agency for Harmonisation of Regulations on the Harmonization of Technical Regulations (ETSR).
The Commission is responsible for administering the regulation and its implementing measures.
EU countries must ensure that the regulation is implemented in line with the rules laid down in Directive 2005/55/EC and that it complies with the ETSR.
Implementing measures
The Regulation sets out a number of implementing measures, including:
rules on the technical aspects of type approval;
rules for type-assessment of vehicles;
requirements for the use of exhaust systems and exhaust systems;
measures for the measurement of the mass and number of particulate emissions in the laboratory.
In addition, the Commission must adopt a new measurement procedure replacing the current one. It must also adopt rules on the measurement and reporting of the particulate mass and the number of exhaust emissions in laboratory tests.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMATE APPLY?
Directive 2006/54/EC has applied since 1 January 2006 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2007.
Regulations (EC, Euratom and Euratom) have applied since 31 December 2004.
BACKGROUND
The ETS has been amended several times, most recently by Directive 2004/3/EC.
For more information, see:
Types of vehicles (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC), Euratom, of 30 November 2004 relating to the type of approval of certain motor vehicles (OJ L 347, 30.11.2004, pp. 1-8)

Summary 167:
Scientific assessment of substances on the European Union’s food safety list
Scientific assessments of substances in the food chain
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 178/2002 establishing the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) list of substances to be placed on the EU market.
It sets out the conditions for the European Commission to authorise the placing on the market of substances.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to substances which are classified as foodstuffs (hereinafter referred to as the substances) which contribute to the free movement of food within the EU and to a high level of protection of human health and to the protection of consumer interests.
The European Commission is assisted by the Standing Committee on the Authority, which is composed of representatives of the Member States and the European Parliament and the Council. It is responsible for:
assessing the risks that substances pose to human health;
providing opinions on the safety of the substances;
ensuring that the substances are listed on the Community list.
Authorisation procedure
The Commission must submit an application for the authorisation of a substance to the EFSA. Applications are sent to the Commission. The Commission must inform the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) of the applicant and, if necessary, the European Medicines Agency (EMSA). The Commission then forwards the application to the EMSA for an opinion on the substance to be published in the Official Journal of the EU. The EMSA.
the EMSA then adopts its opinion.
In the Commission adopts the opinion on behalf of the Commission and informs the EU countries of the reasons for its decision.
Implementing measures
The EMSA adopts implementing measures in the event of an emergency concerning a substance on the EC list. These measures must be adopted in accordance with the procedures laid down in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2004.
BACKGROUND
   
The EFSA has a mission to:
ensure that substances placed on EU market are safe;
assess the risks associated with their presence on the list;
make recommendations to EU countries and the Commission on how to deal with these risks.
For more information, see:
Food safety (European Commission).

Summary 168:
Accreditation, market surveillance, controls of products from non-EU countries and CE marking
Assessment of conformity assessment bodies and the CE marking — rules on accreditation, monitoring and controls
Assessing conformity assessments and the European Commission’s role in the implementation of Community harmonisation legislation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2008 — rules for accreditation and market surveillance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the accreditation* and monitoring* of the conformity assessment* bodies* of bodies whose task is to ensure that products meet the requirements of the European Union (EU) legislation on conformity assessment.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
conformity assessment bodies;
market surveillance*;
the CE marking;
controls of products imported from third countries.
Accreditation
The accreditation procedure is carried out by national accreditation bodies (known as ‘accreditation bodies’). They assess the competence of the bodies whose job it is to assess whether a product meets the requirements set out in the legislation. They then issue an accreditation certificate to a body that meets these requirements.
The European Commission may, after consulting the EU countries, recognise a body which satisfies the requirements in Annex I to this regulation. The Commission may not recognise more than one body at a time. The accreditation body must publish audited annual accounts prepared in accordance with generally accepted accounting principles.
Market surveillance
EU countries must periodically review and assess the functioning of their surveillance activities. They must also periodically update and update their knowledge necessary for the proper performance of their tasks.
If the market surveillance authorities find that a product does not comply safety or cannot be regarded as being in breach of the legislation, it must be released, provided that all the other requirements and formalities pertaining to such release have been fulfilled.
Where a product is found not to comply with the legislation it has been placed on the market, the product must be withdrawn from the market and returned to its place of manufacture. This may, if necessary, include prohibiting the product's being placed on sale.
In addition, the Commission must ensure that the competent authorities of EU countries monitor the activities of the body to which has been accredited and that it is independent of any conflicts of interest with the bodies it assesses and of commercial pressures.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2008 has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Accreditation’ and ‘Market surveillance’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Conformity Assessment Body: a body whose job is to carry out a conformity assessment of a product in order to ensure it complies with the relevant legislation.
* CE marking: a CE marking that indicates that the product meets all the essential requirements for the marketing of products in the EU.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council regulation (EC), Euratom, of 21 February 2008 laying down rules for:
accreditation* (OJ L 127, 28.3.2008, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, e.g. Annex II) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 169:
EU-Norway agreement on extradition of suspects in criminal matters (Criminal Justice Cooperation Agreement between the EU and Norway, Iceland and the Republic of Iceland (CJCA)
EU-Nordic agreement on extraditable persons in relation to criminal matters
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement on extradition between the European Union and the Kingdom of Norway, the Republic and the country of Iceland
Decision 2004/852/EC on the conclusion, on behalf of the EU, of the CJCA on the extradition of persons suspected of criminal offences
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to ensure that the arrest warrant issued by an EU country to a non-EU country for the purpose of extradition of a person suspected of a criminal offence is executed.
The decision approves the conclusion of the agreement by the European Council of 26 June 2004.
KEY POINTS
The arrest warrant is issued by the judicial authority of the issuing country which is competent to issue an arrest warrant by virtue of the law of that country. The issuing country decides whether to execute the warrant. The executing country determines whether to do so on the basis of the person’s consent or on the grounds that the acts for which the warrant has been issued constitute an offence under the national law of the executing country.
In the case of consent, the requested person has the right to legal counsel and, where appropriate, to legal aid. The requested person may also be subject to the following conditions:
the requested person must be informed of the nature of the offence;
the person concerned has expressed his/herself-awareness of the consequences of the act;
consentities and property are liable to seizure or confiscation in the country in which the person is to be returned.
Consent and renunciation
The request for consent must be made in writing and must be recorded in accordance with that country's national law. Consent must be given before the competent judicial authorities of both the issuing and executing countries.
If a person does not comply with the conditions laid down in the agreement, the request for renunciation must be accompanied by a declaration or notification from the requesting country that the person has expressed them voluntarily and in full awareness of their consequences.
Where a person is not subject to a speciality rule, he/she may refuse to comply with these conditions. In this case, the person concerned may be detained for a period of detention in the territory of the country where the warrant was issued.
Time limits for surrender
The requesting country must decide, within the time limits and under the conditions defined in this agreement, whether the person must surrender or be handed over to the country which issued the warrant or to which it has been handed over. It must ensure that all the material conditions necessary for effective surrender are fulfilled.
Surrender or subsequent extradition
The executing country may decide to postpone the surrender of a requested person so that he or she may be prosecuted in the executing state or, if he orshe has already been sentenced, so that they may serve, in its territory, a sentence passed for an act other than that referred to in the warrant, other than the act for which they have been arrested.
Procedures
The requested person is required to give consent before the issuing judicial authorities before the execution of the warrant and before the subsequent extradition. Consent may not be revoked. In the event of multiple requests, the executing judicial authority must decide whether to act on the request or not to act upon the request.
When a person refuses to cooperate with the executing authorities, they must inform them of the reasons for their refusal. The reasons must be in writing.
Legal aid
In addition to the right of legal aid, the requesting person is entitled to:
consultation with a lawyer,
the right of access to legal assistance,
legal aid in the event that the request is refused,
privileges and immunity of the judicial authorities.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Council Decision 2004/884/EC entered into force on 27 June 2004 and the decision of the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the signature and provisional application of the Agreement on extradition within the meaning of Article 30(3) of the Treaty on European Union.
BACKGROUND
Criminal justice cooperation in the EU
The European Union (EU) and its Member States have signed a number of agreements on extradition, including the Cajun Cotonou Agreement, which aims to improve cooperation between judicial authorities in the field of extradition.
For more information, see:
‘Cajun Arrest Warrant’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Council Directive 2004/ 852/EEC of 27 May 2004 on the signing, on the behalf of Member States of the Union, of an extradition agreement between the Community and the countries of the Faroe Islands, Norway and Iceland (OJ



Summary 170:
Compensation and assistance for passengers in the event of major disruption to a rail passenger service
Compensating and assisting passengers in order to ensure their rights are respected
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on compensation and assistance in the case of major disruptions to a railway passenger service (recast)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes uniform rules for the compensation* and assistance* of passengers and persons with reduced mobility on board a train and during boarding and disembarking from a train.
It aims to ensure that passengers are compensated and assisted in case of serious disruption to rail passenger services.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all passengers, irrespective of their nationality or place of residence.
Passengers are entitled to:
compensation for loss or damage caused by the carrier;
assistance on board the train;
payment and refund of the carriage charge.
Compensation for delay
A passenger may be entitled to compensation for delay between the time of taking over by a carrier and the time it is delivered, as well as from delay in delivery.
The carrier is liable for any loss caused by:
the carriage charge;
the delay caused by an accident;
damage caused by a vehicle;
death of a passenger.
If a passenger dies, the carrier is jointly and severally liable for the death of the passenger, and for the funeral provided by a substitute carrier, but by a different carrier.
Disabled persons and persons who have reduced mobility assistance must be provided free of charge on board trains.
Complaints must be submitted in writing to any railway undertaking involved.
A complaint must be made in writing by the person who has been the victim of an accident or damage. The complaint must state the nature of the damage or injury caused, the person’s name and address, and the date and place of the occurrence of the accident.
Claims must be brought in writing. The person entitled to claim must be able to draw up a statement of the nature and extent of the injury or damage, the loss caused and the person to whom it may be brought.
In addition, the claimant must submit a complaint to the competent authority of the EU country in which the accident took place. This complaint must contain the following information:
name and address of the person or persons who are the victim;
name of the railway undertaking responsible for the management of a railway station and which may be the infrastructure manager;
date and time of occurrence;
location of the incident;
type of damage caused;
number of passengers who have been injured or who have suffered damage;
duration of the delay;
time of delivery;
amount of compensation.
Where a complaint is brought by a passenger, it must be filed in writing and must state in writing the nature, extent and duration of the loss and damage caused. The claim must also state the name of the persons to whom the claim is addressed and the place and date of occurrence.
There is no waiver or stipulation of limits on the amount of compensation or assistance a passenger is entitled to.
Liability for luggage
The liability for luggage must be based on the condition of the luggage and the extent of any damage caused to it. The carriage charge must not exceed 8 000 units of account.
Carriers must be liable for liability for damage to their vehicles. The liability must also include liability for loss caused to their passengers’ luggage.
Exclusion of waiver and stipulation
The carriage charge shall not be waived or stipulated as to the extent to which a passenger may claim compensation for damage.
Uniform rules on the rights of passengers
The rules apply to passengers who:
have a disability;
have reduced mobility;
are physically or mentally handicapped;
live in a country other than that of the European Union (EU);
are not entitled to travel within the EU.
EU countries must ensure that the rights and obligations of passengers are respected by railway undertakings and that passengers have access to information and assistance on board their trains and at other railway stations.
Information and service quality requirements
Railway undertakings must:
provide passengers with travel information in the most appropriate format;
ensure that the information they provide is in line with the requirements laid down in Directive 2001/16/EC on the interoperability of the conventional rail system.
Railways must also:
monitor their own performance as reflected in the service quality standards set out in the technical specifications for interoperability (TSIs) referred to in Article 153(2) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union;
take measures to improve the quality of their services, in particular in relation to the provision of information and service to passengers.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
Directive 2004/49/EC has applied since 19 March 2004 and had to become law in the EU countries by 19 March 2006.
BACKGROUND

The Commission’s tasks include:

Summary 172:
Energy statistics
Energy Statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on the annual collection of energy statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the scope, units, reported period, frequency, deadline, deadline and transmission modalities for the collection of annual energy statistics.
KEY POINTS
Energy statistics have traditionally been focused on energy supply and on fossil energies. However, the European Union (EU) has adopted a number of measures to improve the quality of these statistics. These measures include:
the introduction of the European Energy Information System (EISA);
the harmonisation of the reporting requirements for energy statistics;
the creation of a European Energy Data System (ERISA) database.
EU countries’ obligations
The regulation applies to the following sectors:
energy production and consumption;
energy storage and distribution;
electricity generation;
transport;
natural gas production and distribution.
The European Commission may grant exemptions or derogations to EU countries from those aspects of the energy data collection that would lead to an excessive burden on respondents.
Scope and reporting requirements
The energy statistics covered by the regulation are based on the following categories:
total energy production;
total consumption; and
total demand for energy.
These categories are broken down into the following types of energy production:
carbon dioxide emissions;
carbon monoxide emissions.
Electricity generation and distribution: electricity plants;
combined electricity generation and consumption.
transmission: electricity generated by electricity plants.
Natural gas production, distribution and consumption: electricity produced by power plants and electricity generated from natural gas;
conversion of natural gas into electricity.
Transmission of energy data: electricity generation, distribution, consumption and distribution from power plants, electricity generated and distributed from electricity generators, electricity produced from renewable sources.
Quality assessment and reports: the European Commission assesses the quality and reliability of the data collected and publishes a quality assessment report every 2 years.
Reporting requirements: the Commission must report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the quality, reliability and timeliness of the information it receives from the energy sector. The Commission must publish a quality assurance report every 3 years on the basis of the quality assessment and quality reports it receives. It must also publish an annual report on the energy statistics it has received.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2005. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted measures to reduce greenhouse gas emissions (GHG emissions) in line with the EU’s commitment to the Kyoto Protocol. The EISA is one of these measures.
For more information, see:
The EISA (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Carbon dioxide emissions: GHG emissions from the burning of fossil fuels.
* CO2 emissions: greenhouse gases emitted from burning fossil fuels, such as oil, natural gas, coal, hydrofluorocarbons, hydrocarbons, kerosene, heavy fuel oil, bitumen (including orimulsion), petroleum coke and other oil products.

Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2004 of 21 December 2004 on the collection and transmission of annual statistics on energy and repealing Decision No 280/2004/EC (OJ L 157, 28.12.2004, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 550/04/2004 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 173:
Passenger rights — transport for persons with reduced mobility (Regulation (EU) 2016/856 on passenger rights
Passenger Rights — Transport for Persons with Reduced Mobility
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EC) No 856/2016 — passenger rights relating to the transport of persons and their mobility
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rights and obligations of passengers travelling by public transport in the European Union (EU).
It applies to passengers travelling with occasional services where the initial boarding point or the final alighting point of the passenger is situated in the territory of an EU Member State.
KEY POINTS
Passengers and terminal managing bodies must:
ensure that:
travel agents and tour operators provide information on the rights of passengers;
make reservations and tickets at no additional cost;
offer passengers the choice of an alternative transport service;
provide them with first aid, accommodation, food, clothes and transport.
The regulation does not apply to:
carriers, travel agents or tour operators who refuse to accept a reservation or to issue or otherwise provide a ticket on the grounds referred to in the regulation’s Articles 9 to 16;
the carriage of passengers by motor vehicles;
terminal managing bodies which are responsible for the design of new terminals and for major refurbishments;
any other type of public transport services.
Accessibility and information
The carrier or terminal managing body must ensure that disabled persons and persons who have reduced mobility receive the information required under paragraphs 1 and 2 of the regulation in accessible formats.
In addition to the information provided by the carrier, travel agent or tour operator must also provide:
information on the right to transport;
accessibility to the ticket and reservation system;
information to passengers on their rights in the event of cancellation, delay or cancellation of a transport service.
Non-discriminatory contract conditions
Carriers must not discriminate between passengers on the basis of their disability, age, gender, sex, race, ethnic origin, religion, sexual orientation, age of the person concerned, disability or disability, disability status, age or disability status.
They must also ensure that passengers are fully informed of their rights under this regulation, so that they can effectively exercise those rights.
If a passenger is refused a ticket or a reservation, the carrier must inform the passenger concerned about any alternative service operated by the airline or travel agent.
Continuation, re-routing and reimbursement of the ticket price from the carrier
In the case of cancellation or delay, passengers have the right:
the right to the continuation of their journey;
reimbursement of their ticket price;
refundable of any ticket or ticket-related charges.
Disabled passengers and terminal managers must also:
make sure that the information they receive is in an accessible format and that it can be easily understood and understood by passengers; and
make passengers aware of the rights they have under the regulation.
Complaints
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 3 years on the implementation of the Regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 March 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU has adopted a number of consumer protection measures to protect passengers’ rights. These include:
rules on the liability of motor vehicles and the enforcement of the obligation to insure against such liability (Civil Liability Directive);
measures to protect the interests of passengers and their families against the risks associated with motor vehicles.
For more information, see:
Consumer protection (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Passenger rights: the right of passengers to travel by air, rail, bus, coach, sea or other means of transport and to have their ticket, reservation or ticket issued or otherwise provided by a carrier.
* Terminal managing body: the body responsible for managing a public transport service in the EU.

Summary 174:
Statistics on aquaculture
Statistical data on aqua-fishery
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 788/96 on the statistical collection of statistics on the aquacultural sector
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and analysis of statistics relating to the production (volume and unit value) of fish, crustaceans and molluscs (aquaculture) in European Union (EU) waters.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC), which had to become law in the EU countries by 2008.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the annual production of fish and crustacean species* in EU waters;
the volume and unit values of molluses* and other aquatic animals (e.g. aquatic plants and fish);
the production of ornamental species* and ornamental aquatic animals;
aquaculture-related data on the production and use of aquacultures.
EU countries must submit the data to the European Commission (Eurostat) within 12 months of the end of the reference calendar year.
The data cover:
fishing areas for statistical purposes;
total production;
volume of fish;
unit values of crustacea;
number of species;
annual production of aquatic animals.
Annex V sets out methods for collecting the data.
National authorities must ensure that the data are collected in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation.
Eurostat is assisted by the Standing Committee for Agricultural Statistics, which is composed of representatives of the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions. The Commission is assisted in its work by the standing committee for agricultural statistics and the Standing committee for fisheries statistics.
In addition, Eurostat must:
ensure that the Commission has access to the data;
assess the quality and relevance of the data collected;
make recommendations to the Commission on the quality, relevance, relevance and usefulness of the statistics collected.
Compilation of statistics
The European Commission must submit a report to Eurostat every 3 years on the data it has collected. The report must contain:
a description of the production, volume, unit value and year of the year;
an estimate of the number of species of fish caught, caught and released;
methods for collecting and processing the data, including methods of sampling, sampling and processing;
data on the structure of the aquaulture sector, including the number and stage of aquatic life cycle;
measures to be taken by the Commission to ensure the confidentiality and integrity of the information collected and to ensure that it is used for the purposes of the EU’s common fisheries policy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2008. It repeals, except for Article 8(1) which applies from 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The increased contribution by aquaciculture to the EU's total fisheries production requires a wider range of data for a rational development and management of this sector within the Common Fisheries Policy.
For more information, see:
Fisheries statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Aquaculture: places for the artificial breeding, hatching, rearing and rearing through the early life stages of aquatic animal.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EEC, Euratom) No 768/2008 of 23 June 2008 on the Statistical Collection of Statistics on the aquatic sector (recast) (Codified version) (OJ L 127, 29.7.2008, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euram) No 770/2008 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
